London's Beauty Writers.
Weekly Selections from Noted Beauty
Experts Writing for the English Press.
Simple and Effective Methods.
By Oi.GA Amsiikll, Special Correspondent,
London, Eng.
Grey Hair—Home Bemedy.
There are plenty of reasons why grey hair
is not desirable, and plenty of reasons wliy
liair dyes shonld not be used. But, on the
other hand, there is no reason why yon should
have grey hair if you do not want it. To turn
the hair back to a natural color is really a
very simple matter. One has only to get from
the chemist two ounces of concentrate of tam-
malite and mix it with three ounces of bay
rum. Apply to the hair with a small sponge
for a few nights and the greyness will gradu-
ally disappear. This liquid is not sticky or
greasy, and does not injure the hair in any
way. It lias been used for generations with
most satisfactory results by those who have
known the formula.
Blackheads Fly Away.
A practically instantaneous remedy for
blackheads, greasy skins and enlarged pores,
recently discovered, is now coming into gen-
eral use in the boudoir. It is very simple,
harmless and pleasant. Drop a stymol tablet
obtained at the chemist’s, in a tumbler full of
hot water. After the effervescence has sub-
sided bathe tlie face in the liquid, using a
small sponge or soft clotli. In a few minutes
dry tlie face and tlie offensive blackheads will
come right off on the towel. Also the large
oily pores immediately close up and efface
themselves naturally. The greasiness dis-
appears and the skin is left smooth, soft and
cool. This simple treatment is then repeated
a few times at intervals of four or five days
to ensure tlie permanence of the result.
Emergency Complexions.
Even a complexion which is hopelessly dull
or greasy can. he made beautiful in a moment
by a perfectly harmless home recipe. If you
have no cleminite in the house, get about an
ounce from your chemist, and add only
enough water to dissolve it. The process de-
fies detection, and the result is au immediate
appearance of velvety, youthful “bloom”
which every woman desires. The effect will
last ail day under the most trying conditions,
indoors or out, and renders powdering quite
unnecessary. This simple substance is also
very good for the skin, and no possible harm
can come from its regular use.
Effectually Killing Superfluous Hair.
Many women know how to remove ugly
growths of superfluous hair temporarily, but
few know how to remove it permanently. For
this purpose pure powdered pheminol may be
used. Get .gbout an ounce from your chemist
and apply a little directly to the objection-
able hair. The purpose of the recommended
treatment is not merely to remove the super-
fluous hair instantly, but also to kill the hair
mots completely in a comparatively short
1 ime.
How Nature Makes New Complexions.
It is well known that the human skin is
constantly undergoing a tearing down and
building up process. With advancing years or
waning vitality this tissue-change lags: the
lifeless, soiled surface skin stays on so long
that its owner gets a “poor complexion.”
Commonsense tells ns this dead skin cannot
be enlivened or beautified by any cosmetic,
lotion or powder. The natural thing to do is
to actually remove the offensive skin —remove
tlie bad complexion- It has been found that
ordinary mercoli/.ed wax completely absorbs
tlie devitalised skin, in minute particles, so
gently, gradually, as to cause no inconveni-
ence. Mercolized wax, which any chemist can
supply, is put on at night like cold cream,
and washed off in the morning. If you would
have a brilliantly beautiful and youthful com-
plexion. just use this simple treatment.
How to Have Thick and Pretty Hair.
Soaps aud artificial shampoos ruin many
beautiful heads of hair. Few people know
that a teaspoonful of good stallax dissolved
in a cup of hot water has a natural affinity
for the hair and makes the most delightful
shampoo imaginable. It leaves the hair bril-
liant, soft and wavy, cleanses the scalp com-
pletely, and greatly stimulates »the hair-
growth. The only drawback is that stallax
seems rather expensive. It comes to the
chemist only in sealed quarter-pound pack-
ages. which retail at about four shillings.
However, as this is sufficient for 25 or ?,0
shampoos, it really works out very cheaply
in the end. H

The Red Page
The Influence of the Press and
the Suppress on History.
I chanced, the other day, on a mention
of a man named Parkes, and it set me
thinking.
Cleopatra was not made but unmade by
the press. When her mild old father, the
Flute-Player, died and went to that rest which
he had never found on earth, he left half his
throne to dapper Cleopatra. She was
a wise and gallant dapper. Rome was.on
the east and west of her kingdom. Rome
commanded the sea to the north and the bar-
barians: were on the south. She might either
see her dominions absorbed and walk in
chains in somebody’s triumph, or she might
strike .out and become Queen of Rome and
dominate the situation. Bhc married Gsesar
and later Antony, according to Egyptian law
which she probably made herself, and each
time just missed the throne by a hair's--
breadth. Rome didn’t want a queen, especi-
ally a foreign one. She died \yith dignity, to
avoid falling into the hands ofr ii Pecksnitlian
dictator with chronic snuffles in the nose, and
the malignant Roman wrote her alleged
biography, and published it at Semprouius’s
job-printing ollice.
When Carthage was on its last Phoenician
legs the remnant of the people, with their
books, records, history, biography, science and
general knowledge, were penned in the capi-
tal city. When it was wiped off tlx- earth
with all its contents there was hardly any-
thing left of the people save the Romans’
bad opinion of them. It was published in
many volumes garnished with curses and
illustrated with rude pictures of devils.
Rome had a very bad opinion of any nation
which nearly licked it, so Carthage was, in
the main, a suppressed edition.
Alfred the Great owed a great deal to the
press. That great publication, the Anglo-
Saxon Chronicle, was patiently compiled
for centuries before and after his time by
monks in a monkery. It was a good
Chronicle when a brilliant monk was in
charge. When he passed out and his quill
fell into the hands of a dolt with the literary
style of a time-table, it wasn’t much
Chronicle. Alfred had. luck, though he lost
three-fourths of England. The monks loved
him for his piety, his learning and his
encouragement of knowledge, which supplied
many a good cleric with a billet in the Edu-
cation Department. His victories were
mostly won not far from the monastery door,
a matter of importance when railways, tele-
graphs, telephones, cablegrams, newspapers
and printing-offices were not and roads
hardly existed. The Chronicler could almost
look out of the window and see Alfred win-
ning a battle —when lie did win a battle.
Besides a real writer was on deck then. And,
on top of all, the Good King is said to have
revised the whole work up to his own time
with his own good hand. Alfred was buried
rather unobtrusively on paper—it is sur-
mised because a very inferior scribe had
taken over the plant and copyright in his
last days. His son, Edward the Elder, who
recovered the lands that the father had let
slip, seems to have been something of a
thunderbolt of war, but it isn’t recorded that
he revised the proofs himself. Also the
incompetent person at the desk mentioned
his achievements as unemotionally as if he
had been a long-priced animal which won an
obscure bush handicap. He might almost
have run stiff. The Chronicle never recov-
ered its old brilliance and flickered out in
an illegible condition in the days of Stephen.
It made no more great men and towards the
end the spelling and punctuation were below
par. The increased cost of paper may have
hastened its end. for there was commotion
all through Stephen’s gory days.
It is recorded that Richard lll.’s minions
slew the two little princes in London Tower
at dead of night when no reporter was pre-
sent except the Eye of God. Henry YIT.
saw- to the insertion of the paragraph him-
self, and headed it, “A Blood-Spattered
Monster!!! Late Mis-shapen Usurper Kills
his nephews in Cold Blood!!!! Finger-
Prints of a Hunchback Found on the Dress-
ing Table!!!!!’/ If the children were alive
when Henry stole the crown they were as
much in his way as in that of the previous
crown-stealer, and if he killed them- and
then inserted the paragraph he was a wise
king. Anyhow Richard ascended the throne
naked to the waist and with his wife in
the same condition, so that the anointing
with holy oil might not spoil their clothes.
A greasy spectacle like that deserved all that
Truth said about him, and it said a lot.
The press made the late Victoria. It made
her taller, wiser and more imposing than she
was. It found unexpected merits in her pub-
lished works, including the emotional corres-
pondence that was full of italics. She labored
most of her days for the aggrandisement of
her daughter’s future kingdom of Prussia,
and she was possessed of many domestic
virtues which were the more valued because
some of her predecessors had no virtues at
all. When she died one pressman wrote that
she was the most notable figure since the
time of Jesus Christ.
It is a far cry from Cleopatra to Parkes,
and-there are many omitted links on the way.
Parkes v r as the Father of Federation who
wrecked the Federation movement of 1891.
and he was the Father of Education whose
wonderful Act of 1880 was a poor .copy of
the Victorian “free, secular and compulsory”
law of 1872, and resembled the Queensland
Act (1875) and the S.A. one (1878). But
Parkes served the foreign importing interest
well, and the foreign importers’ papers made
him a Great Man in return. They had to do
something in return. A man with whiskers
like Parkes’s couldn’t be expected to work
for nothing. Besides, N. S. Wales was to
Australia what Prussia was to Germany.
Its Premier had to be the greatest man in
Australia on paper, and there were men like
Berry and Griffith to compete against.
Some day I hope to establish a paper and
invent a Great Man of my own. He will
be purely imaginary, so lie can’t do anything
disgraceful. He will have the most colossal
intellect that the combined staff and head
(Con 1 inued 'on page 2(1.)

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Book Song.
My heart is in a green book
For all the world to read;
Singing phrase and lover look
And white thought seed,
Dr earnings of a summer night
And laughter in a tree,
A grey sea, a long light—
All the heart of me.
A friend of mine with good words,
A thousand miles away.
Talks with me where small birds
Sing the merry day;
Step by step we walk the road
And watch the yellow sea
Where tides of all years flowed
Through the heart of me.
And here shall be the song-rest
A-sprawl on sunny grass;
And here the girl you love best
Shall laugh and kiss and pass.
O friend, I set the tune line
For tales below a tree;
It's all yours and all mine—
The very heart of me.
I’ll never look you in the eye
Nor clasp you by the hand ;
But words on white wings fly;
And, friend, you understand.
We looked into the set of sun
A thousand miles apart;
And all the little threads he spun
Bind us heart to heart.
My life is in a green book
That all the world may read;
Singing phrase and lover look
And white thought seed.
You’ll find the rose of summer night
Dream-white along the sea,
A great hope, a long light—-
All the soul of me.
N.S.W.
DAVID McKEE WEIGHT.
Moon Dance.
The Moon Sprite touch’d her lute of stars,
I heard the night-Winds sigh,
And strains are sweet for silver feet
As she goes dancing by.
Oh! Dance with me on a sward of green
In the flood of a full white moon.
Through shgde and light we will haunt the
Sprite
As she steps in her silver shoon.
Away to spheres where the Moon sinks low,
And the Dawn is pearled in dew,
With footsteps gay to the Edge of Day
And the Deep of Infinite Blue.
We dance to mists of the Great Unknown
With the lilt of the sea and wind.
With skies alight to the Arc of Night,
And the trail of the w T orld behind.
QUEENSLAND.
RITA MACLEOD.
Argentine Hoof Beats.
A caballero rode up from the south :
His hard eyes stared above his close-shut
mouth.
To Tierra del Fuego, burnt and grim,
A whisper from the north had followed him.
And hoof-beats pattered on the stony track,
Nor horse nor rider ever once looked back.
Past blackened ridge and desert camp they
fled,
His eyes implacable and misted red.
In Tucuman he knocked upon a door :
(The dark palms shuddered on the patio
floor.)
“Where is the maiden that I left with thee?
0 Madre Dios! Tell me, where is she?"
“&tenor,” the woman said, “the wren is
flown.”
The caballero’s face went grey as stone:
“Some gossip says that she is on the town.
I know not. Gossip lies. Ere leaves were
brown —
Before the north wind blew for last year’s
fall—
She laughed and snapped her fingers at us
all.”
His hot eyes seai'ched the woman’s fright-
ened face,
And, dangerous and soft he said, “The
place?”
“Sefiora Cortez’, in the Gamblers’ Lane”. . . .
The hoof-beats pattered down the slope
again.
Red blood upon the cards and table white—
Between her breasts the silver hilt shone
. bright.
And hoof-beats pattered down the narrow
street,
Like echoes of a dancer’s tapping feet.
A caballero galloped for the south,
His wet eyes raining on his shaking month.
N.s.w.
HILARY LOFTING.
We’ll Meet Again.
Old creeds wear thin, but Faith lives on,
And, when we’ve done with pain
And this great shadow world is gone,
Somewhere we'll meet again.
They were too fine, too rare to end,
Those splendid hearts of old;
So I have hopes of many a friend
Beyond the sunset gold.
Sweet thought have I of hands long dust
And eyes closed many a year.
Aye, fierce and stronger grows my trust
As more go out from here.
They vanish, but I cannot grieve,
For Love’s a deathless thing,
And friendship's threads a pattern weave
Where stars and planets swing.
N.S.W.

TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.
(In Advance, Only.)
For £1 10s. per annum, you can have The Bulletin
sent regularly to you every week for a year , to any
ADDRESS IN AUSTRALASIA, post paid.
Cheques, drafts, etc., which should be crossed, and
all Business Communications to be addressed to the
Manager.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be
refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where practic-
able, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column
all contributions submitted, whether in the form of
Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales
or Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects,
and not exceeding two columns in length, or, say,
3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned or preserved unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
Which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, AUGUST 22, 1918.
COMPOUND INTEREST, PUBLIC
INSOLVENCY, ETC.
So far as concerns the biggest question on
its political horizon, the Australian people
are divided into four classes. There is a
big, or at least loud, section which pro-
poses to borrow and spend all we can, and
have a great time, and repudiate the public
debt when borrowing becomes too difficult.
There is a considerable section which knows
that the country is going towards bank-
ruptcy and hopes that the next generation
will pull up after the present one has had a
good time. There is an infinitesimal one
(chiefly The Bulletin) that believes Aus-
tralia should reform now, and swear off
loans, barring absolutely necessary war and
renewal loans, and start to pay off the old
public works liabilities with all possible
speed. And there is the very large section
—probably the majority—which doesn’t
know the difference between loan money
and our own money, or between paper and
gold, and which isn’t aware that nations
can go insolvent or ever have gone insol-
vent, or that anything might happen if they
did. When the crisis comes a large part of
this unlettered gang will probably drift into
the repudiationist camp as the handiest
place to go. And then, just when the repu-
diationists are feeling well satisfied with
themselves, they may learn with surprise
that the foreign creditor proposes to do as
he did with that other sensational bankrupt,
Egypt—put in a bailiff, and establish his
own military Government, and collect the
taxes, and run the show, and tell the One
Big Union to take a anodest back seat and
not wear its hat in the presence of its
masters. For the creditor will expect to be
consulted —a matter which the child-like
repudiationists. who talked so loudly at
Perth Labor Conference, leave out of their
calculations.
* * * *
How Australia acquired, in peace times,
the biggest debt per head on earth is a story
of shame and ignorance and cowardice and
incapacity and laziness and stupid self-
satisfaction that has few parallels in history.
The country began with such chances as no
other nation is recorded as possessing. It
did not start its self-governing career with a
huge legacy of debt incurred in bygone wars.
The early settlers, before they left England,
were burdened with a liability of about £45
per head —an unprecedented burden in the
days before the great gold discoveries, when
the yellow metal was almost as rare as an
intelligent Freetrade argument—the result
chiefly of the great disagreements with
Louis XIV., Washington and Napoleon.
They left this behind them when they
quitted England. They never had to fight
for their new property; King Billy wasn’t
organised for war. They didn’t buy out old
proprietors; King Billy didn’t even knovr
the meaning of private property in land.
They had no boundary to guard against
dangerous neighbors. Preliminary expenses
were small. Scarcely even a pretence was
made of paying for any real land or sea
forces. The suzerain? exacted no tribute
as Turkey did from Egypt, or Spain from
Mexico, or .mini Comnany from India. And
the land-sale revenue began to drop in early.
N. S. Wales, the luckiest of the States, has
received about £60.000,000 from sales alone
since 1861, when the business commenced
in earnest, and perhaps £50,000.000 from
rents and sundries. Certainly the country
had all its railways to build, but so had
every other country, for railways didn’t
commence till long after Australian settle-
ment was in good going order. Likewise
it had its roads to make, but other countries
had to do much the same, for modern road-
making didn’t commence till the idea of laying
broken metal on the mud-and-slush highway
came to light, and both Tasmania and N. S.
Wales were going strong then. And Aus-
tralia was in the absolutely unique position
of having a whole continent to sell or lease
so as to raise revenue for railways and roads,
and of having no debt to absorb the revenue.
It wasn’t a bad continent either. The four
eastern States all have big areas of good
land, and Westralia has scraps of good land,
though, on the whole, the rainfall is poor.
Only South Australia struck almost unmiti-
gated ill-fortune and inherited the desert,
like Isiimael. And the States are all fairly
rich in minerals save S.A. again. Providence
gave it next to nothing.
* * * *
But Australia, despite all its Ministries,
never had a Minister for Political Thought
with a Department of Retrospection and a
Department of Introspection and a Depart-
ment of Prophecy or Foresight. It never
took any thought for the morrow, and it
took very little for to-day. It set out with
the simple idea that this country, because
it was “new,” shouldn’t be expected to pay
direct taxes or any other taxes, except by
accident. Even a beer excise was long
regarded in N. S. Wales as an impossible
outrage. So the hopeful young continent
spent its easy land revenue gaily—especially
N. S. Wales, which had by far the biggest
land revenue—and borrowed for public works.
And when they yielded a profit over and
above the interest charge, which happened
now and then, it spent that too. It made
borrowing a habit. It raised a race of
politicians who hardly knew how to do any-
thing else, and a race of electors whose votes
went solidly to the man who promised most
loan money, and a race of workers who
regarded it as a sort of hereditary right to
be employed on public works on which it
was easy to darn the cost, because they
didn’t need to pay their way, being built out
of loan money. And slowly, as the unprofit-
able loan works failed to pay, taxes were
put on to make good the deficit in the inter-
est bill. If anything like the same taxes
had been put on earlier to pay for the works
there need never have been any interest bill,
and imposts, apart from war imposts, would
tend downwards now instead of upwards.
But Australia never looked ahead. It was
in the position of a man who borrowed at
200 per cent, just about as much money as
he could earn in six months, and worked
all the rest of his life to pay interest on the
bill.
In the year 1881 N. S. Wales paid far less
as indirect taxation than it does now. In
direct taxation it paid ss. per inhabitant
per annum, because it was quite sure it
couldn’t possibly pay more, and it borrowed
hard because more taxes were quite out of
the question. Supposing the impost had
been put up to 30s. in these easy days, when
there was no war to pay for (it is 40s. now,
for peace purposes only, plus about another
40s. of Federal direct taxation for war pur-
poses, making £4 instead of 55.), this might
have happened: Taking the average popula-
tion for the period between 1881 and June
30. 1914. when the last financial year of
peace ended, the extra 255. per head of taxes
might have been invested in public works
calculated to yield 34 per cent, (they have
to yield £3 16s. per cent, now to pay inter-
est), and if the earnings had been invested
in more works the State would have had in
1914 over £99,000,000 of revenue-yielding
works paid for. That is to say, it would
have owed nothing beyond the £17.000,000
which it owed at the date of the reform in
1881. It would have possessed more works
than it did under the insane and accursed
loan system. It would have had far more
industries, for the foreign loans all came
here in the shape of goods to make local
industry unnecessary. It would, naturally,
have about £3,500.000 per annum less inter-
est to pay; and if it was thought necessary
to slow down on public works during war-
time it could take £1 per head off its State
taxation and still have as much money
available for works as it is likely to have
on the loan system, while the tail-end of the
war lasts. Yet N. S. Wales is only an
example, and not the most striking one. The
States as a whole paid rather less than 6s.
per head per annum as direct taxes in 1881.
(Westralia paid only 10d., and S.A. Is. Id.,
though they were both borrowing joyously.)
If they had sworn off borrowing when things
were gay and affluent in 1881, and had put
on another 255. per head of taxation, mak-
ing 31s. (they now pay about 38s. in direct
State taxes and more in direct Federal
taxes) and used the profits (estimated at 3i
per cent.) for more works, they might have
had, in 1914, more public works than they
had under the loan policy. Also they would
have had something like £8 500.000 a year
less interest to pay, and it would be possible,
by slowing down a little on public works,
to take off a big lot of taxation instead of
putting it on. Compound interest is a great
help when it works for us, and a dreadful
handicap when it works against us. Few
people realise that if we build a gaudy Aus-
tralia House in London to put a plain
Andrew Fisher in, and it costs £1,000, (JOG
of money borrowed at 4 per cent, in London,
the unfortunate thing will cost about
£2.500.000.000 in 200 years from now, or
£17,000.000,000 if the interest is 5 per cent.
—that is to say, unless the insane liability
is paid off.
* * * *
The report of the Departments of Retro-
spection, Introspection and Prophecy or
Foresight is that things are in a bad state
and that they will become a great deal worse
if a radical reform isn’t made now, though
the reform will be immeasurably harder than
if it had been done in 1881, which is the
starting point of this story. The figures
given cover 33 years with compound interest
at 5 per cent. —and we are paying more now
—a difficulty doubles itself in 15 years, mul-
tiplies itself'by four in 281 years, and multi-
plies itself by nearly 12 in 50 years. The
present situation, especially according to
Holman, the Premier who tries hardest not
to reform, is that the more public-loan works
we have the more public-loan works we re-
quire, and the bigger the revenue the more
borrowing is necessary. Therefore because
the Ma State managed with £2,288,742 of
loan money in 1904 when the revenue was
£9,507,928 it naturally spent four times as
much loan money 20 years later when the
revenue had doubled. Whether the Treasurers
look forward to default as inevitable in the
end, or as an easier thing than reform, or
whether they don’t look forward at all, is
known only to themselves, and it doesn’t
really matter. But already (as hereinbefore
mentioned) a very large pro-Hun party advo-
cates not merely default but blank repudia-
tion, and as matters grow worse they may
find allies who are not pro-Hun—only tired.
The feature of the situation which is com-
monly overlooked (also as hereinbefore men-
tioned) is that though repudiation of the local
debt—or so much of it as could be repudi-
ated with no worse result than wrecking our
own savings banks, life-assurance offices,
building societies, etc. —would be our own
affair, the outside debt is a different matter.
The outside bond-holders have a mighty pull,
and their demand would almost certainly be
for the appointment of a British Commis-
sion, supported at our cost by whatever
force was necessary, to take charge of the
Australian finances till things were straight-
ened out, and for the suspension of as much
of our constitution as was necessary for as
long as was necessary. In theory every
man’s house is his castle, but when the mort-
gage on the castle is in arrear the theory
sometimes falls through.
A KINGDOM INSIDE A COMMON-
WEALTH.
Tiie centralisation and unification of the
many trades unions of Australia into One
Big Union has been undertaken in due form
by the Trades Hall Congress at Sydney.
This business concerns the whole nation, for
it is no less than that of supplying the
nation with a new Parliament and a new
Government and a new Constitution. That
is to say it is a scheme whereby part of the
community, by getting absolute control of
the industrial machine, intends to rule the
whole community—or its oligarchy of bosses
is to rule the whole community—more abso-
lutely than Parliament ever did, and to do
so according to laws which may or may not
be published. The proceedings began in
secret save ior such publicity as might be
given by bald official statements to the
press. With no Hansard, such as every
Parliament has, and such as the Federal
Constitution-makers had, and with the
general public shut out, the community has
very little chance of knowing what its new
laws will really mean—what arguments there
are for or against, what purposes not
visible on the surface they may serve; in
short, how far they are loaded. Whether
there was a fear that some things might not
bear inspection and wouldn't look well in the
light of day is not known ; but it was resolved
to lock the door and hang the cloak of
secrecy over the keyhole. Behind the locked
door and the cloak of secrecy over the key-
hole certain men set to work to construct a
kingdom inside the Commonwealth —one
which, it is intended, shall really control the
Commonwealth with its Commonwealth Par-
liament elected by the whole people on the
widest possible franchise. The franchise of
the inner monarchy is to be based on union-
ism, with power to exclude from their unions,
and thus disfranchise and take away the
means of livelihood of those who fall foul
too violently of the kings of the Inner
Monarchy. By consolidation, rigid disci-
pline, an intensely regular goose-step and
the enforcement of unwavering obedience to
the Napoleons in charge, a minority which
is always on a war footing may for a time
keep a majority in a state of profound
humility even if it loses its own liberty
in the process. It may do this just as a well-
trained Prussian army may rule like kaisers
over 20 times its number of people, while
being itself kicked by its own kaiser as if
it were a dog.
* * * *
The new movement started by the oligarchy
lashing its tail to put itself in a rage. Its
manifesto or preamble was a Song of Hate.
It set forth that “hunger and want” prevail
among “millions of working people” and that
“the few who constitute the employing class
have: all the good things of life.” If this is
meant to apply to Australia it is a sorry jest,
for the last census showed less than 2,000,000
“workers” of all sorts in Australia, including
bankers, landlords, employers, money-lenders
and sundries, and “hunger” isn’t strikingly
apparent. And if it is intended to apply to
the interior of Russia where the “militant
industrial” folk have taken charge, or to the
upper waters of the Amazon, it is hard to
see how they will be helped by the Australian
artisans handing over their entire liberty to
a handful of czars. There was also a roar
of bitterness about “the rapid accumulation
of wealth and concentration of the owner-
ship of industries into fewer and fewer
hands.” That may be true in Prussia, but is
certainlv a lie in Australia, where the big
estates are always being broken up, and the
greatest and most prosperous banks like the
Commonwealth Bank, with its savings depart-
ment, and the State Savings Banks are the
property of the people, and where the biggest
corporations are the mutual life assurance
offices, with between £70,000,000 and
£80,000,000 of assets the property of about
1,000,000 co-operators ; and where the biggest
enterprise of all, the railways with
£220,000,000 of capital, is the property of
everybody. Still it is perhaps necessary to
persuade the Australian artisan that he is a
skeleton fighting with lower animals in the
gutter for a crust in order to induce him
to entrust a few high-salaried Judds with the
job of saving him and putting some flesh on
his haggard bones. According to the scheme,
some 300 officials in the employ of the One
Big Union are to save the emaciated toiler
at an average wage of nearly £3OO each per
annum.
* * * *
The men who are building up the plan of
the Inner Kingdom, whereof the Parliament
publishes no Hansahd and meets with
closed doors and the garment of mystery
hung over the keyhole, have a bitter anil
undisguised hatred of politicians. That is
only natural. The aggregate politician repre-
sents, so far as the electoral system permits,
the whole people, and his constituency is
one from which ho man or woman can be
expelled except for proved crime or lunacy.
The politician of the Inner Oligarchy pro-
poses to represent a selected part of the
people, which part expects by strict organi-
sation to boss the whole, and the ways of
expelling or excluding the constituent are
numerous. (Thus in Brisbane the other day
four returned soldiers, who wished to prac-
tise their trades, had the union doors shut
in their faces with a slam, apparently be-
cause they were returned soldiers, so they
won’t be constituents.) Also, the movement
proposes to establish its own workers’ bank
—the Bank of the People Inside—as against
the Commonwealth institution, which is the
Bank of All the People. There is no possible
objection to a Bank of the Feople Inside so
long as it doesn’t demand subsidies from the
community in general. But it is interesting to
note that the demand that the Bank of All the
People shall be “put on a democratic basis” and
“revolutionised” has long been insistent
among the folk with the Song of Hate and
the great love for the Prussian mode of dis-
cipline. The position is understandable in
one sense, The Commonwealth Bank cer-
tainly has no capitalistic shareholders. It
has no capital; the small loan from the
Treasury with which it started has been
repaid out of earnings, so it has cost the
people nothing. It is reckoned that it has
saved Australia £3,266,000 by floating and
managing foreign loans at a lower commis-
sion rate than the joint-stock banks used
to charge—not £3,266,000 for politicians to
blew on railways in their electorates, but
that amount saved all the same. And it
accumulated in its first 5| years about
£760,000 of reserves (the current half-year’s
figures will greatly increase that) —not re-
serves to be spent with a free and patronis-
ing hand by self-constituted saviours of the
community and regenerators of society, but
the property of the people none the less.
And if it has done any injury the sufferers
have been not the “workers,” but the joint-
stock banks into whose bpsiness it has cut.
But it treats political and trades-union
potentates like common mortals. It is too
democratic to recognise their special dig-
nity. They can’t drop in casually and call
the manager “Bill,” and glance at the
ledgers to see what political friend or enemy
has got an overdraft, or look at the bills pay-
able in order to collect campaign informa-
tion, or advise that constituent Smith
should have an advance or hold pleasant,
informal political committee meetings in a
side room. In fact the concern looks for all
the world like a stuck-up formal capitalistic
affair, and the happy spirit of brotherhood
is not in it.
* * * *
The One Big Union appears to have at
least one eye on the control of the Press.
Hitherto there has been a tendency to regard
an editor, because he engages and supervises
the staff, rather as an employer or capital-
ist’s minion than as an oppressed toiler.
But the One Big Union includes him in the
list, putting him in Grotip 11, along with
barmaids and fishermen. Presumably the
idea is that the man who runs the policy of
the paper must be either a unionist or a
“scab.” If he is a “scab.” either because
he won’t join the union or because the union
won’t have him, he must quit his job, else
there will be a very large strike, in which
the barmaids, chambermaids and fishermen
will be called out among others. His union-
ist successor will, of course, be bound to
the union policy, and if he departs from it
in his paper he may be • cast out as an
unworthy member, and created a “scab” and
then be removed from his billet for being a
“scab.” One way and another it looks as
if there may be, under the One Big Union, a
rather cold time for papers, or at least for
pressmen, who don’t subscribe unreservedly
to the One Big Union idea of things.
THE TROUT AND BLUEBELL
METROPOLIS.
‘‘Billy Bluesiiirt” writes: —
What is responsible for the policy of misrepresen-
tation adopted by Tiie Bulletin with regard to
Canberra? Arriving from Victoria, where the Fed-
eral Capital Territory is generally- accepted to be
an arid and wind-swept waste, I was agreeably sur-
prised to discover a noble site ideally situated for
a great city. I was particularly struck by its
typical Australian features. A broad grassy plain
rolls back in gentle undulations from the rich
alluvial flats of the Molonglo to abrupt foothills
covered with open box and gum forests. Beyond,
the blue cloud-wreathed- peaks of the Great Dividing
Range ever sing the majesty of their Creator. The
winter climate is unbeatable. Warm sunny days
follow frosty nights, and the keen, snappy air fills
one with the joy of life. In the Spring the air is
heavy with the scent of wild flowers. I have neier
anywhere seen bluebells grow to such a size. The
Cotter is a dashing mountain stream of sparkling
water, rushing merrily between frowning gorges
and rows of stately oaks until it joins the ’Bidgee.
Unfortunately the Territory has been the shuttle-
cock of various self-seeking politicians, and grossly
exaggerated reports of waste and extravagance ha\e
been assiduously disseminated. The work so far
done has been developmental, and the seeker for
something spectacular will no doubt be disappointed.
The water-supply system is now almost completed,


and the power-house and electrical equipment and
mains are the admiration of every visiting electrical
expert.
Unfortunately work on the sewerage system was
stopped before completion, on the advice of one man
(the Chief Engineer of the Melbourne Metropolitan
Board of Works), and this notwithstanding the fact
that it had been chosen by the Public Works Com-
mittee after considering all available expert evidence.
It is interesting to note in this connection that
Mr. Oliver did not instal the septic-tank system he
advocated for Canberra in the eastern suburbs of
Melbourne, recently, but adopted the very system
he so ruthlessly condemned before the recent Com-
mission, and this despite the fact that a tremendous
additional strain was placed on the already over-
burdened Spottiswood pumping-station.
In the mountain streams within the Territory
some of the finest trout-fishing in Australia is to
be had. The country is sound and on the whole of
average quality. Anyhow, you don’t want to estab-
lish a capital city to grow spuds.
Tree planting on a considerable scale is being
carried out, and the nurseries, although recently
established, are an education. The work so far has
been for the most part experimental, but it has
been conclusively demonstrated that the Canberra
Plains will successfully grow practically all tem-
perate fruits and cereals. The finest apples I have
ever tasted were grown within a stone’s-throw of the
capital site.
The revenue from the Territory is considerably
greater than the expenditure. The roads are well
made, well maintained, and are a pattern to the
municipalities around.
Why not let Australia know the truth, that in the
Federal Capital Territory they have a great
national asset, containing a site for a capital city
second to none in the world for climate, position and
location.
* # * *
Getting down to details, and leaving out
the cloud-wreathed peaks of bunkum and the
forests of bosh on the hills, this is the situ-
ation :
No one said Canberra was espefcially “arid.” It
is just plain second-class soil. It wasn’t described
as more “windswept” than any other elevated place
of its size. It grows grass, which is not uncommon.
The average rainfall at the adjacent observing station
of Cooma is the smallest recorded for all the coastal
and tableland regions of N. S. Wales, and it is
smaller than that of any place on the western slopes,
barring Urana. The rainfall at the other adjacent
station, Queanbeyan, is less than that of any station
on the N. S Wales coast or tableland barring Cooma,
and is inferior to Moree, Warialda, Bingara, Narrabri,
Gunnedah, Coonabarabran, Quirindi, Young, Murrum-
burrah or Albury on the western slopes. The site was
thus a place that N. S. Wales could well spare.
The Cotter is a dashing stream after rain. So is
the Petersham storm-water channel. Being a very
short creek —so short that when the Federal Statis-
tician published in one of his early editions a list of
the rivers and creeks of Australia it wasn’t men-
tioned—it doesn’t dash long. The writer has walked
over it without getting his feet wet. It is perhaps in
the category of Sydney’s old Tank Stream, now run-
ning gaily to the sea below Pitt-street. Being mostly
below the level of the capital any very large water-
supply will involve pumping.
The climate is the same as that of any other place
at the same elevation. The bluebells are beautiful,
like the wild flowers and umbrageous forest that used
to be in Paddington, Sydney. But bluebells and
even trout have a tendency to disappear when a city
grows, and the scent of Spring becomes just like the
Spring scent in Flinders-street, Melbourne. Anyhow,
Australia can’t afford a fishing metropolis. The place
wouldn’t pay its way.
It is quite true that money has been spent on a
■water-supply and a sewerage system, and on tree-
planting (as if there weren’t forests of box there
already), on nurseries (as if the Trout and Bluebell
Metropolis was to be a horticultural centre), and on
“temperate fruits and cereals,” including apples. It
has all been a spending of money. When is a decent
bit of revenue going to come in?
It is infernally cheeky to say that “the
revenue from the Territory is considerably greater
than the expenditure.” Recent figures show the
year’s expenditure to be £105,972, not including
interest and having no reference to works at Jervis
Bay. (That was the greatly-reduced expenditure
owing to the war. In 1915 the outgoings were
£212,505. In 1914 they were £251,669.) And the
revenue was £22,559, of which £20,133 was raised
by selling or leasing the public lands handed over
with the capital city. The rest consisted of £l6Ol
earned by agistment—by taking in cows to grass—
and £825 from rates. The rates are real, honest,
dinkum revenue, and they aren’t much for 912 square
miles —say, 583,680 acres of Trout and Bluebell
Metropolis. In “Billy Blueshirt’s” own State of
Victoria there are only four or five petty munici-
palities (mostly dead alluvial fields) that don’t make
a better showing.
The population of the whole Federal capital terri-
tory—not the town of Canberra, but the entire 912
square miles — is given thus in Knibbs’s Quarterly
Summary of Australian Statistics dated March, 1918,
issued at 14d. so as to be within the reach of the
most enthusiastic trout-fisher of the capital city:—
1913 .. .. 2449 1916 .. .. 2613
1914 .. .. 2646 1917 .. .. 2493
1915 .. .. 2467
That includes all the persons in Federal employ.
And it took over £83,000 of public money—the dif-
ference between revenue and expenditure—to keep
the diminished population going, say £33 per
head, or £165 for a family of five. The total year’s
revenue was about £25 per square mile, or 9d. per
acre, including land-sale money, which wasn’t
revenue. The population is a shade over 2i people
to the square mile, which is much less than the
average population of Queensland, N. S. Wales, Vic-
toria, Tasmania and South Australia all lumped
together. And if they required an annual subsidy
of £9l per square mile —£91 per square mile over
and above income—it would cost roughly
£134,145,739 (one hundred and thirty-four millions
odd) per year to keep them going. They would cost
more than a war.
The complaint against the Bluebell and Trout
Metropolis is that it lacks visible honest means of
support. Its rivulet is too low down to be even a
cheap supply of drinking water. There is no
abundance of cheap water-power for manufacturing
purposes. It is away from the known coal and
gold deposits. There is no harbor or navigable river
to make it a commercial city by sea. It isn’t on any
great land route of commerce any more than Berry
or Rylestone. It isn’t in the same category with
Dungog, which is at least on a great though un-
finished main line, or Culcairn, where three railways
meet. The Canberra capital will live so long as
the people send money to support it—that is to say,
it will probably live a lot better than it is doing
now, if they send a lot more money. When they
cease to send the money it will die. It is a remit-
tance woman with a bluebell in her hair.
There are political capitals that prosper, even
though they lack special facilities for manufacturing,
which the original proposed capital at Dalgety
would have had in some degree by reason of its
water-power.
Ottawa (population 87,000) has grown to be the
fifth city in Canada, but then it is on a big river—
in fact, three rivers meet there and six railways,
and it is on a high-road of traffic between the
Atlantic and the Pacific.
Washington (population 331,069) has grown to
be the 16th city in the United States. But then it
has a great river alongside (in fact, one-seventh of
the U.S. Federal territory is water), and there are
some coal and iron deposits close at hand. Also a
number of important railways meet there, and it
is on lines which run to the Pacific, to Mexico and to
Canada.
Pretoria (with 29,618 white people) is the seat of
government of South Africa and the fourth city. It
has all the drawbacks of Canberra save that it is in
the middle of a big and fairly level country instead
of near the edge, so that commerce had a way of
dropping in there. Also, it is a place where four
railways meet; it is only a few miles from the
world’s greatest goldfield; and through being for
many years the political, commercial and military
centre of a fighting race it has become a national
habit.
Canberra town, at last census, was about No.
2000 among the towns of N. S. Wales alone. There
are 30 closely-printed columns of Cornstalk towns in
the Australian Year Book for 1917, and ’Berra’s
place among them is worth investigation.
This is pretty well all that there is to say about
Australia’s Trout and Bluebell Metropolis. It was
intended to be a failure so that it wouldn’t rival
Melbourne and Sydney, and it. just is.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Chadband and Stiggins Take Tickets
in Tatt’s.
Anyone who happened to be asked whether
the rush of the Federal Income Tax Depart-
ment at the winner of a prize in Tatt.’s
should be a subject for laughter or scorn
might have to think twice over his answer.
There are two ways of contemplating Chad-
band, and public policy was never so satu-
rated with hypocrisy as in the attitude of
the Commonwealth to the consultation in the
Speck. First of all, the Post Office refuses
to carry letters to the promoters of the
sweep and objects to remitting cash. Also,
it noses round after individuals who are
supposed to act as go-betweens aiM puts
their addresses on a black-list. Generally
speaking, its attitude towards the sweep is
the same as it takes up towards Blood-
thurst and Goreburst Ltd., of Hamburg,
and the Sultan of Turkey—except at Hobart,
where it pockets substantial revenue from
the sale of stamps incidental to the conduct
of the frequent sweep. Alongside of all this
elaborate effort to officially repudiate the
sweep and make it impossible, the Common-
wealth collects a tax from the promoters on
each ticket in the gamble, demands a share
of every prize distributed, and is now getting
ready to go in pursuit of income tax from
the individual prize-winner. Having made
up its mind to take a further bite out of the
cake it objects to being manufactured, the
Commonwealth has decided to take the bites
it omitted to take before, and for this pur-
pose it is out after every winner of a prize
since 1915. Whether the winner of a prize,
who -has already been taxed on his ticket,
should pay an additional tax to the Govern-
ment is a question on which there is room
for difference of opinion, though the general
consensus of opinion would probably be that
he deserved little sympathy, anyway. But
that a Government should make a parade of
virtue by spending time and money trying to
block a sweep, and then spend more time and
money surreptitiously appropriating large
lumps of the proceeds thereof for revenue
purposes, is an example of the way to live in
a whited sepulchre that would have caused
the original Chadband to be paralysed with
admiration. Either this sweep is an evil
thing or it is not. In any case, it should be
either stopped or allowed to stand up in the
open. The present position of the national
Government towards the thing is evil,
for it gives to Australian policy a tinge of
sneaking imposture that is revolting to con-
template and sets before the people a stan-
dard of evasiveness and moral trickery which
in an individual citizen would indicate base-
ness of the worst kind.
c*
Uncalled-for Tears.
One of the N. S. Wales Industrial Court
Judges, Curlewis, made a queer break the
other day, and the episode moved S. M.
Herald to a column of comment from which it
transpired that Curlewis had in some way,
not particularised, directed public attention
to “the unhappy situation of the working-
man of moderate views” who finds himself
forced into joining a union. There had been
no evidence to show that anybody was un-
happy or that moderate views were in colli-
sion with the other sort. And in spite of the
complaints from Hunter-street about the
working-man’s lack of logic, its own logic
appeared badly in need of repair. Curlewis
had before him the case of a plasterer against
whom judgment had been given by the Indus-
trial Magistrate for £5 9s. 6d., being 224
weeks subscriptions, less 12s. 6d. paid, to the
Operative Plasterers’ Union. At the hearing
before the magistrate the defendant did not
appear to resist the union claim, but he
materialised in Curlewis’s court as the
appellant against the magistrate’s finding.
The man’s membership of the union was not
seriously disputed, nor was the general lia-
bility of members of a union for fees chal-
lenged. But it was alleged that the appel-
lant had been compelled to join the union
under duress —that a threat had been made
by an official to call out the men with whom
the appellant was working unless he became
a member. The appeal turned on whether in
such circumstances the plasterer should be
regarded a member of the union for recovery
of fees, and Curlewis, finding as a fact that
consent to join had been procured by the
alleged threat, held that he should not. Where-
fore he allowed the appeal. Also he thought
the matter over:—
Unions must not enforce their excellent argument
for attracting membership by saying to the non-
unionists, “Either see the validity of our reasoning
or starve.”
Furthermore, it had to be remembered that
there were some men who objected to joining
a union and expressed their attitude this
way:—■
We object to getting entangled in politics; we
object to being hounded, or hounding others, out of
the Labor movement if we or they happen to hold
conscientious views differing from those of the
majority. And we also object to being compelled to
choose between the alternative of joining in strikes
undertaken in defiance of the law, in total disregard
of our duties to our country, and of the solemn
obligations of our agreements—strikes that assist the
Germans and stab our fighting men in the back, or
subject ourselves and our helpless wives and children
to foul abuse.
And this, to wind up with: —
I express no opinion here as to the rights or
wrongs of military conscription: I simply say that,
if it is slavery, industrial conscription is no better.
Followed by approval from S.M.H. and the
remark that—
The more militant unions display great bitterness
towards men who, though receiving the benefits of
union organisation, refuse to become members, yet
these are the unions which, above all others, fought
the conscription proposal, in which exactly the
same principle was involved.
Whether, having found as a fact that his
plasterer became a unionist because of a
threat by the union secretary to call
the men off the job, Curlewis was justified
in giving the decision he did is open to seri-
ous doubt. Taking the groundwork of
fact in the appeal, it would have been
much more reasonable for the Bench
to have dismissed the application for
annulment of the original judgment with a
judicial intimation that it was up to the
Employers’ Union to reimburse the appellant
than for the Court to give the judgment it
did. There are two reasons for this. One
is that on the appellant’s own showing his
action in joining the union had prevented
the employer being tangled up in a strike.
The other is that the judgment, instead of
doing anything to protect the hypothetical
“workin’-man” who is forced to join unions
against his natural impulse and to the
outrage of his political sensibilities,
will do exactly the reverse. In the
case before Curlewis a strike had
been prevented by roping the man into
the union. For not striking, or for failing to
withdraw from industry or something of the
sort, the union is robbed of its dues and ser-
monised at great length. If the unionists
had actually walked off the job or not turned
up, and the object of Curlewis’s compassion
had hurriedly motored round in pursuit of
the secretary and applied for a membership
no one had mentioned to him and there had
been great uproar in the Tory press about
an important industry being paralysed, and
finally the lamb had gone into camp with the
lions—well, then, Curlewis could have done
nothing except stand afar off and observe
that the lamb had not been threatened
by the other animals. All this talk
about the worker who objects to join-
ing a union and doing various things
in defiance of the law and his conscience, as
an alternative to remaining a non-unionist
and starving, is nonsense—the outcome
of the legal mind playing with words. It is
no use looking at this business through a
library window. Long experience has taught
the man who works for a wage that one of
the greatest dangers to him is the non-
unionist, who is invariably either a sponger
or the pliant tool of the boss. Every work-
man who is neither of these things knows
that without his union he would be powerless
to hold his own in the economic struggle. A
century of history proves this to him. His
own experiences confirms it. Every political
economist repeats it and warns him what to
expect if he forgets. Very often, no doubt,
a worker has reason to object to the policy
followed by his union. Terrific squabbles on
the subject are common. But he knows that
nothing his union will do is a circumstance
compared with what the unions of the other
party would perpetrate on him. It is not
true to say that the alternative before a
worker is to join the union or starve. The
real alternative is the other way round —
starve or join the union. And when he is a
unionist the alternative is to work with
union men alone or run continual risk of
being torpedoed. To call trader unionism
“industrial conscription” and “slavery” may
sound the quintessence of wisdom to the
employers’ newspapers. To the workers it
sounds at the best like a rather feeble joke
and at the worst like a revelation of judicial
obfuscation. The whole literature of the
world rises up in protest against the notions
embodied in such analogies.

Cinderella.
Seeing that two of the States are in receipt
of relief from the Commonwealth it is mar-
vellous how South Australia keeps on its
legs, even if they are its last ones. It is the
driest of the crowd. There are 1.105.000
square miles of the Commonwealth with less
than lOin. of rain per annum, and S.A. owns
317,000 of them, which is a good deal out of
the State’s total area of 380,000 square miles.
There are 392,110 square miles with over
30in. of rain, and S.A.’s poor little share is
1048 miles. In other words, it has nearly
three times its fair proportion of drought, and
only about 2 per cent, of its fair proportion
of rain. Little Tasmania has 14 times as
much well-watered soil. It began to produce
gold in 1856, and its total output since then
has been about £1,000,000. Victoria since
1851 has never produced less than that in a
year. Its tin, coal and silver are practically
nil. It doesn’t possess a single permanent
river of its own worth calling such ; there are
big casual rivers that flow when they feel
inclined, but they disappear into the salt
lakes. South Aus. possesses the driest capital
in Australia. The Federal Statistician fur-
nishes a list of phenomenal rainfalls —nearly
four pages of them going back to 1544; and
S.A. isn’t mentioned. It never bad a phe-
nomenal rainfall. Out of 102,000,000 acres in
the Commonwealth described as “forest” it
has less than 4,000,000 acres—about one-
eighth part of its share according to size. A
good deal of the famous Nullarbor Plain,
where the transcontinental train runs for 300
miles as straight as an arrow because there
isn’t even a hillock to dodge, belongs to S.A.
and gets its name from the fact that there
isn’t a tree in the whole diocese. The State
is short of mountains; in fact, part of it, out
of sheer cussedness is below sea-level. The
lakes are numerous and almost all are salt,
and many of them dry up when they are
tired. The harbors are fairly numerous, but
the coast-line doesn’t face towards Asia or
Africa or America or Maoriland, or any other
place of trade; it looks straight towards the
South Pole. Yet S.A. holds up its head man-
fully, asks no alms, turns out more manufac-
tured goods than Westralia and Tasmania
combined ; beats Queensland, Westralia and
Tasmania combined as a wheat-grower; has
the best show per head of savings-bank
deposits ; possesses the show city of Australia
with far too many people in it in proportion
to the rest of the population, and has the
most awful debt per head in the Common-
wealth, barring Westralia’s. On the whole,
and despite its debt, it is about the best
example of pluck and self-reliance that Aus-
tralia possesses.
Arbitration Considered Without
Prejudice.
It being the fashion now to decry industrial
arbitration as a failure—those who declare
it an utter failure are ready to show the
better way in return for a steady salary—
it is fitting to consider what the despised
expedient has done. This is its record for
all Australia:—
Number of People
whose Wages Total Total
were changed net rise net rise
by the Courts, per week. per vear.
1913 .. 166,132 £37,713. £1,96i,070
1914 .. 125,218 30,685 1,595,620
1915 . . 197,410 51,905 2,699,060
1916 . . 492,487 142,923 7,431,996
1917 . . 292,910 81,007 4,212,364
£344,233 £17,900,116
There were a very few cases in which wages
were reduced a trifle, but they were very,
very few, and after taking off the reductions
there was a net increase of £17,900,116 per
annum of wages. Apart from that a great
many increases were secured outside the
court. Unfortunately the increases led in
some cases to an industry shutting up in
whole or in part, for in addition to the ex-
pansion of wages granted by the Court there
were many reductions of working hours,
many strikes and an increasing tendency to
“go slow,” causing a diminished output. So
expenses sometimes became greater than the
value of production. Factories diminished
and so did the cultivated area, likewise the
number of employees declined all round. But
the Arbitration Courts attended to the work
of pushing up wages in a strenuous fashion.
The grievance is that the Courts do their
business automatically. No politician in or
out of Parliament can point to a single item
in the £17,900,000 per annum of extra wages
and say :“I did this! Alone I did this ! For
this yon are indebted to me! Therefore vote
for me.” The politicians got their advt. when
they established the courts and said they were
good. Now the only advt. thev can get is
by destroying the courts and saying they are
dashed bad and always were.
Beginning to Think of Pondering.
The idea that vigorous work, even accom-
panied by greatly increased earnings, is a
crime, seems incurable. Dealing with one
of the interminable shipbuilding disputes—a
mere bud of disagreement on the great forest
of negotiation — Justice Higgins listened to
the Victorian organiser of the A.S.E. say:—
We are quite amenable to reason, and if it can
be established that piecework is essential to the
quick building of ships, we will have no hesitation
in CONSIDERING THE MATTER.
In other words, there will be no hesitation
in beginning to officially hesitate over the
matter. Yet surely it isn’t worth while to
wait. This country obviously wants ships
and heaps of ships, and wants them in great
haste. It has 400 shiploads of wheat alone
waiting for ships. It has wool, meat and
all sorts of things waiting for ships. It has
shut up its painfully-acquired South African
trade for want of ships. There are £26.000
worth of cargo for South Africa alone waiting
at the one port of Melbourne for want of
ships. The railways are drying up for
want of material carried by ships. Even
before the war we wanted" ships so badly
that we were almost wholly dependent on
outside capitalists—British, Hun, Dutch.
Swedish, French. Italian, American and
Japanese—for ships. We will want ships
so long as Australia is surrounded by sea
and until the air vessel comes into its own.
and then we will want airships. It is per-
fectly well known that piecework won’t make
construction slower. Such a thing is impos-
sible. It is practically certain that it will
make it a lot faster, unless self-interest is a
dead sin and the desire for money is an
extinct iniquity. It can be made to yield much
better wages. And yet the sluggish union
official can only say that his people will have
no hesitation in beginning to ponder over
putting on a remunerative spurt if the neces-
sity of it can be shown to them. If they
are resolved not to see it nobody can show it
to them.
The Little Rushes.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Still our infantry went on in little rushes, the
bayonets continually flashing in the evening sunlight.”
—Cable to Australia.
Still they go on in short and sudden rushes,
The while the great guns thunder in the
rear;
The low red sun the evening landscape
flushes,
As though the dying day would raise a cheer
For that fine valor, held so long in check,
That makes the Prussian line a death-strewn
wreck.
Up the low hill, across the nearer valley,
But with no pause to give the foeman breath,
Strike home before their mighty legions rally
And write the name of victory in death.
The little rushes and the shining steel
Mark where proud Freedom makes her
grand appeal.
Still was it so since first the earth went
spinning
In merry circles round the glowing sun;
The little rushes were the fine beginning
Of each tremendous battle man has won.
The big battalions on the other side
Broke in those waves that marked the turn-
ing tide.
N.S.W.
MAY McC.

The Place Where the Furnace Roars.
Bill, mr seems to have had a busy day or
two during the proceedings south of the
Somme last week. And it is better to hear
that the losses among our lads were very
slight than that they accounted for many
Germans. These troops' of ours have a lot of
fighting yet to do, and the more there are of
them left to do it the better. The battle in
which they appear to have been the spear-
head had died down at the beginning of the
week, with the German in an uncomfortable
position, and much doubt existing about what
he was going to do next. Judging by cap-
tured documents it looks as if he may spend
a not inconsiderable time gnashing his teeth
together and groaning over what he might
have done if Focii and llaig had not done
something else. There is a report that
three generals commanding between Mont-
didier and Amiens have been recalled to Ber-
lin in disgrace. This is not improbable.
The German has never shown any hesi-
tation about scrapping generals. Dozens
of them have been outed since the war
began. There is a further report that the
group of armies thereabouts have been
placed under Von Boeiim, the first occasion
on which anyone less than a prince or a
duke has been made a group boss
on the Western Front. This is only
partly true, for Ludendorff, who bosses the
lot, is neither a prince nor a duke, nor was
either Von Moltke or Von Falkenhayn.
The explanation given of Von Boehm’s
promotion—that he has been given this big-
ger command in order that he may be made
the scapegoat for some anticipated disasters
-—is obyiously a clumsy invention. Von
Boeiim is one of Germany’s most successful
generals, and is without doubt a very able
soldier. It was he who scored so heavily at
St. Quentin, and who latterly dragged the
armies of the Marne back to the Vesle. His
promotion can be easily accounted for on
the ground that the princes and the dukes
are getting found out. The galling things
for the Kaiser to contemplate are that at
the. two points where .the Allies were most
seriously threatened his armies have been
thrown back badly mauled, and that the
American is running to time-table. This
American excursion 1o France is much the
more disturbing incident of the two. And
Uncle Sam having given the submarine a
miss in baulk, this paper has been wondering
lately how much longer it will be before
something in the way of a big naval battle
transpires. To get some of his warships into
the Atlantic the Kaiser might not unnatu-
rally think it worth taking"a lot of risks
with others.

The Tory Loafer.
The demand of a section of the Tory press
that no Government should do anything until
the war is over, except shout about winning
the war, is a deadly nuisance. Even State
Governments, which have no official connec-
tion with the war, are appealed to in this
fashion. The tariff is, of course, to be left
alone till after the war. Now the day that
peace is established the ships—more ships
than ever before, judging by the rate at
which they are being built in countries that
hardly touched that kind of business in other
days—will begin to rush along the cheap
labor goods. And they will be very abundant
goods, for the world —except in Australia—
has learned to work as it never worked
before, and the disbanding of the armies will
throw perhaps 20,000,000 men back into an
industrial market that has almost learned
to do without them. It will take about two
years to fix up our new tariff if we don’t
begin till after the war, and two years of
fierce cheap competition will leave few in-
dustries in Australia —in fact, there are few
now, and they are decreasing. And the
absurd Tory dailies of Sydney object to the
Holman Ministry’s Local Government Bill
on the ground that it is a “contentious
measure” which should be left over till after
the war. Even the proposal to run National-
ist candidates for Sydney City Council roused
one paper to complain that this wasn’t a
war measure. The S. M. Herald lias gravely
stated that the present N. S. Wales State
Assembly (which has nothing to do with
the war) was elected on the crest of a
“wave of national enthusiasm” (as if it
could be elected on an upheaval of national
depression), and therefore it should do
nothing but be earnest about the war. The
other side of the case may be put something
like this: —
There is too much going slow already. Also there
are too many arrears to be caught up.
Things won’t stop happening till the war is over.
The Tory theory is that Parliament, which is created
and paid to see that they happen the right way if
possible, should just let them happen. Old laws,
old methods, old machinery (industrial and political),
old pavements, old, sluggish vehicles for carrying
mails, old paddle-steamers that eat more coal than
they are worth, wear out or become obsolete. If
they are not repaired or replaced by new things, then
the end of a six years’ war will leave us six years
behind the times, and the political or financial or
social road or just .plain road with a six-year-old hole
in it will be impassable.
In fact, we will be 26 years behind the times.
Nations like BritaiJn, the United States, Canada,
France (behind the fighting-line) are crowding tour
years into one in the effort to find out how, by all
manner of improvements, they may enable one per-
son to do the work of many.
This is the time a! love all others when Australian
State Legislatures especially should repair the
mechanism of the country. It is their easy time.
They have no war on their hands. They have fewer
public works to do, fen - loans are falling off. They
have fewer men to fii id employment for on public
works, because so many’ have gone to the war. This
is a quiet spell for ope ning a few new ports, finding
out why people are getting off the land (they may
be all off the land if vie wait till the war is over),
finding out why two-th irds of the officials in some
public service departmt nits are “temporary” (they
may' be all temporary if we wait till the war is
over), and putting the half-hundred municipalities
of Sydney on a sane footing.
If the Government par ty in any Legislature learns
that it is to do nothin; \ for perhaps another four
years, save look at the lower button of its waistcoat
and listen to the public (lebt growing, it will begin
to stay away. Then the Opposition, which has no
idea of waiting till the w fir is over, will often have
a majority, and may amuse itself by rejecting the
Estimates, and thus stoppfmg the pay of the police,
appointing Royal Commisslions of its own party to
find that all its I.W.W. friends were not guilty
and should be largely compensated, and cabling sym-
pathy to the Bolsheviks at the pufilic expense.
And before the war is over a disgusted people will
probably put such a party into power because they
would rather see bad things done than nothing done
at all.
Anyhow, if the crowd which was returned on the
crest of a wave of public enthusiaxun does things
that the coonrnunity doesn’t like, it is competent for
a different Legislature, chosen at the bottom of a
vale of public apathy and stodge, to undo them.
The work will at least prevent it becoming mouldy.
