Another Blighty.
The combination called the British War
Cabinet is a thing of the moment —-the result
Of compromise and stress. It is a Cabinet
within a Cabinet, and a mighty interesting
experiment in Britain. The outfit known as
the Imperial War Cabinet is not quite the
same thing. No one seems to know what it
does or what it is responsible for. The
responsibility of Lloyd George and Bonar
Law and other members of the War Cabinet
constructed out of the British Ministry to
the House of Commons is absolute; but the
House of Commons knows nothing about
Borden, Hughes and Massey in any official
sense in the war plan. All it knows about
Hughes and co. is that as representatives of
oversea communities that are up to their
neck in the war these p>arsons are in some
vague way “conferring” with the British
Ministers about the proceedings. The posi-
tion is just about as an< mialous as it well
could be, yet, in a way, ;it was inescapable.
In every essential point this Imperial War
Cabinet is a sham—an ' attempt to make
something out of nothing and to give it the
appearance of reality by ta. Iking mysteriously
about its great usefulness and importance.
Except in the most remote way it is an
entirely irresponsible bortj y for obviously
enough, though Australia may have some
measure of control over Hughes, it has not
even the shadow of authority over Lloyd
George, Borden and Massey. There is now
the proposal that Austral ia and other coun-
tries shall have permanent representation
on the War Council, or rather that their
representatives shall be remitted to all sit-
tings of the Council other than those
attended by Prime Mil listers. And the
suggestion is made that; Senator Pearce
shall be the man from Australia. The
bizarre character of this suggestion entitles
it to admiration. Peal ice fe certainly an
individual the Commonwealth could do with-
out for quite a long tir ie, but before the
departure of Pearce or a ntyone else London-
wards, there is the p>reliminary ques-
tion of whether the Con innonwealth Parlia-
ment is ready to put a lather Minister on
the pay-roll to discharge m jnsterious functions
at the other end of the earth. From what
has been seen of that 1 ’atrliament hitherto
the probabilities are t hint a substantial
majority thereof is ready to do the deed and
that the opposition of the ■minority would be
tempered by the reflect’ ion that when it
becomes a majority the' :e will be another
attractive billet to fill. ! ill this is no reason
why the majority shoal I pretend that the
Minister-Resident-in-Lonc lon scheme has any-
thing to do with the wa: -. Tbe idea is that
the “Imperial War Cabi net” having formed
the nucleus of an Imperial organisation, this
should be maintained sej that .there may be
something in the naturi* of an accomplished
fact when the war e xpires*.. Of course it
will not be much of a tact, but. that matters
very little just now. Meantime, assuming
the possibility of PKiauce being manoeuvred
away, The Bulletin.; hopes for a preliminary
explanation of what, hold .the Commonwealth
Parliament would bav-e on. him in the event
of him muddling the job over yonder—of
how he could be challenged' or otherwise
dealt with —and of ’now’ the practical dis-
franchisement of his electoral supporters
would be provided for. These minor points
are at least worth paying attention to.
Meat Muddles and Other Muddles.
A letter about oxen and wattle and sun-
dries :— ■
If this country is any kind of country in particular
it is a gold- and food-producing land. (Wool is only
a substance that grows on food as a side-line, and is
cut off once a year.) Yet at time of writing the
cities (at least Sydney, and to a less degree Mel-
bourne) are short of eggs, butter, onions and especi-
ally meat—there is a famine in meat. Also a very
large proportion of gold-mines are closing or going to
close because of the enormous advance in the cost of
imported iron machinery, and the ruinous freights
charged by imported ships, and the difficulty of get-
ting the stuff imported at any price. The rich mine
which in pre-war days spent only £2 2s. 6d. in
getting an ounce of fine gold worth £4 ss. is still all
right, but the poor one which spent £4 in getting
an ounce of gold has gone dead for an indefinite period,
unless it is helped with a subsidy. And nowadays
rich mines are few and poor ones many. The in-
crease in wages helped the increase in the cost of
imported machinery to break up the gold industry,
but increased wages were inevitable owing to the
enormous increase in the cost of imported goods of
all sorts, including building material, which latter
item, along with increased wages, skied the price of
houses. Then the increased price of imported loans
helped on the trouble.
The local food scarcity is a recent development, but
it was only to be expected amid the general confu-
sion. It was required to complete the symmetry of
chaos and round off the cosmos of muddle. Now it
only needs a scarcity of drought and a disastrous
famine of strikes and caterpillars to make things all
in order. We may even run short of locusts and
have to import them.
The culmination of the trouble is set down to the
fact that stock-owners resent the fixing of meat
prices and have combined to keep their animals off
the market, but that doesn't account for the butter
and onion racket. Nor does it wholly account for
the mutton racket, for jumbuck-raising is now
largely a poor man’s industry, and the number of
sheep-owners is so enormous that combination is
difficult. No doubt the reduction in prices decidAi a
great many that it would pay better to wait a while
and get the wool off before selling, for the price of
wool wasn’t reduced ; in other words, the sheep was
changed by price-fixing from a meat implement to a
wool implement.
As for the bullock, it is up against a matter
which hadn’t been much taken into account. In a
very great part of the ox-country, including much
more than half N. S. Wales, the ox isn’t expected to
fatten except in rare seasons. He has to travel over
too many acres a day in search of food, and becomes
an active beast almost fit for tree-climbing. The
outline or framework of this creature is sold, and the
buyer puts him to fatten on grass that is too valu-
able to be used for other purposes. Presumably if
the wholesale seller, instead of the wholesale buyer,
did this final fattening the result to the retail eater
would be much the same. The wholesale buyer with
a lot of stock on his hands when the price reduction
came tried to put an extra cwt. on the purchased
framework before selling it, so as to avoid loss, for
it would be his loss—not that of the man who grew
and sold the sketch of the beast; and the delay while
waiting for that extra cwt. struck the market hard.
Also, the cattle-man doubtless tried to be very nasty.
But he isn’t responsible for butter and onions or
potatoes or the fearful price of eggs and a lot of
other things, so there must be other causes.
The number of sheep slaughtered in N. S. Wales
dropped from 6,000,000 in 1914 to 4,000,000 in 1915,
and the number of cattle slaughtered diminished
nearly one-half in the same time, so the trouble
began long ago and the reason isn’t explained. The
stocks of sheep and cattle and horses are all less
than they were before the war, and the cause of that
isn’t explained either. Area under cultivation is
diminishing also, and that isn’t accounted for any
more than the rest. Some things are certainly
known. In the principal States the meat animal
(like the wheat) has, every year, further to travel to
market. In New South Wales, which isn’t the worst
of the States, the last official count dealt with 152
municipalities outside Sydney, and in 56 of them the
poprrmtion was dying out. The people had gone to
Sydney which is always growing, and the meat had
to follow them regardless of expense. Sometimes it
had to follow them 400 miles. Then the cost of
shifting the meat per mile has gone up, likewise the
wages of the shifter, and the difficulty of shifting
has increased, and the cost of slaughtering at the
State muddle at Ilomebush has increased, and but-
chers’ rents have increased. In fact everything
seems to have increased save the public capacity to
understand the cause of all this increase.
The commandeering of meat seems inevitable, but
to put the screw on about 20,000 sheep-owners in
N. S. Wales alone, fully 14,000 of them small owners,
seems as big a job as taking a census. (How many
ox-owners there may be this writer doesn’t know.)
Anyhow, a Government that can’t commandeer
recruits for the army, even when the country’s
existence is at stake, and which can’t commandeer
labor even at its own price, and which can’t fix
wages even when they have been fixed by law after
consultation with both parties, and then raised
above the rate thus fixed, and then unfixed again,
and which can’t fix the rate of interest on its
loans, doesn’t seem able to fix or commandeer any-
thing very well.
Further, it seems to me that when this country
has taken to importing wattle-bark from Africa, hav-
ing apparently to some extent forgotten how to
grow its own national vegetable, nothing is impos-
sible, not even eggs at 3d. each, and it is hardly
worth while for anybody to ask the reason of
anything. A while ago South Africa bought some
wattle-trees from us, and at latest advices it was
exporting 20 times as much bark as we do, and
even selling the product of our own treasured shrub
back to us.
There is something in the Scripture which forbade
the Israelites to plough with a hoax and an ass
harnessed together. I think that is the sort of
team which ploughs the field of Australian policy.
—— ♦ —-
The beauties of the foreign-trade gospel can
be easily detected by contemplating the
Red Cross Society’s shortage of wool. De-
mands are made upon the Society in N. S.
Wales for wool, incidental to war-work,
amounting to about 4001 b. The supply is
limited and precarious, and in order to meet
the demands in this and other States the
Australian branch has made arrangements
to procure direct supplies from England. At
present the Society gets a small helping
from the residuum of a local mill at ss. 6d.
per lb., which, to say the least, is a fairly
solid price. For the balance of the inad-
equate supply it is invoiced at Bs. 6d. per
lb., a figure which calls for charitable judg-
ment. We cannot abuse the Ilun for this.
The blame rests on our own heads.
Here is Australia, the greatest wool-produc-
ing country on earth, with millions of jum-
bucks’ waistcoats tied up in parcels along the
shore waiting for shipment—a country packed
with the raw material of manufactures for
which the outside world is always clamoring
—yet here, too, is Australia, in the pathetic
state of haying to depend on the wool sent
back across the sea to produce socks for the
nation’s soldiers. Of course this humiliating
evidence of political and industrial shirk was
bound to be forthcoming. But realisation of
that makes it no more tolerable. Everyone
almost can see now that the time was long
ago ripe to put Australian industries on the
sound basis of effectiveness. Whether quite
as many will continue to see the same thing
after the war is another matter. Meantime
details of this wool episode ought to be
posted up in public places as a reminder of
one of the things that happened from shirk.
*
“Buck”: Some ideas as to the superiority of
imported goods over Australian have been
sharply corrected by active service. The
first question we ask now is, “Is it Aussie?”
British factories have done their best to pro-
vide us with an Australian-pattem hat
with truly awful results in coloi', shape
and weight, and fortunate “Diggers” in
possession of the oldest Aussie Saieeda tend
it carefully, cleaning, rebraidiug and even
painfully ironing it, rather than accept the
present issue. As for boots, stand and
watch the stream of men towards the boot-
maker’s tent! A stock of Australian-made
has just arrived. They are handsome, com-
fortable and honest. Diggers who have
avoided boot parades for weeks, fearing an
issue of British ammunition boots, now
crowd up, hopeful of getting a pair that
will be kind to their feet. There is an Aus-
tralian polish, too, that does up leather
like nothing else we can discover. The
blankets brought from home are far better
than the issue here, and the lucky soldier
who has retained or has acquired an
“Aussie” travels light and sleeps warm. This
appreciation of the home-made article,
which extends to breeches, puttees and under-
clothing. is by no means sentimental, but
the outcome of experience. Now about
food. An experimental issue of Queensland
beef was a great success, but we have not
had any since—just the same old salt-horse
from the U.S.A. and the Argentine, and that
does not approach the Bananaland “bully”
for flavor, quality and method of packing.
It is hard to realise the comparative excel-
lence of our Australian tomato-sauce until
one has tried all the best English and
American brands. One division imported
stocks of a good brand of Australian sauce
for its canteens, and although twice and,
in some cases, three times the price of
English makes, it is the only line sold while
it lasts. Finally, the man who could export
Australian beer to the troops would, rapidly
acquire a fortune.
“Croson”: Melbourne can’t yet see the
obvious remedy for its oongestion-of-traffic
problem. The chief reasons for that conges-
tion are its habit of importing millions of
pounds’ worth of articles that it could pro-
duce itself, and its other habit of scattering
factories haphazard in the residential sub-
urbs, so that all day long the main arteries
are packed with long processions of lorries
and drays, carting goods to and fro, to the
increased cost of the articles and the annoy-
ance of everybody. Yet Melbourne has, in
Fisherman’s Rend, a huge area lying idle,
an ideal site for docks, wharves, factories,
timber-yards and coal dumps. With a bridge
across the lower Yarra hardly a ton of goods
need be carted through the city—the rail-
ways could take all to the suburbs required
—and factory and wharf workers could be
taken thither by electric trams running to
and from the opposite side of the city—no
residences being allowed on this area.

Who slew Edward Wales with a stab in the
back.
When the scene of the seance was eerily
black,
And never a cause for the fatal attack
Was stated or heard?
On whom did suspicion of guiltiness rest?
And who, when appalled by the Medium’s
test,
Fell under the table and weakly confess’t?

“The Thirteenth Chair,” shifted to the
Royal (Melb.) last Saturday night, has the
attraction of a good murder. Says one of
the village gossips in “Silver King,” talking
of newspaper entertainment, “Now, my Avifc
is all for divorce and the little titbits; but
give me a Good Murder —one that ain’t
found out for a long time.” To which one
of his cronies adds; “And where you ain’t
sure whether you’re got the right man till
after he’s hanged.” The knifing of the
occupant of the unlucky chair doesn’t result
in the hanging of anybody during the action
of the play, but the audience is kept in
the dark as to avlio did it, and the search
for the truth is the only thing that matters.
Therefore all persons present at each per-
formance, including persons Avho report these
theatrical cases for the press, are desired to
refrain from giving away the identity of the
person (male or female) who stabbed
Edward Wales between the shoulders whilst
sitting in a spiritualistic stance circle at
the home of a harmless American capitalist.
The present writer grants himself the liberty
of saying that it wasn’t the host of the
seance who did the deed. The rest is
silence. The mouth of criticism is gagged
as regards the probability of the person
who committed the murder being afraid that
a cunning old fraud of a “medium” could
reveal the name of a person avlio had com-
mitted a previous murder of the same sort.
Nor would threats of the rack and the
thumbscrew scare this critic into discussing
the probability of a murderous person
(male or female) being present at the
seance aforesaid with a large, murderous
knife concealed about his or her person. In
the light of truth Bayard Veiller’s play
might seem founded on unreasonableness;
but the light of truth is turned off at the
meter by request of the management. It
Avas done in the case of “Grumpy.” and this
is at least a more ingenious mystery-play
than “Grumpy.” Also, the old woman pre-
sented by Margaret Wyeherly is more
“The Thirteenth
Chair,” at Melbourne
Royal.

Infatuated believers in spiritualism get some
nasty jars from “The Thirteenth Chair.” The
nastiest is when the old professional humbug
is herself deceived into thinking she has
summoned two knocks from the spirit world,
whereas it was merely the butler knocking
for admittance. But the wily author of the
play makes a concession to credulous spook-
hunters. He depicts Rosalie as a fraud-in-
ordinary, x and an unscrupulous, conscience-
less conspirator at that, who yet is vaguely
connected with supernaturalism. She is the
same fraud as all the other mediums —said
to be quite genuine until somebody finds
them out. Although her supposedly psychic
powers are useless at a crisis, except for
playing on the improbable terrors of a cold-
blooded assassin, they are supposed to give
her a mild and dreamy nature, as though an
old woman who lives by fraudulent con-
spiracy and vigilant cunning could really
afford to have a
mild and dreamy
disposition. Miss
Wycherly’s make-
up . might have
been studied from
a picture by Pet-
tie, P».A. “An
Arrest for Witch-
craft.” The poor
old creature in the
picture is not much
older than Rosalie
in the play, who is
understood to be
“a wonderful
woman,” on the
word of Lizette
Parkes. Why won-
derful ? Sha w
plays an ultra-
melodramatic de-

the fact that he
can dominate the

and spring one or
two surprises on
the company. The

The 11th week of “Katinka,” at Her
Majesty’s (Melb.), was marked by the
return of Florence Young to duty in the
glad rags of Mrs. Hopper. The convalescent
was in good heart and voice, but she didn’t
make her old part seem quite the same as
when Phil Smith used to present her to a
harem-keeper by mistake. Smith continues
to be sadly missed from the clothes of Thad-
deus T. Hopper. They fitted him neatly,
whereas they look like a slop-suit on Welch.
Fortunately the chorus defies influenza and
shows no signs of war weariness.
To-morrow (Friday) is the last night of
Emelie Polini as the siren book-canvasser.
Annie is about to close her long innings at
the King’s (Melb.), and the unconventional
leading-lady will appear in “The Invisible
Foe’’ on Saturday.
** * *
Belgian pianist de Bourguignon found a
vacancy on the Chappell concert programme
at the Auditorium (Melb.) on Saturday.
The newcomer is a tall and athletic-looking
youth, who apparently isn't sufficiently
developed to venture beyond an established
interpretation. In his bits of Tscliaikowsky,
Rubinstein and Saint Saens he was more
in the nature of a human pianola than an
artist coloring music with his own personal-
ity. Guido Cacialli buttered several songs
with the sentiment of a warm and winning
nature, and was almost blown from the
platform by the gale of applause. The
trouble with Cacialli, however, was that
he excited odious comparison with a couple
of amateurs in operatic singing, Joseph
Mummery and Ivor Boustead. The pair
did their ambitious best from “Faust”
and “The Barber of Seville,” but
their earnest and expressive tootling was
passed down by the veteran’s robust art
into the kindergarten class. The only lady
soloist, Constance Buchan, showed evidence
of an artistic temperament in “The Young
Nun” of Shubert and Arne’s “Lass with the
Delicate Air,” whilst she had the help of a
sweet-voiced quartette in Liza Lehmann’s
“Sherwood Forest” and the dainty “Trip-
ping Hither, Tripping Thither,” from
“lolanthe.”
At the Auditorium (Melb.) another Chap-
pell ballad concert will happen next Satur-
day, and then the season will be interrupted.
The Taits announce Leila Doubleday for
August 24. Miss Doubleday, a child when
she last sounded her violin in Melbourne, is
a notable artist these times, and the local
musical world is expected to make her glad
to be back in her native country.
The advt. of “Time, Please,” at the Tivoli
(.Melb.), foreshadows the approach of ,'■closing
time. The revue is in its last-nights stage.
A large and comfortable female, who, looks
like an abdicated queen of palmistry, .-'is pro-
fessing to read people’s thoughts at the
Bijou (Melb.). The lady rejoices in the
name of Sa-IJera, and under the voluble
direction of a male confederate sits blind-
folded near the O.P. wing mentionirug num-
bers and names written on the blackboard
behind her, whilst subsequently enlightening
the public generally about tilings 'in the
minds of various spectators. T’ne whole
business is painfully wearisome*, and is
less convincing evidence of telepathy
than the demonstrations by this Howard
Brothers some years ago. TJae dame’s
peeps into sundry brain-boxes are par-
ticularly unexciting, and merely tend to
reveal the weird unanimity *of thought
among any dozen casually selected citizens.
A party of new artists throng the rest of
the bill, but none of them cauise the same
commotion as Fred Bluett. Thl sis his first
appearance at the Bijou.
The stars that pass in the nig ht at Hoyt’s
(Melb.) this week are Williram Farnum,
SessueHayakawa and Wallaceßeid. Farnum,
who was lately featured in the Fox version
of “Les Miserables,” is the centre of gravity
in “The Heart of a Lion,” fn-eely adapted
from Ralph Connor’s novel, “*The Doctor.”
Hayakawa represents the piebald lead of
“Hidden Pearls.” a five-act Hawaiian story
highly embellished by the hero’s conscience
and some brilliant local scenery. For Wal-
lace Reid as “Rimrock Jonies” there are five
reels relating to the ownership of a copper-
mine. Among the lesser lights, Tom Mix,
as “Six-shooter Andy,” turns out to be a
cowboy who conceals Baptist principles
beneath a wealth, of artillery.
The Majestic (Melb.) is fomenting popu-
lar prejudice against the K tiiser with a long
riot called “The Beast of: Berlin.” “The
Wolf-hound of Potsdam,” As the advt. also
describes him, is fearsomehe depicted by ex-
Australian Rupert Julian, who doesn’t err
in revealing Wilhelm’s awful character. A
Triangle five-acter, entitled “The Hand at
the Window,” follows the “degenerate viper”
of Germany, and unwinds a seething detec-
tive mystery that gives the C.I.D. brain-
fever before it cools down. A. sweet Triangle
heroine, Margery Wilson, makes the leading
bachelor uneasy in this spectacular yarn.
John Cosgrove should be admiring him-
self in Adelaide. He has put up a fine
series of performances at the Tivoli, with
Anderson’s Co., as Rasputin, in the Rus-
sian melodrama; a kindly cleric in * “The
Woman Thou Gavest Me”; and lastly
as Dennis O’Hara in the Australian racing
play “Coo-ee,” written by himself. The
Royal finished a too-brief season (a week)
of “The Thirteenth Chair,” with its daring
plan of going back to the old “unities” of
tune and space, and showing that the action
of a drama can be represented, on one spot,
in just the time taken to play it. One ought
to see the “Chair” twice—once for the sur-
prises, and again to conceive the clever act-
ing. At the Majestic, the usual revue is
rather overshadowed by Abd.v’s kangaroo
and the Jazz Band, both of which have an
element of novelty. At the Royal is the
“Lest We Forget” picture, which shows some
of the horrors of war. At Wondergrapli,
Mary Pickford charms. West’s announce
proudly that they got “Panthea” past
the S.A. censors only by accepting “certain
restrictions as to advertising.” Alex. Watson
has made hosts of new friends at the Town
Hall. He is strong in every department, but
the writer would like to suggest that he
should make his characters’ laughter more
moderate.
The Palace (Sydney) is fighting for
breathing room with “The Nigger.” Guy
Bates Post is due to leave for America on
Wednesday. August 28, and four perform-
ances of “The Masquerader” are to be
crowded into Monday and Tuesday (August
26 and 27). Till then it’s “The Nigger”
twice a day.

“Oh. Boy!” is ending its hectic existence
at Her Majesty’s (Sydney). It lived gaily
and tunefully, and will be gratefully remem-
bered by all but confirmed wowsers.
“Business Before Pleasure” continues to
keep the wolf far from the door at Sydney
Cri. and the clerk at the box-office busy find-
ing a place for the citizen who merely wants
somewhere to sit. Matinees are now on
the menu.

last out of the dish.

Mrs. Vernon Castle is. as usual, exuber-
antly well-dressed as Marion Sterling on
the Strand (Sydney) screen. On this occa-
sion she has as side-lines a murdered father,
a family lawyer and an innocent lover sus-
pected of a double crime. It’s a large order,
and in “The Hillcrest Mystery” it is ex-
plained to be really the work of German
spies. The Crystal Palace unblushingly
reveals Constance Talmadge in what is ad-
vertised as a “nifty nightie,” taking a Hying
leap into bed—the wrong bed —not the one
that holds Aunt Martha, but the couch that
encloses a horrid man, who has been storm-
bound like Sallie and auntie in a house,
the owners of which for story purposes are
absent. There are live days full of compli-
cations, ravelled and unravelled to the joy-
ousness of the onlooker. “Up the Road with
Sallie” holds a laugh for everybody. “The
Brand of Cowardice,” at the Globe, is the
story of a carefully-brought-up young
American who thought himself too exalted
to fight in one of the Mexican flare-ups. till
the best girl hands him back the ring. Then
lie fights and in such a way that we under-
stand why the Hun steps back rather than
tread on the corns of Uncle Sam’s advanc-
ing army. The Lyceum and the Ilaymarket
feature Sessue Hayakawa, with mystery
and romance amongst the South Sea Islands
for a background. It is the storv of n quest
for pearls to satisfy the desire of a girl.
Ilawkesbury” : There is much wild reason-
ing about the cursed drink that might be well
ballasted by a few facts rarely mentioned, but
well known by experts :
Alcohol is the excrement of the yeast plant, and,
as with tuberculosis, man never acquires immunity
by habit, but by evolving a race less susceptible to
it. Every aboriginal race so far as it has had the
chance destroyed itself with alcohol. It is an in-
herent craving of primitive man. Every temperate
people were once drunken, as shown by their ancient
writings. Diet, climate, race, or structure do not
appear to be factors. The black in tropical Australia,
or the Esquimo near the Pole are both eager to
drink themselves to death. Negroes on'the African
Coast, long accustomed to palm wine, are not victims
to "in like those of the interior. As regarus alconui,
Asiatics as a body are temperate because long ago
they evolved against it. The more modern European
is in the hall-way stage. This is illustrated bv
opium. In its home, India, the natives long ago
evolved against it; but China, taking to it in modern
times, is still a victim. The higher apes and the
negro have evolved against malaria, but not against
tuberculosis; the contrary is the case with the white
These _ are some facts given by Dr. G. H.
Reid in “Heredity”; but for his interpreta-
tion evolved against” may be substituted
acquired the habit of.” This is a matter of
opinion ; but whichever view is taken, man
2I ? himself or ancestors seems doomed to an
alcoholic jag: and it is curious to note that,
while the jamboree of the race was on, or
shortly after, it shows its most vigorous
mental development. There is evidence that
Australians are rapidly losing the drink
crave, although you wouldn't think it judg-
ing from their fiscal policy. Incidentals,
years ago, Windsor (N.S.W.) had 36 busy
pubs; now it has six far from busy, and that
with an immensely increased population.
The Sydney police took no chances last
week when they went down to Woolloomoo-
loo to collect Walter Sutherland, the speedy
person who jumped handcuffed from a mov-
ing train at Strathfield and got clean away
from the warder who was talcing him to
Bathurst Gaol. A file of John Hops in
their best official togs proceeded to the
piemises, around which plain-clotlies men
were ringed. Two uniformed men went in
and introduced themselves to Sutherland
(who has 10 years to work off). He made a
dash for the window ; but the duet of advance
guards assured him he would be only wast-
ing energy and raising unnecessary dust. A
watching carter who saw the escapee in
pyjamas and bracelets bundled into a motor
that the 25 Hops had requisitioned, remarked
disgustedly, “Well, he might ’ave made a
fight for it!” There’s no satisfying some
people.

This co.’s business is not expanding very
rapidly, as the following sales figures show:
1912. 1916. 1917. 1918.
Wool (bales).. 101,295 119,661 108,370 119,685
Cattle 15,368 11,699 12,191 12,993
Sheep . . . . 307,736 206,926 172,795 176,390
Meantime, owing to the high prices ruling
the total turnover has increased from
£2,038,025 in 1912 to £3,263,995 in 1917, and
to £3,605,370 last year. And, of course, that
has meant increased commission earnings.
But what extra has been earned in this way
has nearly all been swallowed in increased
expenses, particularly in wages and rents.
Owing to the scarcity of shipping, stocks
have been piling up, and the co. has had to
look round for storage accommodation. How-
ever, the net profit for the past year is the
best the co. has shown since the present co.
was formed in 1910 “to give woolgrowers
and stock-owners an opportunity to acquire
an interest” in the old-established business of
Winchcombe, Carson and Co. That move
brought £lOO,OOO more capital into the show.
For goodwill £41,083 was paid, though about
£lO,OOO of reserves were brought over from
the old co. Up till now the good-
will item has been retained at the original
figure as an asset in the balance-sheet. Now,
however, the directors propose to do the
proper thing and write off £5983, reducing
the item to £36,000. Winchcombe, Car-
son’s is an instance of a co. in
which, at the reconstruction, the personal
factor was an important one. Here were
men who had spent years building up a fine
business, and whatever goodwill was worth
was due to their energies. Since the present
co. began, death has robbed the Board of all
the old hands with the exception of Duncan
Carson. As things have turned out the co. is
doing even better than it used to. But if
the old directors had been succeeded by men
less capable than the present team, the re-
sults might have been quite different; and
if things had gone badly the shareholders
would have found that their goodwill asset
was not worth much. Everything has
turned all right in this case ; but the direc-
tors are doing a wise tiling by writing down
the goodwill item.
The balance-sheet figures have expanded
considerably in eight years:—
1910. 1917. 1918.
Liabilities .. .. £78,348 £274,728 £261,907
Freeholds . . . . 85,483 158,415 159,215
Advances, etc. .. 118,372 308,344 292,203
Investments.. .. 9,252 21,646 23,696
Consignments .. 7,887 - 1,151
Plant, etc 4,935 14,452 23,506
The advances are a solid item, and are made
up of “advances against wool and produce,
stock, mortgages, liens and sundry debtors.”
Capital is £205,755, all in fully-paid £1
shares. So to finance its advances the co.
is leaning on the bank to the extent of
£89,929. while £171,978 is owing on current
accounts and due to sundry creditors.
The big increase in the freeholds item is ex-
plained mostly by the extension of the business
to Brisbane shortly after the present co. was
formed. Since 1910 £39.993 of new capital
has been got in; and liabilities have in-
creased by £183,559. Meanwhile £273,844
has been tacked on to assets. So if the assets
are worth every penny shown in the books,
Winchcombe, Carson, Ltd., has improved its
position by over £50,000 in the eight years
it has been in business. Meantime it has
been paying 6 and 7 p.c. divs. with ease, the
average net profits for the past three years
being equivalent to over 11 p.c. on the co.’s
capital, though on the total of shareholders’
funds in the business it is slightly under
9 p.c.

Take another view of the Victoria’s figures
before and since the war: —
1914. 1916. 1917. 1918.
Gross profits* £119,245 £106,935 £109,004 £117,693
Management exes. 54,67 S 54,658 55,861 56,617
♦Less taxes and provision for contingencies.
So gross profits are now within £l5OO of
what they were before the war, and for the
first time since Wilhelm upset the world
disclosed net profits have exceeded the
£60,000 mark. Arid despite the disturbance
of overseas exchange business and added tax-
ation, there seems to be little reason why
profits should be leaner than they were
prior to 1914. Certainly the management
has been proceeding cautiously in the matter
of advances, “discountenancing any -business
of a speculative nature, but fully aiding all
legitimate producing, manufacturing and
other sound business enterprises.” Never-
theless, including the £475,730 advanced
under the Government wheat scheme,
advances are now greater than they were
in 1914. Further, the holding of public
securities has increased from £250,240 in
1914 to £573,833 as at June 30 last. Calcu-
lated at, say, 4 p.c. p.a., the return on this
increase would add about £6400 to each
half’s profits. At any time a hank’s dis-
closed profits are really nothing more than
an estimate, for it never knows, for certain
what lies ahead and what losses it may have
to meet. Probably, because of the uncertain-
ties of the future, the Bank of Victoria man-
agement has not been so much concerned
about the profit it discloses, but has preferred
to provide liberally for contingencies.
The Victoria has also been strengthening
its position by piling up liquid assets, which
have been increased considerably by realising
on assets that were not showing a satisfac-
tory return, and investing the money in more
profitable lines. This shows, how the balance-
sheet figures have moved since 1914: —
Liabilities.
June, ’l4. June, ’l7. June, ’lB.

The directors don’t show the figures ,in the
same way : the £475,730 advanced under the
Government wheat scheme is included with
the liquid assets, and not among the advances
as in the table. Taking the figures as shown
in the table, liabilities now total £8,066,541,
of which £3,119,860, or 38.6 p.c. is held
liquid. Including the wheat advances as
liquid assets the proportion is 44 p.c. This
compares with between 30 and 35 p.c. before
the war, and as low as 27 p.c. as at Decem-
ber, 1915. Paid up capital is £1,478,010, and
reserves £477,544; total, £1,955,554. So fob
every £1 of its own funds the bank holds
slightly over £4 of the public’s money. Thus
from the depositors’ point of view the posi-
tion is very snug. But it means a compara-
tively small return to shareholders, the pre-
sent 6 p.c. dividend being equal to a little
over 4\ p.c. p.a. on capital and reserves.

1911, paying £2OOO for goodwill. But the
old plant was unsatisfactory, and the first
year of the new co. was chiefly devoted to
getting new machinery together and making
arrangements for handling and distributing
the output. Further, there was a slump in
the value of seed, and the co. lost heavily
on its stocks. The following year’s opera-
tions also showed a loss, but the co. was able
to demonstrate during the 12 months that its
new plant could turn out linseed oil of a
quality equal to any imported. Reviewing
the position at this stage, the directors
decided that half the capital (£14,938)
had been lost, and they therefore wrote down
assets to that extent. The third year showed
the first profit, but towards the end of the
year the whole of the works and stocks were
destroyed by fire. Besides having protected
its assets by insurance, the co. had also in-
sured its profits and standing charges.
Within three months new works had
been established and in a little while the
plant was in a position to increase the
former production. Next year the co. did
better and showed a net profit of £12,730,
hut, as the directors pointed out, that “did
not by any means cover the loss which was
incurred in establishing the business.’’ How-
ever, shareholders got their first dividend,
and the goddwill item of £2OOO was written
off.
At this time the prospects of the industry
in Australia were looking very bright. The
scarcity of shipping was helping the market
for the local product, which had already
proved its quality. The directors seized
the opportunity and approached the Federal
Treasurer on the question of raising capital
to extend the plant to supply the whole of
the Commonwealth’s requirements. The
proposal was agreed to, on condition that
£20,000 of new second pref. capital was sub-
scribed. Under the arrangement the writ-
ten-off capital was restored, and bonus
shares for a similar amount were also issued.
The figures in the table show what has hap-
pened since. Last year the co. had a gorgeous
time, even though it sold its oil at 2s. per
gallon under the proclaimed price, and the
year’s operations showed a net profit of
£67,627 on a paid capital of £84,814. The
War-time Profits Tax, however, has yet to
take its cut. After establishing a reserve
to cover goodwill (£14,938) and paying
dividends, reserves and the balance of the
profit totalled £51.962. Of this the meet-
ing recently decided to distribute £29,876 in
bonus shares, two new bonus shares being
tacked on to every three of the old ordinaries.
Capital as at May 31 was £84,814, made up of
89.628 ords. of 10s. each, 20,000 £1 first prefs.
and 20,000 £1 second prefs., both fully paid.
Liabilities total £87,333., Freeholds and build-
ings stand in the books at £28,110, plant and
machinery at £27.254, while a depreciation
reserve of-£3237 has been accumulated. The
co. was carrying stocks worth £69,764,
sundry debtors owed £47.861, and there was
£75.160 in cash and War Loan; but as
stated previously the War-time Profits Tax
has yet to be collected. Though Meggitt's
has been having a wonderful time, there
have been formidable difficulties. While the
shortage of shipping has helped the co., it
has also interfered with the importing of
seed, and the works were temporarily shut
down a while ago. Later on, no doubt, it
will be found that linseed can be grown
quite as profitably in Australia as it is in
India, Canada, Argentine and Russia. As
it is, the industry has given employment to
about 8000 Australians.

The net profits for the past half-year show a
splendid recovery on the previous term’s
trading. Catering is an erratic business: the
adjustment of prices to meet continually
varying costs being a ticklish problem. And
a scattered business like Sargent’s, with
27 cafes and tea-rooms in Sydney and half a
dozen shops in Melbourne, requires a particu-
larly keen and watchful management. In this
respect the, co. has been exceptionally fortu-
nate, for it has succeeded where many others
have gone under. The measure of its suc-
cess is shown in the string of uninterrupted
10 p.c. dividends handed out and the hand-
some additions to reserves after paying the
divs. Another view of the progress Sar-
gent’s has made is given in this comparison
of the balance-sheet figures:—
June,’lo. June.’lS.
Capital £90,000 £110,212
All liabilities . . .. 9,395 40,303
Freeholds.. .. .. 10,800 70,000
Leaseholds .. .. 14,550 18,300
Furniture, plant, etc. .. 20,035 53.870
Stock 3,166 19,616
Cash and book debts .. 12,126 17,464
So since 1910 £20,242 of new capital lias
been got in, and liabilities have increased by
£31,448. Meantime £59,200 has been added
to freeholds, £3750 to leaseholds, £26,935 to
plant and £21,788 to stock and cash. Thus,
after paying a regular 10 p.c. dividend, in
eight years the co. has strengthened its posi-
tion by almost £60,000. The goodwill item of
£45,000 has now been written right out of
the balance-sheet, a similar amount of accu-
mulated reserves also disappearing. As pre-
viously mentioned, good profits are dependent
entirely on good management; and so long
as the shareholders have men as capable as
those at present in charge, everything should
be all right. A very big trade has been
built up, the gross profits on manufacturing
and trading ha vine expanded from £35,589
eight years ago to £83,249 as at June last.

Commonwealth Steel Pkoducts Co.,
Ltd.- —This co. represents an attempt to
establish the liigh-class steel manufacturing
industry on a large scale in Australia. The
co. was the outcome of the efforts of Messrs.
A. Goninan and A. M. Henderson, two men
whose experience and ability got the Clyde
Engineering Co., Ritchie Bros., the Pioneer
Spring Co., Howard Smith’s and other users
of steel interested when early in 1917 they
discussed the possibilities of establishing the
industry. Fifteen months ago the co. was
provisionally formed and Messrs. Goninan
and Henderson were sent abroad to investi-
gate the most modern methods and make
arrangements with regard to plant. In
March this year the co. was registered with
a nominal capital of a quarter of a million
(250,000 shares of £1 each) —the Federal
Treasury granting permission for the issue
of 150,000 shares paid to 10s. —and the New-
castle Brick and Tile Co.’s property of 47
acres, at Waratah. near Newcastle, was
acquired for £13,650. For its manager, the
co. appointed A. M. Henderson, who de-
signed, built and operated Ruwolt’s, where
he produced manganese steel for the first
time in Australia, and who also installed the
first modern steel-casting plant in the Com-
monwealth at the Steel Co. of Australia’s
works in Melbourne.
Since March much progress has been
made. The first difficulty was to secure
plant, but the manager has a profound be-
lief in the capabilities of the local engineer-
ing firms, and, except for portion of the elec-
trical equipment, the designs for which are
not available locally, the whole of the plant,
including steam hammers, electric furnace,
the tyre-rolling mill and machine tools, has
been manufactured locally. The co. expects
to produce railway and tramway tyres from
purchased steel early next year, and to be
turning out its own steel within nine months,
the latter depending upon the arrival of
electrical equipment and the supply of suffi-
cient power from the Newcastle Council.
The co. is setting out to produce only
the highest quality steel, and is installing
the most modern electric furnace, similar
to those used in Britain and America for
turning out gun steel. Practically the only
doubt about the future is the protection
which the politicians will give the industry
after the war. In 1913 the Commonwealth
imported wheels and tyres valued at
£239,000, on which the general duty was
5 p.c. and the preferential duty nil. Of the
total, £129,000 worth came from Germany
(most of it from Krupps), £96,000 worth
from the U.K., and £14,000 from Belgium.
In the majority report of the Inter-State
Commission, which conducted a tariff investi-
gation in 1916, this appears:—
If Australian blast furnaces had been able to
provide the grade of pig-iron for the manufacture of
steel for cast-wheel centres, and the manufacture of
tyres could be also undertaken, we would have un-
hesitatingly. .. .recommended only one duty, which
would have been sufficient to at once encourage their
local manufacture; but on account. .. .of having to
rely on imported tyres....arid on imported pig-iron
for wheel centres, we can only make such recom-
mendations as, in our opinion, will give encourage-
ment to the initiation of the manufacture of cast-
wheel centres and the completion of the imported
tyre in Australia.
The Commission under those circumstances
recommended a duty of 30 p.c. But the
politicians have, as usual, done nothing. And
since the Commission made its recommenda-
tion the steel industry has been established
in Australia, and this co. will also be turn-
ing out its own steel in about nine months.
Of course, the war is providing all the neces-
sary Protection just at present, but cos. like
this, which by establishing new industries
are doing something practical towards kill-
ing German post-war trade, are getting little
encouragement from the politicians who are
most talkative on the subject.

at S e if ie important provisions in
IV to. Wales Mines Minister Fitzpatrick’s
Amending Bill is that throwing open to
piospectors the private lands on which the
mineral rights, outside of gold, have been
reserved to the owner. Should the clause get
through the numerous areas of loc'ked-up
mining land in this State will become the
subject of applications for authority to enter
by processes similar to those already existing
m respect of unreserved lands. The only
new condition attached is the payment to the
owner of a sort of royalty in the form of a
percentage of the net profits. For years
prospectors have unsuccessfully barracked
for this reform. Another clause intended to
encourage the genuine prospector relates to
the right of entry to leases over which the
existing leaseholders have little or no surface
rights. In the past many cos. have held up
developmental work by hanging on to big
areas carrying underground rights with only
a . small piece of the whole having surface
lights. Some time ago the matter was argued
m the Supreme Court, which held that the
original holders in any form were the only
persons competent to enter the land. This
rilling will be definitely upset if the present
Bill succeeds.
* * * *
T. J. Greenway, who in the old days ran
the Broken Hill South, Block 14 and Chilla-
goe, has taken on a bigger job than any of
those shows. This is to shake up things
generally in respect to oil problems in Aus-
tralia, and incidentally to divert the local
oil-flow to the Anglo-Persian Co. This is a
British semi-State affair, in which Britain
holds a two-thirds interest. Strathcona and
Breen way’s brother Charles are the British
Government nominees on the Board, the lat-
ter being also general manager. The lure
to the Federal Cabinet to join hands with
the Anglo-Persian Co. is that the Common-
wealth can become a partner, share in
Britain’s oil-fleet, and get the benefit of the
co. s organisation and its business connec-
tmn. Last year the Anglo-Persian netted
out of a restricted oil output £400.000, and
it has £4,000,000 of capital to plav with.
Other big oil people are also offering a willing
hand to the Government, but Greenway’s big
card is that they aren’t in partnership with
Britain, and that because the British Govern-
ment is taking our wheat, wool, meat, copper,
tin, lead and zinc, Greenway can claim it to
be a fair thing to extend the partnership to oil.
Anyhow, if outsiders are to be allowed into
lapua or any other Australian oil-fields,
the Federal Government must have the final
word in the control of the show. The techni-
cal and business may be left to the
shareholders to organise and develop, but the
dominating interest must be in the hands of
the State.
* * * *
.n. * ng Suffering” : What is this yarn about
the big metal octopus putting out another
tentacle to get hold of the tin producers?
Copper, lead, zinc and the rare metals,
scheehte, wolfram and molybdenite, all
have been yarded up very nicely. Now
comes_ the whisper that someone holds that
Britain is paying Australia too much for the
white metal it ships across the seas. If
the deduction is that the same sort of con-
trol should be exercised which allows private
people to get Australian wolfram and
molybdenite at about half the price Canadian
producers obtain from the U.S. for those
products, then there is going to be a terrific
yell from tin-mining community. If
the price of tin is to be reduced producers
will want to know why pig-iron, steel, zinc
shavings for gold-mines, steel plates for tin-
dredging shows, coal and wood for use under
the boilers, the wages of the workers, and all
the other oddments should not have their
price reduced to a decent level. If the idea
is to get tinplates for the canneries, will the
price of the inside steel plates be cut? No
one has ever sought to jolt Broken Hill cos.
out of their fat profits when zinc, lead and sil-
ver sky-rocketed, nor is there any move to cut
copper down to about £BO, or iron and steel
to anything approximating pre-war prices.
Anyway, if the Australian tin-producer is to
be hit, how will Australian cos. that mine
tin in Malaya be brought within the scope
of any control?
* * * *
The activity of Government Geologist
Harper, and the granting of a Government
subsidy of £lOOO to go on with, gives hope that
the old Hill End goldfield (N.S.W.) may look
up again. The recent company re-organisa-
tion marks the opening of a scheme for the
unified development of the field. The main
proposal is the driving of a tunnel from the
southern to the northern end of the leases,
starting at about 700 feet and deepening
with distance, as the surface runs up a
hill towards the north. The drive will go
through the old Amalgamated leases, the
Central, and the Reward, passing under the
famous Robert Emmett cross-course. This
is for the purpose of unwatering the lower
levels, which are fully virgin, but which
have not been accessible before owing to the
heavy make of water. The greatest depth
sunk has been 525 ft. in the Patriarch shaft,
under which the tunnel will pass a further
couple of hundred feet down. This will
allow of a connection with the shaft for the
hauling of the stone broken down en route
a hundred feet or so up the Patriarch to the
old Consolidated tunnel, along which it can
have a smooth passage. For the present,
however, the new Deep Levels Co., in co-
operation with the earlier shows, will pro-
ceed with the drive. The Reward continues
to get nice-looking stone from a newly-cut
reef met with in a crosscut, and is busy
improving the plant to deal with the big
body of low-grade ore in sight in the inter-
mediate levels.
Carpathia tin, Ardlethan (N.S.W.), per-
haps “never looked better,” but heaps more
information is wanted by a share-market
that has climbed to 28s. per share. Very
few mines overlook informing the public
how much ore has been hauled in a
week, and there certainly should be a
fair quantity raised from' faces 6ft. wide.
The lengthening of the slope from the 121 ft.
winze is a favorable development, but while
developments underground have been unusu-
ally good, the quantity of tin ore hauled
week by week appears to have been small.
Quite recently 13 tons of special milling ore
were despatched to the mill in a week, and
3 tons scwt. of concentrates were recovered.

An additional £523,000 was dug out of
the Great Boulder (W.A.) last year, and
the usual 3s. per share was paid in divi-
dends. The average grade of ore handled
was a little over 134dwt., and from this,
with costs at 28s. Bd. per ton, a net profit of
£253.000 was earned. The mill encroached
on the reserves to the extent of over 50,000
tons during the period, but when the year
ended there was still 387,000 tons of an esti-
mated gross value of £1,175,000 in sight
Manager Hamilton hopes to treat a larger
tonnage during the current 12 months, but
while values are not likely to show much
variation, costs are expected to be higher.

What are the Princess Royal Co.’s inten-
tions in regard to the Happy Jack at Comet
ValeV After allowing the water to rise the
co. recently decided to drain the workings
and do some sampling in order to determine
whether the show was worth bothering
about. A start was made to put the plant
in order, and then word came to do nothing.
An inquiry into the circumstances surround-
ing the acquisition of this property is being
urged by local shareholders.
* * * #
Bemoaning the lot of the Westralian mine-
owner at the Golden Horseshoe meeting,
chairman Purcell alleged that the shortening
of the week’s work from 48 to 44 hours and
other concessions granted to the miners by
Justice Powers was making it very hard for
the mining industry to live. Experience at
Broken Hill had shown that the 44-hour
week reduced tonnage by about 12 to 15 per
cent. Such a reduction of output in the
West would, he reckoned, almost paralyse
gold-mining. Turning to recent mine de-
velopments he said the No. 4, or Boundary,
lode was showing up well, and in the bottom
level a run of at least 400 ft. of payable ore
could safely be relied on.
* * * *
Many Perth and Adelaide holders of Com-
modore (W.A.) scrip are bitterly complain-
ing about the recent boosting of the show
and _ the conditions which made such
boosting possible. The authorities in
the West have been urged over and
over aaain to frame a law making it
a criminal offence for anyone to circulate
misleading reports about wild-cats, but
nothing has been done. After all the talk
about the good values heading from the
Ingliston into Commodore ground and whis-
pers about good prospects in other parts of
the show, the last crushing of 115 tons
yielded only £96 worth of gold.
* * * *
The draw to take up the wad of forfeited
Yilgaim Consols (W.A.) scrip, was the offer
of mine-manager R. J. Salvado to accept the
bundle and pay any calls due. The co. is
again financial, so Salvado has gone back to
unwater the main shaft and to tackle the
Big and the parallel reefs at the Karridale
lease. However, if the yarn concerning a
6ft. reef worth 60s. per ton and a small
reef carrying 3oz. values over a length of
600 ft. was correct, why did Salvado leave
the mine to worry over any doubting Mel-
bourne crowd?

Riverina South (W.A.), having been
stuck with heavy calls, a debenture debt and
a recalcitrant gas-machine, gets the West-
ralian Government to back a p.n. to the tune
of £2OOO in order to get on the go once again.
With the new debt the co. will have to pay
off £BOOO before the shareholders can start
to get back the £60,000 the mine owes them.
* * * *
The Tyrconnel North Co. has left West-
ralia and gone to Trentham, in Victoria.
The co. made two attempts in the West, and
both failed. The first was at Laverton,
where its prospects were frequently declared
in Perth, Adelaide and Melbourne papers
as being either bright or brilliant, but the
show ultimately proved to be the very
reverse. The second was at Kalgoorlie,
where reported good values in one of the
trans-Australian railway cuttings vanished
in the course of development. The co. now
proposes to try the old Egerton-Sultan group.
Lots of gold came out of the Annie
Laurie and Grace Egerton reefs from the
stopes of these ancients, above a big tunnel.
This working was never continued to the
Sultan line and the Tyrconnel North is out
to hunt for it. If reefs are got, there will
be 300 ft. of backs. “If” is the big factor in
this new go, because the old miner who
worked the Sultan reef down to 900 ft.
wasn’t the sort of chap to let much gold go
out of sight under foot.
G. D. Meudell and J. L. Strevens have just
completed a tour of certain supposed oil areas
in Westralia, and claim that boring for
petroleum with modern oil-drilling machin-
ery is fully justified on some of them. All
that Meudell asks of the State Government
is the undertaking of a proper geological
survey to determine the best site for boring,
and when this is done he says he will do the
rest. But as the Government geologists say
that there is no oil in the State, the W.A.
Oil Syndicate will probably be invited to
bore where it likes.
From J. L. Strevens, an English oil-man,
who has lately had a look at Westralia: —
Modern petroleum technology is continually bring-
ing to light facts which indicate that oil is widely
distributed throughout nature, and in places where
geological evidence as to its presence is either not
available or distinctly unfavorable. It is rather
surprising, therefore, to read “Oof Bird’s” damaging
comments (B. 15/8/T8) in connection with the
Westralian oil discoveries, and which he has doubt-
less based upon statements made and published from
time to time by the W.A. Geological Survey. It is
ti'ue that some months ago one Walter A. English,
said to be acting for the Vacuum Oil Company, made
a cursory examination of certain areas in Westralia of
a supposedly oil-bearing nature and came to similar
conclusions on its unlikely possibilities as had been
arrived at previously by the State Geological Depart-
ment, after surveying the same districts. Unfortunately,
Mr. English’s statement, which condemned the whole of
the State from the few casual observations that he
made personally, is likely to have far-reaching
deterrent effects upon those having faith in the oil
possibilities of this continent. It is only fair to
state that at least two of the best-known mining
and geological experts of the Westralian Government
have expressed to the writer their possession of
perfectly open minds upon the question of the exist-
ence of petroleum in their own State. The W.A.
Oil Syndicate certainly found oil from the districts
of Busselton and Pingelly, and a bituminous material
from east of Esperance. So far as one of the
districts is concerned a recent investigation has
indicated that it is a likely spot to bore for oil with
the right kind of modern, drilling rig. The settle-
ment of the all-important question as to whether
oil deposits do or do not exist in Australia must
be left to the drill and the drillers, who are, after
all, the final arbiters in the controversy. The slight-
est positive evidence of the presence of petroleum in
the Commonwealth should be diligently followed up,
whatever the cost, as a vital national necessity.
Right on the Indian Ocean, at Yampie
Sound in the nor’-west of Westralia, is a
mountain of iron, which a Queensland syn-
dicate is in danger of losing. It has had
possession for several years, hut as appa-
rently nothing has been done to develop the
deposit, it has been jumped by some Perth
people.
One of the mainstays of Dyason’s Bendigo
Amalgamated scheme was the late George
Lansell’s Virginia, but the co. could do no
good with it, and so passed it on to the
tributors. They soon got on to payable
stone, and Opie’s party, at 1950 ft., led the
way by snicking 12Soz. from 28 tons, and
cleaning up an additional 230 z. from the
plates. From a little 50ft. block £3OOO
has been taken in six months. Next door,
in the Great Northern, another party, with
Betts as leader, got on to ounce stuff, and
now they are topping off with 4oz. crushings
won from a 100 ft. block. These yields draw the
co. back to the same formation, so that after
all this ore channel may turn Dyason’s luck.
His liberality to the tributor is not the
least reason why Bendigo smiles on him
nowadays.
* * * *
The output of electrolytic zinc from the
Risdon works (Tas.) has grown from 2501 b.
per day in March, 1917, to the present
22,4001 b. (or 10 tons) per day. Zinc assay-
ing 99.9 p.c. is exceptionally pure, and is
particularly valuable to munition-makers
and the electrical trade. Silver and lead
are recovered as by-products, and zinc works
treating, say, 100,000 tons of Broken Hill
concentrates a year would, in addition to
40,000 tons of zinc, turn out large quantities
of silver and lead products.
* * * *
The old Tyrconnel, Kingsborough (N.Q.),
crushed 825 tons in June and .July for
11490 z. of bullion, equivalent to 27dwt. per
ton. The money value of the crushing and
a statement of working costs would be in-
formative, for the bullion is' seldom worth
more than 60s. per oz. Numerous good
crushing's were reported last year, but wages
and other expenses took the lot. At present
the deepest level is at 265 ft., and stopes 6ft.
wide carrying payable values indicate that
this reef Jives down. So a contract has been
let to sink the shaft to 385 ft.
A deposit of phosphate of lime on Hol-
bourne Island, near tne Great Barrier Reei.
has given fair assays for phosphoric acid, and
the owners have now formed a subsidiary co.
to convert the raw material into superphos-
phate. The Australian Co-operative Fertil-
iser Co. has taken a half-interest in the
venture, and the erection of treatment works
on the Queensland coast is in contemplation.

WARNING TO THE WEAK AND
NERVOUS.
THE DANGERS OF DRUGGING.
It seems so easy to take a small dose of
some drug and thereby secure relief from
pain, forgetfulness of worries and cares, or
the sleep that has long been denied; but just
consider the awful consequences of this
drug-taking—think how the habit grows.
The small dose soon becomes larger and
larger, and has to be taken with increasing
frequency. Thus drug slaves are made. But
because drugs are harmful it does not mean
that sufferers from neurasthenia, insomnia,
debility, failing strength, loss of weight, etc.,
are to be denied comfort and relief. On the
contrary, it is quite likely such sufferers will
turn their attention to a product known as
bitro-pliosphate, and as a consequence much
good will be done, for bitro-pliosphate gives
better results than any of the harmful or
dangerous drugs, yet no re-action or ill-effect
will follow its use. Moreover, it is within
the easy reach of all, for the tablets are now
stocked by high-class chemists everywhere.
One tablet taken with meals three times
daily soon strengthens weak nerves, increases
vim, vigor and vitality, and ensures refresh-
ing, health-giving sleep at night.— Advt.

Titles get in the way sometimes. For one
exciting day recently the S. M. llerAld’s
law reports spoke of “Mr. Justice Sir
Edmund Barton.” Then it went back to the
old style. Perhaps Mr. Right Hon. Sir
Judge Griffith, who is a stickler for accuracy,
put in a remark.
“Swanstjon-street”: Gone on to the
Highest Court, at 62, Judge W. E. Johnston,
of Victoria. A large, kindly man with an
intellectual face. I first became aware of
him in the late ’nineties, before the motor
had arrived to rush the democratic push-
bike off the roads and the “scorcher” had
to depend on his own wind and leg-muscles.
In those days his Honor used frequently to
pedal into town, and if the weather was
warm he would have to shake the beads of
perspiration from his sparkling brow on to
the doormat before he wheeled into his fav-
orite bicycle shop and called for the manager
in a deep, legal voice. Like Madden, the
late C. J., Johnston collapsed and died while
ou a Sunday visit to a friend. If he exer-
cised as strenuously of late years as he did
when I knew him, perhaps his heart gave
out.
London Observer deals tenderly with
Hughes’s lack of inches in an article by
“An Australian Correspondent”:—
Though physically of no great size, though handi-
capped by severe deafness and recurring ill-health,
lie is invariably a fighter for his sincere convictions.
“No great size” is a delightful way of ex-
pressing the fact that William barely turns
the scale at 7st. 21b.
The oldest Anglican clergyman living in
Australia, Rev. Septimus Hungerford, cele-
brated his 93rd birthday on August 12. He
is the sole survivor of the State-paid clergy
of 1863, and he is the seventh out of eight
sons of Emanuel Hungerford, of Farley, near
YVest Maitland (N.S.W.), lieutenant of the
32nd Foot and afterwards captain, of the
South Cork Militia, who emigrated to the
State in 1828. The padre was ordained by
Bishop Tyrrell, of Newcastle, in 1853, and
worked at Armidale for 21 years; at Darling
Point in 1876; at Newtown 1877; at Holy
Trinity. Fort-street, 1878; at Ashfield, 1879 ;
and at Enfield, 1879-95. He is now in retire-
ment at Mosman.

Killed in France lately, Major Herbert
Musgrave, whose only connection with Aus-
tralia was that he was born Tiere. His
father, Sir Anthony Musgrave, was Governor
of S.A. in the eighteen-seventies, and two
sons were born in Adelaide. The one just
dead had a brilliant career. He got “men-
tions” in the Boer war as well as in the
world war (several times), and had won a
D.S.O. before going back to France for the
last time. He was 42.
Passed on from Portland John Huxley,
famous as one of the discoverers and owners
of the golden Londonderry pit (W.A.). Be-
fore joining the Western rush in the early
’nineties, J.H. was a wheelwright in Hamil-
ton (Vic.). After selling out in W.A. he
retired to Portland, and then purchased
Struan estate, near Casterton. This turned
out another Londonderry, and John’s pos-
sessions at his exit were well over six figures.
Bar a weakness for buying and holding fast
to Portland town allotments, Huxley had no
visible hobbies. He was a bachelor. Per-
haps that was his hobby.
Captain Brian Desmond Hughes, M.C.,
who was killed in action on August 6, was
one of four sons of the late John Hughes,
M.L.C., N. S. Wales, and a nephew of
Thomas Hughes, ex-Lord Mayor of Sydney.

“O. Blin” : The late Commonwealth archi-
vist Pethericlc was certainly jealous of his
collection, as “T. 0.1.” asserts (B. S/8/T8),
but he never objected to it being inspected.
Indeed, Petherick’s complaint was that he
could never arouse any interest in his
treasures, though he promised
a kindly welcome to everyone.
Practically buried in the base-
ment of Federal Parliament
House, the archivist almost
every day during his lifetime
heard busy faraway feet, but
with few exceptions the foot-
steps never came nearer. The
only man who visited him
regularly was Professor Ernest
Scott, then a Hansard re-
porter, and Scott’s calls were
not unconnected with the bor-
rowing of some scarce inforjna-
tion for one of his books. G.-G. Munro
Ferguson was, I believe, a disinterested
visitor who sought out the old bibliophile
in his cellar at various times.
“M.’s” send-off:—-
William Samuel Byrne. M.D., Bac. Surg., Mus.
Bac., news of whose death comes from Johannesburg,
was a very gifted man. As a surgeon he was in
the first flight of his profession, and he was a musician
of unusually high attainments. No one outside of
three or four topnotch professionals has ever inter-
preted Chopin as did William Byrne, under whose
clever fingers the spirit of the music was presented
with unfailing charm. He was a welcome guest
among London’s foremost orchestras (with whom he
has frequently appeared as soloist), and he positively
reigned over music circles in Brisbane for 10
years. He was president of the Liedertafel, presi*
dent of the Johnsonian Club (and what a host!),
and president of the Golf Club. Never was a fashion-
able practitioner pursued and captured by such a
host of poor patients. With him the fretful
rich were apt to be left waiting while he gave of
his best to an inmate of a hospital ward. His
science knew no class. Then one day he was sud-
denly gone—back to the fuller life for which he must
have continually longed—to America, Europe, South
Africa, and now the Unknown.

“Bad Australian” : Lieut. Can-
delaria has flown over the
Andes, away down south in the
Rio Negro territory. The Re-
public hasn’t gone wild over the
performance because, although
he has crossed what there is of
the range down in the Black
River country, his feat, com-
pared with that of flying across
the Uspallata Pass, where New-
berry, Macias and Zanni came
to grief, is like flying over the
Dividing Range where it peters
out on the-Western Plains and
crossing over from Gifipsland to
the Upper Murray. Candelaria
tackled an easy route both on
account of altitude and to be
sure of landing almost anywhere
en route if his engine went back
on him. The Uspallata Pass,
on account of the altitude
(13,000 ft.) and the absolute im-
possibility of making a success-
ful descent except at three
points in about 300 kilometers,
still remains for the man who
proposes to do something really
worth doing on wings. Zanni’s 140 kilo-
meters to Las Vacas, although it ended in a
smash, is an immeasurably greater achieve-
ment than Candelaria’s crossing.
One of the last of the Barcoo (Q.)
pioneers, Mrs. Donald Charles Cameron, died
lately, aged 99, In 1866 with her four
daughters and two young sons she was met
at Rockhampton by her husband and son
John, who had been members of a party
which started from New England (N.S.W.)
a few years earlier on a land-hunting expedi-
tion. Cameron’s mates were James and
William Crombie. James Crombie repre-
sented western constituencies for several
years in the Bananaland Parliament. Cam-
eron became a considerable cattle-station
owner, and of the Cameron sons John was
M.L.A. for the Mitchell and later North
Brisbane and a Q. N. Bank director. Donald
is now managing director of John Cameron
and Co., which owns Kensington Downs and
Caledonia stations. Mrs. Cameron left 84
direct descendants. Ten grandsons have put
on khaki.
Australian histories generally remark,
in a hazy sort of way, that Angus McMil-
lan “discovered” Gippsland; but McMillan
himself made no such claim. AVhat
he did claim, probably correctly, was
that he was the first to cross Gippsland
from the Monaro country to the sea at
Corner Inlet. At a dinner given to him at
Port Albert in 1856, he definitely stated
that other Monaro men had gone into Gipps-
land before him. When he arrived at Carra-
vong, in the Monaro country, in February,
1839, with instructions to look for
cattle country farther south, Bayliss had
already been down to Buchan (where the
caves are, not far from Orbost). Omeo was
already occupied when McMillan struck it
in 1840, and Walter Mitchell, a nephew of
MacFarlane, the pioneer of Omeo, had been
as far as where Bruthen now stands, near
the mouth of the Tambo. It was not till
May, 1841, that McMillan, starting from a
station which he had formed on the Avon,
reached Corner Inlet. He related that
for 16 months on end he never slept in a
bed nor had even a tent to shelter him.
THEY FOLLOWED HER, TO SCHOOL.
Mary had a little lamb—which was such an
unusual tinny during the meat shortage
that it blocked, the traffic.
Brisbane lost its oldest Anglican cleric a
few days ago when Canon Thomas Jones
died. He came out in the year 1860 as one
of a little bunch of curates introduced by
Bishop Tufnell. The young clergyman
served under Bishops Tufnell, Hale and
Webber (all now deceased), and was an
unfailing helper of Archbishop Donaldson,
the present supreme Anglican dignitary in
Bananaland. Canon Jones used to boast
that he had married hundreds of people
whom he had also christened, and that he
had baptised numbers of babies whose grand-
parents he had also sprinkled with christen-
ing water in the early days. He served for
a brief space in Hobart, but the rest of his
life in Australia was passed in the North,
mostly near Brisbane. In his 82nd year the
infernal climate put an end to the career of
a good and vigorous shepherd.
“F.” : William Andrews, who died last week,
was a solicitor of 43 years’ standing, a Sydney
native and a member of the firm of Andrews
and Manning. The latter is a son of the
late William P. Manning, four times Mayor
of Sydney. An early Andrews kept the
Ship and Mermaid Inn, in Gloucester-street,
about 80 years ago. He was a shipowner,
shipbuilder, and prospered generally when
whalers sailed in and out of Sydney Harbor.
The firm of solicitors was founded by the
late William Whaley Billyard, who took his
son-in-law, Captain Moseley, into the busi-
ness. Eventually the firm became Billyard,
Andrews and Moseley, and later Andrews,
Moseley and Manning. Captain Moseley, at
85 or about, is still a mere youth, busily
doing War Chest work.
J. li. G. Adams, of Adelaide Public
Library, has had just 40 years in the service
of the Board which controls it as well as
the Art Gallery and the Museum. He is
secretary of the Board now, and all his
colleagues got together last week to mark
the occasion by a presentation. It took
the appropriate form of a water-color paint-
ing by G. A. Barnes.
CHEER-O!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
If I should fall and Death unknown should leap
Out of the distant darknesses 1 face;
If I should leave this pleasant, dreaming place
For some strange sphere, do not so much as weep.
Na} r , all the old familiar customs keep.
Sing Joy, and gather Love in swift embrace;
Nor turn aside with slow and solemn pace,
From Life’s bright morning-ways to Sorrow’s deep.
I should not know you if you walked in woe
And garbed yourselves in Grief’s grey drapery.
Be blithe with laughter all the long years through,
That I, remembering, may come and go.
O friends, keep living full and free for me
So I, the seeming dead, may live in you.
N.S.W.
V. C.

MINCHINBURY
Tied tape did not count,, for much in the
early official letters in N. S. Wales. In 1803
Governor King received from his secretary,
W. N. Chapman, the following reply to
some remarks of Captain Colnett, of H.M.S.
Glatton :
I hereby declare that Captain Colnett, or any
other person who dares to say that ever I gave it
out publicly and officially, as secretary to his
Excellency Governor King, that the Glatton was to
sail at any particular time is a liar, a scoundrel and
a vagabond, and that whoever he is, if he has the
spirit to come forward, that I will wring his nose
and spit in his face.
No gentleman could go further without being
offensive.
The Germans interned on Soames Island
(M.L.) had reason to fear last week
that a new form of frightfulness was
about to be launched, with themselves in
the role of victims. The wooden coastal
steamer Defender caught fire at a Welling-
ton wharf while loading case-oil from an oil-
steamer, and after frantic efforts had been
made to put out the flames the little ship
was towed across the harbor aw r ay from the
city towards Soames Island, leaving behind a
long trail of smoke and flame. Eventually
the blazing vessel was beached on a reef
on the windward point of the island, and the
Ftitzes enjoyed a stirring and stifling after-
noon. Two nights previously four crippled
Germans, who because of their infirmity had
extra liberty, escaped on a semi-submersible
raft made of kerosene-tins and biscuit-boxes.
After four hours spent in sitting lip to their
waists in water and rowing with spades,
they reached the railway line and road on
the coast of the harbor, where one of the
adventurers collapsed. The others, though
more dead than alive, got a passing taxi to
take them to the city. He ran them to
the police-station, where the brotherly Huns
mentioned casually that they had left a half-
dead mate on the beach some miles back.
John Hop tore out aboard a taxi, and found
the German half awash and completely
settled. He died on the way to town.
“Dave L.”: One dark night I was stroll-
ing along a badly-lit Melbourne street, when
a hungry shadow caught up to me and
yelped cheerfully with the rising inflection
of Yorkshire, “Will ye give, me some money
for a plate of soup? I'm going away to the
country to-morrer.” As I was feeling pretty
good, and hoped never to meet him again, I
gave him a shilling and lie left me. Half
an hour later I was coming back the same
way, deep in thought, when a familiar cry
hit me on the ear. “Will ye give me some
money for a plate of soup? I’m goin’ away
to the” “D —n you!” I shouted, “I’ve
just given you enough money to swim in
soup!” He left me deeply dejected. If lie
is still practising I guess he has revised his
system- so that he progresses at regular
intervals down the bill-of-fare.
* * * *
The occasional civil servant who tries to
give the public good value for his wages is
often worse than the other sort who thinks
he is dying of overwork when he’s really only
suffering from sluggish intellect. A Sydney
woman recently tried to register her new tea-
room as (say) the Jane and Betsy. There
was a succession of hitches at the Circum-
locution Department, and at last she tackled
the Comptroller of Signboards and demanded
to know what all the shuffling was over—she
only wanted to paint two homely names on
a wall. “Well,” remarked the' cautious
official, “it’s war-time, you know, and we’ve
got to be careful.”

The Lloyd George Government is unani-
mously condemned in London press for
sanctioning a flood of knighthoods in
connection with the new Order of the
British Empire. It was originally supposed
that this was to be the civilian D. 5.0., a
recognition of exceptional service in war time,
but in practice thousands of minor officials,
contractors and departmental parasites of
all sorts and characters, many of military
age but drawing big salaries in cushy jobs
in London, have been nominated to its mem-
bership. Westminster Gazette says the
whole thing is calcu-
lated to bring title-
giving into con-
tempt and derision,
though it’s late in the
century to suggest
that. The Daily
News indulges in this
pleasantry: “What
did you do in the
great war, Daddy ?”
“I kept out of the
Honors List, my son.”
Hall Caine is one of
the new’ Knights of
the British Empire,
in reward for his un-
paid penwork on be-
half of the cause of
the Allies in America,
Italy and Ireland.
Poet Alfred Noyes
was only deemed
worthy of a “com-
panionship.” and the
same small satisfac-
tion was given to
war-writer “lan Hay,”
who in real life
is Major John Hay
Beitli.' The K.B.E.
also goes to Harry
Brittain (who organ-
ised the first Imperial
Press Conference),
Bertram Lima (who
runs the London Daily Mirror and Sun-
day Pictorial) and to Howard Spicer,
member of the paper-manufacturing firm of
London and Sydney. Spicer is said to have
done good work in providing the Ministry
of Munitions with numerous paper substi-
tutes for metals.
“Nor’-West” : Education-Minister James,
of N. S. Wales, recently deplored the fact
that out of some thousands of school-children
who were examined by the Department’s
medical staff and found to be suffering from
adenoids and other throat troubles, a very
small percentage was treated for these com-
plaints. He expressed considerable astonish-
ment that, although a fair number of chil
dren with defective teeth were attended to.
the more serious complaints were apparently
let “rip.” Well, the reason is not very diffi-
cult to find. About four years ago my uni-
versity was visited by one of the Department
doctors, or doctoresses—it was a lady—and
my heir-apparent, among others, was found
to be suffering with adenoids, and I was
strongly advised to have him operated on. I
was assured by the oldest inhabitant that it
would only cost a “few bob” in Sydney, so
young Nor’-West was taken to the city,
underwent the operation satisfactorily, which
took place in a private hospital, and we went
home rejoicing. Then the shock came. The
doctor only charged six guineas, which, toge-
ther with hospital fee and the train fares,
made 10 pounds look a mere trifle. The coun-
try medico usually charges more than his
average brother sawbones of the city, conse-
quently it does not require a very brilliant
flight of imagination to solve why parents
don’t give the defective children the needful
professional attention. Little Billjim is
taken to the dentist to have his teeth “fixed
up,” for that, as a rule, does cost only a
“few bob,” while his adenoids, etc., are left
to look after themselves. As the Education
Dept.’s travelling hospital staff can, appa-
rently, only treat a very limited number of
children it is up to Minister James to make
some arrangements with Sawbones to be
more reasonable in his charges for the benefit
of the nation.
