A. _ Barnett, formerly one of Sydney sti-
pendiary beaks, appeared last week before
the Royal Commission which is inquiring
into the working or otherwise of N. S.
Wales Civil Service. With 40 years’
experience of blight and barnacles behind
him, Barnett denounced the whole chloro-
formed contraption from the Public Service
Board down. As an instance of the ineffi-
ciency of the P. 5.8., when Reid ordered
retrenchment, he quoted the case of an old
civil servant with 11 children who was
suddenly handed a fortnight’s notice. The
wretched man fainted away and, when he
came to, although previously no drinker
and an efficient worker, he went out, got
drunk, and remained so for a week. Eventu-
ally he died as a result of the way he had
been treated. Barnett told how he tackled
a job years ago with a staff of 11, of which
eight were workers and three were merely
civil servants. They yawned so much and
so exhaustedl.v that Barnett fled to an
Under-Secretary and begged him to sack
them. But it couldn’t be done. Probably
the Under-Secretary was too tired.

NIGHT STOCK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
From Winterbroolc to Ashton
The road is white and clear.
In the low light, in the moonlight,
In the heyday of the year,
To Winterbrook from Ashton
Riding came my dear!
A columbine, a red rose,
A tiger-lily grand,
And pansies blue as elves grew
In fields of Faeryland,
To Winterbrook from Ashton
lie brought me in his hand!
But more than royal red rose
Or tiger-lily fine,
A blue bowl, a pearl bowl
Abrim with wizard wine ;
lie brought me scented night stock,
And all the world was mine!
A purple leaf, a green leaf,
A hint of pink and brown
And deep in charmed sleep
I laid my senses down,
And heard the beat of wee feet,
The fairies come to town!
Oh, Faery Town is Dream Town,
And none may break the spell,
But the earth-scent and the wind-song
And the murmur of a shell
At moonset when the ebb tide
Is tolling like a bell!
And Faery time is all time,
Nor aye is near or far,
For heart’s need or love’s meed,
A mercy or a star,
The bless&d gates of ivorie
For ever stand ajar!
The wind that wakes the clove buds
Blows east by Hindustan.
From Celebes the spice breeze
Brings junk and merchantman,
With frankincense and camphor
And myrrh and frangipan!
But fleeter through the palm seas,
Low-loaded to the rail.
With scented snow of peach blow-
Like foam before the gale;
To Faery Town with witchgold
The fleets of Faery sail!
And first they gave me lad’s love,
And mint and dragon’s tongue,
But the last gift was black thrift—
Slow, slow the sea-tide swung,
And white as bone the moon set,
The bells of Faery rung!
Oh, soon they gave me witchgold,
But syne they brought me rue,
And the earth-scent and the wind song
Like hounds of night ran through,
And loudly belled the white stag,
The hours of Faery blew !
The columbine, the red rose,
The tiger-lily grand,
The pansies blue as elves grew
Were dust within my hand ;
But mine for aye the night stock,
My Faery contraband.
From Winterbrook to Ashton
The road is white and clear.
In the low light, in the moonlight,
In the heyday of the year,
To Winterbrook from Ashton
Riding came my dear!
VICTORIA.
MARIE E. J. PITT.

HROUGHOUT the history
of the player-piano the Pianola
has been the leader —the in-
strument without a peer.
The infinite variety of touch —the rich
tonal effects —the almost limitless range of
musical expression it makes possible—
have brought the Pianola the patronage
and endorsement of the great musicians, of
the leading educators, of the men and
women foremost in every walk of life.
Such universal recognition is won only by
distinctly superior quality —by unqualified
supremacy in all essentials.
Pianola perfection is a product of many
minor perfections, but a few of its features
are outstanding and revolutionary. .
The Metrostyle is an instance. This
wonderful tempo control is unapproached
in effectiveness. It aids the novice in
music to utilise with fine artistic effect the
wonderful technical resources of the Pianola
—this by guidance of the famous “red
line” interpretations recorded by the
most eminent of modern musicians.
Be careful and see that our trade-mark
“PIANOLA” appears on the piano of
your choice! You have a world-famed
selection for your consideration in the
Steinway, Stecß, Stroud,
British Weber,
Farrand and Aeolian
Pianola Pianos.

“0.K.”: Yic. Assembly last week con-
firmed a previous decision to extend North
Melbourne Market to where the old original
cemetery was established. “Because it is
desired that cabbages should be sold a little
more cheaply, it is proposed to act as vandals
and desecrate God’s acre,” said Sergeant
Ryan, as though every progressive city with
centuries of history behind it hadn’t pro-
gressed on similar desecrations. And the
House was solemnly assured that “the move-
ment of the bodies would be done with all
due reverence and respect.” Bodies as good
as those of the forgotten pioneers of Mel-
bourne are not getting much due reverence
and respect in France and Belgium to-day. I
have seen a couple of old London church-
yards dug up, after the churches had been
duly deconsecrated in the presence of 10 men
and a boy (I was the boy), and in one case
I got away with a thigh-bone from a little
pile of mixed “remains.” This was the yard
of the church where the late Milton had been
christened, so the bone I pinched was pro-
bably not more than 250 years old. Some
of the bones in the old Melbourne cemetery
must be already 70-year-old relics. One can
only value the contents of a grave by the
amount of attention or neglect which the
ground has received since the last occupant
was put there. Doubtless the grower of the
bone I made off with from the yard of
that London church was deeply mourned
for some time after her decease (it was a
lady bone), but the mourners passed out in
their turns and the grave ceased to matter.
That’s how it is with these graves in Melb.
old cemetery. When bones have ceased to
matter nobody should lie so silly and hypo-
critical as to talk of doing them reverence,
simply because they are bones.

Henry Cook, convicted last week of hav-
ing, in company with an unknown, stuck up
and robbed two Yic.' Printing Office pay
clerks of £4471. goes into retirement for 10
years, principally because the victims were
not up to schedule time in arriving on the
scene. Cook and his accomplice knew what
hour to expect the officials back at the
printing-office, and wore at the door with a
motor-cycle and side-car; but the clerks
were delayed at the bank for 25 minutes
that chilly morning, so the engine couldn't
be kept running. While they were waiting
the stopped machinery got cold and a couple
of employees had a good look at the hangers-
on. When it was time for the get-away the
chilled engine needed coaxing, which the
robbers hadn’t time for. Then a shot was
fired into the petrol tank and the vehicle
was left behind, necessitating half a ton of
laborious explanation, which a jury declined
to believe. Had the weather been warmer
the job might have worked. Incidentally the
detectives' seem to have neglected no point
in tracing the real or imaginary progress
of Cook that morning. Even the suburban
tearoom waitress who served hint arid bis
lady friend with lunch was called to testify
that the customers ate nothing!

“8.T.”: A curious character turned up
at a' Melb. city hotel recently, and stayed
for a few hours. His name is not Tweedale,
and he owns a lump of land on a Murray
tributary, the finest soil in his district, and
is rich, a bachelor, and lone from choice. He
is, in short. a
notorious woman-
hater, and afflicted
with a quaint be-
lief that all the
females within
miles, young and
old, lie awake of
nights concocting
schemes against
his freedom and
peace. For greater
security he has
made his home in
a hut on an un-
stable island in
the river. This
island is infested
with rats, and
Tweedale encour-
ages the vermin as
a bodyguard, argu-
ing cunningly that
no daughter of
Eve will dare in-
vade his domain
with hostile intent
while it swarms
with famished ro-
dents. It was
almost compulsion
brought Tweedale
to Melbourne, and
from the moment
he set foot in the
city he betrayed
the liveliest appre-
hension. Wherever
he turned there
were women. He
fled to the appoint-
ed hotel, and there
were women there;
women in the bar,
women in the kit-
chen, women in all
the rooms and pas-
sages. Tweedale
complained to the
landlord that he
had been given a
room between two
women, and was courteously conducted to
an apartment on the other side of the
house. The landlord opened a window and
explained casually: “An outdoor iron stair-
case, you see—for escape." “My G —d !”
said Tweedale. “Are they as bad as that
in Melbourne?” By the next train the agi-
tated tiller returned to his island and his
bodyguard.
“Bill": We are fighting in famous wine
country, and there is sometimes as much fun
in getting the right stuff as there is in drink-
ing it. In a village recently captured by
us, our burly intelligence-officer went a-hunt-
ing in some cellars. He lit a match and
was just putting his revolver back in the
holster when he heard a whine of “Ivam-
erad!” He leaped back arid found two
dejected Fritzes waiting for him like lost
children. He swelled up his chest arid
marched them back as if he had been fightirig
lions. After that we rolled a bomb down
each cellar for luck. The French were on
the job, too, and we admired their systematic
way of working. A dozen men went, out with
a stretcher. If unlucky the stretcher brought
back a man. but if the luck was in it bore a
freight of bottles.

“Eastern”: “Roughcoal” (B. l/1/’IS) is
correct in saying that the Loch Sloy was
wrecked on Kangaroo Island in 1809. Nine-
teen years ago I was in the telegraph-office
at Kingscote, the island’s capital, and I
have a clear recollection of preparing the
death-registration papers of 21 of the crew
one day and several others subsequently,
most of them “name unknown” and some
of them “sex unknown," so severely battered
were they. If my memory serves me well
only three were ultimately saved. Four
actually got ashore, one of whom was the
only passenger aboard, a young man making
the trip for his health’s sake. The sailors
were barefooted, but the passenger had on
boots, which he gave to one of his com-
panions to aid him in securing help. The
passenger elected to stay on the spot, whilst
two sailors struck westward along the unin-
habited coast. The other went eastward. The
latter reached the homestead of an Outback
squatter next day, and later the other two
were found near to Cape Borda in a half-
demented condition. They had lived for
some days on dead birds, etc., picked up on
the coast, and, when found, were consider-
ably decorated by numerous over-ripe speci-
mens hung round their necks. With the aid
of a local tracker search veas made for the
passenger, and by reason of the tufts of
wool left from his socks on the flinty stones
he was eventually found a couple of miles
inland —dead, of course. The bodies and
portions of bodies were conveniently dis-
posed of on the spot, mainly by covering
them, where they lay with rocks. Some
months later the then second keeper at
Cape Borda was given the gruesome job by
the Government of removing all the scraps
and giving them proper burial at a more
suitable spot. I greatly doubt “Roughcoal’s”
statement re the hidden whisky. “Rough-
coal” chides “Briner” for omitting the Loch
Vennacher from the Loch sinkings off K. 1.;
but he himself omits the Loch Torrid'on'.
which disappeared with all hands somewhere
south-west of Cape Willoughby. The Island
has been unkind to the Loch line.

BIG-GAME HUNTERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Says a Melbourne' philosopher: “In nothing is
hereditary more pointedly demonstrated than in
the persistence of the hunter’s instincts in civilised
man. Man remains the hunter in spite of training
and environment, while in woman we find a powerful
natural aversion to all those pastimes that delight
the hunter.”
The little maid comes from her school
In sweet simplicity.
She is not taught the killer's rule,
No lethal brand has she.
Her hands are soft as roses white,
Her great eyes fill with sudden fright.
Her dimples shelter witchery,
They are not pits for game.
Her jewelled mouth is good to see.
From that with cunning aim
There issues forth no poisoned dart
To cleave a trusting creature’s heart.
The strong man strikes the lion down,
He takes the mammoth’s skin,
He sees the horned hjppo drown,
And brings the tiger in.
Man hunts big game where’er he can—
The maiden hunts and takes the man!
VICTORIA.

HOW TO RELIEVE STOMACH PAINS
IN A FEW MINUTES.
Nine times out of ten when your stomach
hurts, when you are doubled up with the
pains of dyspepsia and indigestion, it is
simply because of acid which burns and in-
flames the delicate stomach lining, and fer-
menting food which forms gas that distends
the stomach just like blowing up a toy
balloon. In cases like this—and most all
stomach trouble is directly or indirectly due
to acid and fermentation—almost instant
relief may be obtained by taking in a little
hot water immediately after eating or when-
ever pain is felt, a half-teaspoonful of Bisu-
rated Magnesia, obtainable at any chemist
at small cost. This simple remedy may seem
old-fashioned, but those who have used it say
that besides being inexpensive it beats the
expensive remedies they have tried for quick
and positive relief. Try it and see. —Advt,
Elsie Ferguson,

POLITICAL
POINTS
The loan of £4,750,000 which the Com-
monwealth is raising in London on behalf of
the States that can’t pay their way was
only permitted by the British Government
on the promise that there would be no more
borrowing for temporary and unproductive
Shirks while the war endures. The war
may endure a long time yet, so the. Last
Loan is a” solemn event, like the Burial of
Sir John Moore or the Lay of the Last
Minstrel, or the Fall of the House of
Usher.
According to the Local Government Bill,
now before N. S. Wales Parliament, coun-
cils are to have power to prevent overcrowd-
ing—whether by providing more space <?r by
shoving the overcrowders into internment
camps pending developments doesn’t appear.
Until the date of last census affairs were
steadily improving especially with the
humbler classes. In 1891. out of every
10,000 N. S. Wales, inhabitants, 305 lived in
one-roomed dwellings; in 1911 only 178. The
two-roomers diminished from 455 per 10,000
to 236. The three- and four-roomers
diminished from 2720 to 2393. The solid
increase was in the five-and-six-room crowd
who stand for unpretentious comfort (3240
to 4103). The seven-to-10-roomers, who also
stand for solid comfort but begin to look like
luxury, increased only from 1973 to 2124 per
10,000 people. The really rich crowd, dwell-
ing in houses of 11 rooms and upwards, re-
presented only 969 out of every 10,000 people
in 1891, and were down to 781 in 1911, so
they were a diminishing race. Getting down
to finer details, among 1,494.504 inmates of
private dwellings in 1911 the medium stan-
dard of comfort was represented by four-
roomers (303,967) ; five-roomers, the most
numerous class (366,640) ; six-roomers
(295,975) and seven-roomers (155,418).
There were nearly 50,000 dwellers in houses
with 20 or more rooms, but nearly 46,000 of
these were dwellers in hash-houses or pious
institutions. The very big house that is
just house and not hash is very rare nowa-
days, and the name of the ambitious ances-
tor who built it is almost forgotten. A side-
issue is that when the count was made in
1911 the average rent in Sydney was 3s. 3d.
per inhabitant per week while in the rest of
the State it was Is. lOd. But congestion
must be paid for. Probably the house
famine has altered things a good deal of late.
“ J.G.’s” recollection ;
Re the late Max Hirsch (B. 1/8/T8). I knew the
oily German very well, and he had an astonishing
following among the squatters and cockies of Northern
Victoria, though this was somewhat strange in view
of their bitter opposition to a land tax of any
description, and Hirsch’s two main ideas were (1)
to tear down the tariff wall and to dump in German
goods to kill local industries, (2) to make up for
the loss of Customs revenue by means of the single-
tax. Hirsch maintained that the land should pay
the State’s whole revenue. He attempted to get
into the Assembly just prior to Federation, when he
made an unsuccessful attack on the seat held by Dick
O’Neill, strong Protectionist and good Australian;
this was the old Mandurang electorate, now absorbed
by Eaglehawk and G’unbower. I never saw an
electorate before or since that was so flooded by
canvassers and spruikers in the interest of Germany,
That contest must have cost the Freetrade candidate
thousands and thousands of pounds, and as Hirsch
admitted being a poor man the question as to who
paid the’ money is easy to answer. But although he
had got the bump, he wasn’t done with. He took
upon himself the work of interviewing Departmental
heads and such like on behalf of Mandurang electors,
work that is always left to the sitting member ; and
the heads, instead of kicking the interloper off the
office mat, seemed to welcome him and helped him
to undermine the influence of O’Neill. Cases are on
record where, after the member for the district was
unable to get through red-tape entanglements, the
plausible German would take a matter up and get
what was wanted done, without any trouble evidently.
When Federation was brought about Hirsch came out
as the Freetrade candidate for the division that con-
tained the State electorate of Mandurang, and again
money was spent like water; and the Protectionist
candidate, Jamie McColl, had his work cut
out to win the seat. He succeeded, how-
ever, by a margin of a hundred or two. Another
State election soon followed this first Federal elec-
tion, and Hirsch again bobbed up in opposition to
O’Neill. This time no chances were taken by the

Foreign Trade crowd, and the sectarian devil was
dragged out by the tail and set to work. Hirsch
this time got the seat. At the Federal contest he
threw up the place, as, of course, a Hun agent could
do very little to assist Protectionist Germany in the

went down this time with a crash, and soon after

It would be interesting to know what information he

And it would be interesting to know what the Win-

time after his arrival in Germany he went to Russia,
no doubt on the same mission as that which brought
him to Australia. He died there.
For the year which closed on June 30
Australia had £15,000,000 worth less im-
ports than in the year before —partly be-
cause we couldn’t get more for lack of ships,
partly because we couldn’t pay for more
through lack of money. If the tariff; had
been fixed to make this diminution perma-
nent we might have made £15,000,000 worth
more goods to fill the gap, but as the tariff
says that the old importing game is to
resume in all its vigor when the war is over
we just did without a lot of things and
paid absurd prices for those that were avail-
able.
“Cosmos”; Queensland Leg. Council is
represented on the Opposition side by many
tired old men who still breathe the Tory
politics of the Mcllwraithian era without
any of the vigor of that dead and gone Scot.
A. J. Tliynne, one-time Minister for Justice,
wearily contributes his quota to the debates,
somewhat feebly resentful of the new bloods
of the Labor party occupying the benches
which used to be exclusive to bloated indivi-
duals who owned vast tracts of Queensland
territory and unlimited quantities of unearned
increment. Leahy snap's out sarcastic inter-
jections from the same end of the Chamber,
while the balance of ancients, with the ex-
ception of Fowles and O’Shea, look on and
silently acquiesce in any slating ladled out
to the interlopers over the way. Fowles,
somewhat commonplace of aspect until moved
by his outraged emotions, fires into a small
tornado of invective, and is an effective casti-
gator. O’Shea, the legal facilitator of the
House, despite some hard-crusted convictions,
is valuable in the polishing of measures sent
U p from the Assembly, obviously wishful to
mould them into law sound and unassailable.
Jones, big and breezy, reminiscent of a
schoolboy going through his task, leads the
Chamber with a cheerfulness that tones down
the acridity of critics. Crampton is a
capable lieutenant, while Nevitt—selecting
three standing out from the Ministerial
crowd—plunges boldly into the fray with
refreshing independence.

“J.C.S.” : Apparently the verses in which
J. C. Squire (B. 1/8/TS) gives “a nasty
one” to South Australia are a plagiarism
from Belloc’s “Lord Lundy,” published a
few years ago. There also the subject is a
young man who is pushed by his relatives
into politics, and one of the verses runs:—
The Duke, his aged grandsire, bore
The shame till he could bear no more.
He rallied his declining powers,
Summoned the youth to Brockley Towers,
And bitterly addressed him thus:
“Sir, you have disappointed us!
We had intended you to be
The next Prime Minister but three;
The stocks were sold, the press was squared,
The Middle Class was quite prepared;
But as it is!....my language tails
Go out and govern New South Wales l”
This (of course unintentional) plagiarism is
very curious, seeing that Mr. Squire and Mr.
Belloc are, I think, closely associated in
“Land and Water.” However, what about
the “nasty one” now?

The Commonwealth is borrowing a mere
trifle of £4,750,000 in London and paying
per cent, for it with a discount of 10s. per
cent, thrown in. In Australia it offers only
5 per cent, with no discount, though it wants
no less than £40,000,000. But in Australia
anybody can buy £lOOO worth of stock with
£lOOO worth of the Government’s paper pro-
mise to pay in gold. That currency doesn’t
pass in England ; the gold itself is required.
A London trade journal has patted the Ma
State on the head with a club re a remit-
tance by cheque of £1 and some coppers. It
was accompanied by four foolscap forms on
thick paper, three receipt forms on large
thick paper, a thick letter signed by a thick-
headed person, and a thick envelope. Still
the Ma State is waking up to the need of
reform. A royal commission appointed on
fine thick paper is looking into things.
But it isn’t only Governments which suffer
from megalomania. The One Big Union,
for instance, is to have a Grand Council of
14; six Provincial Councils of. eight mem-
bers each; six Departments in each State
with their Councils; and six Councils, one
for each division. Also, many other offices
arid officers. And nothing of any importance
is to be done without reference to the Grand
Council at the top.
“The Pariah”: “L.L.W.” (B. 8/S/T8) is
off the track when In 1 scoffs at the teaching
of Japanese in Sydney University and, in
place, advocates Esperanto. His statement
that it takes eight years to learn the Jap
language is ail wrong. I know men who
wrote and spoke it in two years. Three years
is the time set down for learning Chinese —
an infinitely harder task owing to the enor-
mous number of characters. (About 3000 is
all you want in Japanese.) The hidebound
Britisher is hard to teach. From 1900 on to
the year of calamity, British trade in China
was losing ground to (he German. The reason
was that the British boasted that he had been
10 years (or 20 years) in China and couldn’t
speak the language, whereas the young Ger-
man who had learnt the language could do
personally what a Britisher could only do
through an interpreter. As taught in our
High Schools. Japanese is being done well,
but if “L.L.W.” had protested against the
way in which it is being done at the Univer-
sity I would be with Tiim. There must be
some explanation why so many of the 70 or
so enthusiasts who began last year have
defaulted. I am one of the defaulters (and I
am in good company), though I am keeping
the study up privately. One last suggestion
(God bless the Censor!) : England was an
ally of Prussia 300 years ago. If she had
footled round with volapuk or ido, instead of
German !
STEADFASTNESS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The message which I send to the people of the
British Empire on the fourth anniversary of their
entry into the war, is, ‘Hold fast!’ In no other
way can be ensured a world set free from war.”
—Lloyd George.
The years loom long in the future painted;
But the long years shrink in the treasured past,
Since war, at whose shadow our hearts had fainted
Touched palms with the sons of our lands at last.
By shadowy Ways and by quiet waters,
Go hosts whom the valleys of twilight bless,
With white flowers scattered by Death’s pale daughters
Who wept for our soldiers’ steadfastness....
Oh! the beat of their drums said,
“Steadfastness!”
The years burn out over fields of glory,
The deathless glory that lives by pain.
And we must envisage in line and story
The faces we never shall see again.
And we must have courage, like theirs undaunted,
Who faced such deaths as ‘we cannot guess,
Lest vain were the pain of their valor vaunted,
And lost the reward of their steadfastness.
Oh, the smile in their eyes was
Steadfastness 1
The years loom long ’mid the future’s shadows,
But short is the path each man may tread,
By the mountain ways and the blossomed meadows
And the breaking seas, to the sunset red.
And pleasure shall beckon to us to tarry
And sloth shall come in its careless dress;
But the deathless message which we must carry,
For our sons to stand to, is “Steadfastness.”
For the blood of men’s heart spelt
Steadfastness.

FIRST COUSIN JACK : “Dad 'e ever see two
men more alike than John and Silas?"
SECOND COUSIN JACK: “No, I never ded, an’

“J. Ivin”: Whilst shearing up North
recently I came across the remains of the old bush
shanty on the banks of the Barcoo (Q.) made famous
by Banjo Paterson in his poem, “A Bush Christen-
ing.” Although the owner of the shanty is called
“Michael McGee” in the poem, his real name was
Michael McGuffen, and he kept the pub for some
years up to the time of his death. On his tombstone
is an inscription that says he died whilst looking,
for water. He is buried about three-quarters of a
mile from the old shanty.
* * * *
“E.R.P.” : The best scheme that I know of
for exterminating rabbits was that devised by a
farmer at Blayney (N.S.W.) in 1914, the time of
the drought. He had a thickly-wooded 800-acre
paddock which was infested with bunnies. In one
corner there was a small dam, the only watering
place in the vicinity for miles around. This dam
he roughly fenced in with a rabbit-proof wire-netting
fence, and in the netting he cut a few holes over
which he placed wire so as to make a door after
the style used in pigeon traps, where the bird can
push the door in and gain admittance but cannot
get out again, because the door will not open back-
wards. At sunrise and sundown every day he visited
this trap and let the dogs in. Then the slaughter
would begin. llow those dogs did look forward to
sunrise and sunset! It took about three weeks to
thoroughly clear the paddock of the rabbits. I’ll
admit that this scheme is only workable under cer-
tain conditions, but when it is workable it is
absolutely the most efficient rabbit exterminator
known, and I’ve had a little experience, I can assure
you.
* * * *
“Black Bonnet”: Re paw-paws. At
Rossville (Q.), where they grow to perfection
and ripen all the year round without cultivation,
we drop the seeds from a ripe paw-paw anywhere,
and when the young trees appear we wait until
they flower so that we can make sure of the sex,
then pull up the male trees and as many of the
others as we want. It is considerate of people to
set the paw-paws in pairs—male and female. 1 never
bother about it. The nearest male tree on my place
for years was a quarter of a mile away, and we had
heavy and continual crops of fruit. Have never known
a female tree to bear male flowers, but have had
several male trees on which fruit has formed and
ripened, depending from the branching blossoms
instead of growing to the trunk as with the female
tree. The freaks are not the same shape as the
legitimate fruit, being long and somewhat like
cucumbers, but they are perfectly good for eating.
* * * *
“Bouragi”: “Barcoo,” paralysed at the
Papuan giant frogs paraded by me, asks airily if
I have ever heard of the Borneo frog which carries
about a balloon which it inflates when wishing to
rise in the world. I have; but the only specimen I
ever met was attacked by enemy mosquitoes when
aloft taking aerial observations, and broke the frog's
equivalent of a neck in the subsequent fall. Does
“Barcoo” know the giant frog of Surinam? He
allows his missus to lay her eggs on his broad back.
Then the skin softens and swells up round each egg,
embedding it in the hide. When the eggs are hatched
the infants continue to inhabit the holes, and every-
where that father fares the family’s sure to go.
When the old man thinks the time is ripe, he sheds
both hide and offspring and goes a-wooing next
Spring again. The missus apparently fades out of
the picture after starting the trouble. The habit
ought tp be encouraged.
* * * *
“Shepherd” : The acacia family, the white-
wood and the leopard-wood yield great quantities of
gum, which when treated properly is as good as the
best imported. Pound the gum and fill a
bottle two-thirds of its capacity. Add
cold water until the bottle is almost full.
’Hie gum dissolves in a short time, and a
little alcohol should then be put in to
prevent fermentation. This gum will keep
for years if necessary. Resin from the
pine-tree is prepared like gum except
that methylated spirits or alcohol is used
instead of water. This is superior to gum
for sticking purposes, and if once set is
not affected by water; while if turpentine
be added till the mixture is thinned an
excellent varnish results. . When the bark
of the acacia, which grows on low flats
and river-courses out West, is chopped
fine and boiled in water for two hours the
mixture is very suitable for tanning small
skins.. Put the skins into a cask or other
suitable vessel, and pour the mixture over
them. In six weeks take them out and
rub with a little beef-fat or mutton-fat
while drying. When dry they are soft
and pliable and the fur is not damaged in
the least. I have always found this bark
superior to black wattle. The “sandal-
wood” of western N. S. Wales produces a
tar which is equal to the best imported
from Stockholm. Although the pine is
very good for producing tar, it is worth
more for its wood, but the sandalwood is
too small for wood and produces more tar
than the pine. Why do we send to foreign
places for these products, when our own
land teems with as good if not better?

“Gerardy”: Down by Jericho, where sport
is but a memory and amusement a bygone dream,
vermin of every conceivable shade and appearance
abound. Specially prevalent arc two species of
scorpions, 1 one black, the other a pale yellow. When-
ever face to face these horrors duel till one or both
are completely out of action. Size and weight arc
no criterion, but the struggle mostly ends with the
black on top. A common practice is to obtain
one of each breed and place them together in an
empty fruit-tin. The ensuing proceedings brighten
up the monotony of our controversies with Abdul. At
present we are in possession of a heavyweight,
undefeated black bully with over a dozen victories to
his credit.
“F.T.7”: “Eme,” who recently praised
the wedge-tailed eagle (or eaglehawk, as he calls it)
as an article of diet, evidently likes his game “high.”
A confirmed carrion-feeder, the huge fowl is one of
the rankest birds that fly, not excluding the big sea
birds, all the reverse of violets in their way.
“F.G.L.”: In Canberra I witnessed a
battle .between a rcd-backcd spider and a colony of
sugar-ants. The redbaek had blundered into the
ants’ lines, and before he could backpedal and make
a break for neutral territory a couple of ants had
grabbed a leg each while a third signalled for the
main body to come up. In a few minutes they came
swarming over the top and attacked in mass. The
spider disappeared under the first wave of shock
troops. He emerged badly knocked about but full
of fight, and began to slug- his way through a party
that was trying-to cut off his line of ret real. Wave
after wave of the enemy were rushed to the assault,
only to .find their attacks crumple up a few inches

WEEN THE COACH COMES IN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The little town is quiet and still,
And scarce a soul stirs down the street,
While faintly from the distant mill
There sounds the stamper’s thudding beat;
But twice a week, at half-past four,
With quickened life the folk begin
To gather outside Conlv’s store
And wait until the coach comes in.
Down the grey road that skirts the ridge,
Across the dip and round the bend,
With loose planks rattling on the bridge
The wheels spin to the journey's end.
And some there are who, making back
To love and friends, to home- and kin,
Have trodden long the exile’s track,
But leave it when the coach comes in.
A little town, yet wide and far
Its thoughts reach out o’er land and sea,
To fields where shines an alien star
And where less quiet customs be;
From girls who felt the city’s thrill,
From lads who write ’mid red war's din,
From long-left homes, remembered still,
Come letters when the coach comes in.
The great world lives its hate-filled days,
The rifles crack, the cannons roar;
The little town in busliland ways
Is set far-off from lust of war;
Yet even here there chills its breath,
And women’s cheeks with care are thin,
Hearts beat lest word from fields of death
May stab them when the coach comes in.
TASMANIA.

FOSTER’S LAGER
“M.P.”: Can’t answer “Yannatherra’s”
inquiry (B. 4/7/’18) as to whether it is the sight or
the smell of their dead that frightens horses; but
when in France I found that horses coming across a
corpse in the roadway shied and in many cases com-
pelled the rider to make a detour in order to pass the
carcase. Not that the rider objected very much. I
saw this happen only in cases where the prad had
not been in the front area for some time. They
apparently get used to both sight and smell, as I
have seen many horses and mules plodding quietly
along the Bellewarde sleeper-track, which ran from
Menin-road towards Westhoek Ridge, and always had
a good supply of dead horse and mule flesh along it.
“Vee”: William Lane’s pilgrims at New
Australia found it rather difficult to accept the
principle of “the co-operative owmersliip of all pro-
duction and property.” Fortunately there was scant
opportunity of the pioneers securing anything of
value, so that the rule was not strained to an} 7
great extent until Mrs. Jones became the envied
possessor of a fowl. It happened this way. Mrs.
Jones was an expert lace-creator and one particularly
fine piece of dainty fabric she owned attracted the
eye of a Paraguayan maid, who coaxed the New
Australian to barter it in exchange for the hen. For
two days it cock-a-doodle-dooed secure in a coop
near its new owner’s hut. The bubble burst on the
second night, when the usual camp-fire parliament
was held, at which to-morrow’s job and to-day’s
trials were discussed. George Pope, an Adelaide
native, caught the chairman’s eye, and said he wanted
to know who the fowl belonged to that was harbored
by Mrs. Jones. “Is it the property of the settlement?”
lie went on, “or does Mrs. Jones infringe the rules
by keeping private stock which has to be fed from
the produce garnered by the collective industry of
the association ?” As no answer was forthcoming
he moved that the bird be declared an asset of the
colony, as it might lay eggs which in time would
result in chicks and fowls. After a classic debate
extending over two hours, the settlers decided that
the spirit of co-operative effort required Mrs. Jones’s
fowl to be confiscated for the common good. Mean-
time Mrs. Jones had vanished. A deputation was
sent to convey the wish of the meeting that the
poultry be handed over. As they made through
the tropical darkness toward the contraband they
heard several frantic squawks and arrived on the
scene in time to witness the lady holding a headless
chook. Mrs. Jones and family roasted and ate the
basis of argument.

“Waroo” : Apropos of “McNorth’s” re-
cent par. on blood-letting in the Cape York Penin-
sula. In Torres Strait the other day, a girl of 14
ran a needle, into the palm of her hand and in
trying to get it out broke the steel. A razor of
doubtful, cleanliness was procured to cut it out. The
girl’s sole comment during the operation was, “You
cut him more deep or he no come.” I saw the gash,
a good inch and a half long, and was assured by
the operator, “she never sing out.” The wound is
healing rapidly. I have frequently noticed great
fortitude displayed by these natives in serious hurts,
but they will make a fearful hullabaloo over trifles.

“F. 8.” : In a recent newspaper controversy
Archie Meston, the notable Bananalander, traces the
name “wahgoon” (scrub turkey) through seven differ-
ent dialects, from Bundaberg to the Tweed. He
said that in the Oonda district of Bribee Island
spoken by the Goondubbarie, it (the turkey) was
“wahwoon.” In the Coobenpil dialect of Lvtton, the
Noonuccal of Stradbroke, the Cateebil of Ipswich, the
Yoocum of the Logan, the Turrabul of North Bris-
bane, and in the Dippil of Dwumdur wahgoon was
the name of the scrub turkey.
* * * *
“Jobber”: In a recent Bulletin someone
wrote that Billy Doyle had driven the coach from
Bourke (N.S.W.) to Wanaaring for donkey’s years.
He hasn’t; he drove, for the greater part of his
time, between Bourke and Yantabulla and took the
Wanaaring run in April, 1908. My cobber and I did
a trip with the old man once, 82 miles, which occu-
pied 24 hours. The way he piloted his team of five
horses through the scrub on an almost invisible
track was a revelation to us. He doused the coach
lights after crossing the bridge, though the night
was as black as the Kaiser’s heart.
“Waroo”: One of the troubles (perhaps
the principal) incidental to travelling in Torres
Strait is the cockroach. It invades every-
thing that is not hermetically sealed. Open a tin
of milk or jam, and a few hours later a score or so
corpses of the pests are awaiting removal. If by
chance the mixture is left overnight, the undertakers,
provided by themselves, get to work and by the morn-
ing a tin is cleaner than when it first left the
factory. Care must be exercised in covering up
one’s extremities when trying to sleep, or a banquet
of minced fingers or devilled toenails is inevitable.
Some people become indignant if you kill a giant
’roach, because he is the only manicuring utensil
available. Sometimes a lugger is sunk for a day
or two to efrown the beasts. That is a good way if
the boat belongs to some other fellow who doesn’t
mind his fittings being spoilt by salt water. Cyanide
and sulphuric-acid makes an excellent destroyer,
but it is just as well to give that job to someone
else. Recently I tried 141 b. of sulphur with tip-
top results. But not for long. In a month or two
the pests were back and the hideous nightmare was
repeated.
* * * *
“Conbard” : “J.G.” (B. 11/7/T8) is right
in his contention that the Avenue of Honor proposed
at Wallan (Vic.) should consist of perennial Aus-
tralian trees, and not the deciduous elm and oak
and the pinus insignis. “J.G.’s” list of Australian
evergreens is a good one, but what’s wrong with the
kurrajong? It will grow in almost any portion
of the continent. A proof of this is the fact that in
the garden on Noekatunga station, in the Cooper
district (Q.), is one of the finest kurrajongs to be
seen anywhere. The plant originally came from
Bathurst.
“Mookingah” : Thomson Bros, captured a
fine specimen of a white kangaroo lately. This
marsupial had been seen several times and could
have been shot with ease, but they’re sports out that
way. One day the brothers got it in the open and
ran it down with horses, and carried it ten miles on
horseback to the homestead. The ’roo put up a
great fight on the journey by biting and using his
hind feet with vigor. The catch is milky white,
without one colored hair showing, about three-quarter
grown, and premises later to develop into a large-
sized kanga. This is the first white kangaroo caught
alive in these parts (Tibooburra, N.S.W.), although
one was shot at Mount Sturt some months back.
* * * *
“Sundowner” : I have struck a great num-
ber of bunda-bunda snakes (commonly known as
“bandy-bandy”) on the Richmond River (N.S.W.),
but I never saw one approaching 3ft. in length
(“South 0.,” B. 25/7/’18). Twenty-four inches is
considered big up there, The bite is very venomous
to man or beast, and “South C.” is wrong in stating
that the wriggler is harmless to man. A young
woman at Chatsworth, on the Clarence, died two
hours after being bitten by one. As for sea-snakes,
those above the Clarence, particularly about Byron
Bay, are not marked with bands. They are a silvery-
grey color. They have heads exactly like the land
tiger snake. They are very venomous, and will attack
upon the least provocation. Would like to hear
about the short, podgy “heath snake.” This little-
crawler has a long narrow head with a crocodile-like
snout. Has it been found south of the Clarence?
A FOUR-TON CROP.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sometimes I wield the blade in sheds. Sometimes
the axe I swing,
And often down in gravel-pits I make the banjo sing.
I’ve swung the pick on public works, and once I
pushed a pen
(But that was when I led the life pursued by steady
men).
In dungarees and blucher-boots I chase the festive
pound
That buys me ’bacca, beer and beef as I go knockin’
round.
But you can have these capers all for one that comes
on top.
It’s digging out potatoes in a four-ton crop.
Across the field I stride ere yet the soldier-bird’s
a-wing,
I give the butcher-bird salute, his carol in full swing.
The wine of life is in my veins and in my heart a
song—
To Fate and Care I fling a glove as blithe I bowl
along.
The folk who go per motor-car might envy me the
walk
That ends where “up-to-dates” grow thick around a
gleaming fork.
A fig for frost or mountain fog; for rain will
scarcely stop,
When digging out potatoes in a four-ton crop.
I push along the fork by day, and idly speculate
If I shall soak in township beer or jump the rattler
straight.
I own I’d love a month or so in Sydney-by-the-sea,
Or then, again, November time it’s Melbourne town
for me.
So when I get Jim Fahey's cheque—-the last sack
standing up—■
7TI wave my hand to Crookwell side for Melbourne
and the Cup;
And at the pub on Bathurst-road one merry night
I’ll stop
And book again for Easter next a four-ton crop.
N.S.W.

A Little Talk to
Business Men.
“ONLY A COLD,” but a man’s head is not clear for business. He may not be able to grasp
the opportunity that he has waited for so long. His appearance is against him, too. With
swollen face and reddened nose he cannot meet his employer or business friend with that
look of confidence which inspires confidence in return. Socially, he feels himself a
nuisance—almost an outcast. Hardly ill enough to compel him to abandon' his engage-
ments, he moves about, the embodiment of physical distress, and only awakens the feeling
of pity or disgust in his acquaintances.
“ONLY A COLD,” to which a man must not give in, but must run the risk of more serious
ills by braving the weather, no matter how inclement it may be. How many cases of
pneumonia and other serious illnesses, causing the deaths of fathers of families, result
yearly from “ONLY A COLD?”
“ONLY A COLD,” but it becomes localised and inflammatory. Rheumatism has him in its
awful grasp. It goes to the ear, so closely allied to the throat, and becomes a life-long
danger to the man’s life, or affects the eyes, and the suffering is unbearable.
“ONLY A COLD,” but which others catch; it spreads through the family, the office, the
club. There is only one remedy which can be relied upon to stop a cold and is powerful
enough at the same time to arrest the development of much more serious allied diseases.
The sensible and responsible business men of Australia appreciate the value of such a
remedy, and, as a result, hundreds of thousands of bottles of Dr. Sheldon’s New Discovery
yearly pass over the counters of Chemists and Storekeepers of this land, and into the use
of heads of families.
Dr. Sheldon’s New Discovery is found upon the desk at the office, it is carried in the
pocket of his overcoat, it stands on the mantelpiece at home, but wherever it is found the
immunity from coughs and colds, the minimum of danger from bronchitis and pneumonia,
and protection from all pulmonary troubles is also found. Business men and workers from
all parts of this country testify to the grand work done by Dr. Sheldon’s New Discovery,
and gladly proclaim its merits to the world. Price 1/6 i.nd 3/-
Only a Cold!

The Inky Way
“L.E.”: Lord Nortlicliffe, owner of The
Times, Daily Mail, etc., lias long been the
butt of writers like Chesterton, Belloc,
Arnold Bennett and others who are never
tired of making merry at his ‘‘sensa-
tions'’ and literary howlers. Fleet-street is
now laughing at a poem, called "The Old
Volunteer,” that appeared in Tiie Times
over the signature of Uudyard Iyipling.
Next morning Kipling informed the jour-
nal that the verses were not his. The
fatal lines had reached tin* paper from
Brighton, signed “Uudyard Kipling,” and
that seemed sufficient. Kipling, like most
bards, has written some bad verse in his
time; but is it conceivable that any sane
paper could be hoaxed by stuff like the “Old
Volunteer” ! This is the first verse :
I can hear, the bugle calling
And it don’t want me
While the Superannuation-drip
O’ Germany
’S a-fightiiig for the Kaiser in
His Fatherland ;
But our order’s for the young ’uns
O’ the old Brass-Band.
It was the Nortlicliffe gramophone press
that boomed W. M. Hughes, and as its taste
in politicians and poetry is about the same,
“Bellerive,” when published in book-form,
will probably get an enthusiastic review in
The Times Literary Supplement.
Sydney pressmen struck a hurdle which
they bumped and fell over badly during the
first day’s proceedings in connection
with Justice Harvey’s inquiry into the
internment of the Seven Irishmen.
Many letters in Gaelic were read by that
broad old Scotch K.C., Ralston, and the
obstacle that tripped the newspaper men was
the kindly reference to Archbishop Kelly in
which he was described as “that contempti-
ble old slioneen.” The word “shoneen” as
Ralstonised and reprinted was completely
camouflaged. Sydney News had two
attempts, first “leonin” and then “sheonin.
The Sun printed it as “ceonin” ; while the
Daily Telegraph improved on the News
with “leonine.” The Herald knew too little
to use the word at all. but the Melbourne
Argus got the word correct, marked it with
an asterisk and placed a footnote at the
bottom of the column explaining that
“shoneen” is the “Gaelic for truckler . to
authority.'’ What about Gaelic being in-
cluded in the course for the proposed School
of Journalism?
“To”: Ncavs comes of the marriage in
N’Yark of Spencer Krodney (he was
“Brodsky” before the war), the amiable
Jewish youth who was Sydney Sun’s Mel-
bourne representative, llis wife was Esther
Siebel. of Uncle Sam’s Education Depart-
ment. Brodney is now associate-editor of the
Current ’ History Magazine, published by
N’Yark Times, and one of the advisory edi-
tors to the American Labor Wear Book.
“A.RAY.”: I think the Twenty Seconds
Echo— a regimental eight-pager—puts up a
record in more ways than one. It is entirely
composed, printed and published in the
trenches. The plant consists of a small
press and a few other things dug into the
side of the ditch. The difficulties in bring-
ing out this paper fortnightly can better be
imagined than described. We liave a real
fighting editor. C. R. Blatchford. sometime
of the Commonwealth Rubric Service
Journal. The other day lie' was in the
throes of supervising the issue when the
battalion had to do an attack. He exchanged
pen for rifle and bayonet, hopped the bags
that night, and returned to resume compos-
ing the publication the wearer of three
stripes and a Military Medal.
Author John Galsworthy visited Australia
in the long-ago. He was a barrister then,
and went down to the sea in big ships to get
to know their ways, as he intended making
a specialty of Admiralty Bar work. He had
scarcely thought of authorship. At Adel-
aide he met another man who had thought
of it, but had not had anything published.
This was Joseph Conrad. In the Holy City
these two, destined to become beacons in the
ocean of literature, formed a friendship
which endures.
“Pica”: Re “Musketoon’s” inquiry for
lone-hand pressman (B. 18/7/TS). 1 was
editor, leader-writer, comp., machinist, devil,
vendor; in fact the whole staff of a news-
paper, and have seen millionaires waiting
outside the shop for an issue containing the
latest stock quotations received by wireless.
My rag was published weekly on board the
’Murkan liner Philadelphia. There were
only four pages, and well-known authors,
who happened to be aboard, sometimes con-
tributed articles. It had an average circu-
lation of about 300.
“S.T.S.”: “Stargazer’s” verses about the
actor (B. 4/7/’18) whose Hamlet brought
him an engagement as pantomime dame, re-
call an experience. I once wrote practically
the very same thing about a leading man in
melodrama. He was a third-rater, small and
quaint, with a trick of standing with his head
on one side. I suggested, quite innocently,
that he would make a good Widow in “Fun
on the Bristol,” then still a popular piece.
The ungrateful man, being his own manager,
refused me admission to his show the next
time it came along! However,_ Providence
watches over the critic —sometimes. M.\
notice was not due for a day or two, and
meantime the critic of a leading daily came
out with a positively blistering notice ot
the opening night. Then I had only to
write that, being denied admission, I must
accept what other people said. So I quoted
the other fellow's words, which were far
stronger than anything I should have
dreamed of using.
Poet-Laureate Robert Bridges has found
little or no inspiration in the war, although
any number of unofficial and unsalaried
poets have made good use of its heroic deeds
and brilliant episodes. Bridges is a vener-
able octogenarian living a secluded life in
Oxford. He emerged from his retirement
the other day to address a meeting in sup-
port of a Red Cross Library. One class of
sick and wounded soldiers, lie said, needed
only novels and light literature, but another,
composed of young men whose studies had
been suddenly interrupted by the war,
desired to resume their serious mental exer-
cises and intellectual pursuits. Then there
was a third class, comprising _ the large
number of soldiers from Australia, Canada
and the oversea Dominions and colonies.
They were for the most part keen and
practical young men whose reading tastes
lay in the direction of text-books, mostly
on applied science, books of reference and
cyclopaedias. Some relentless humorist must
have been taking a rise out of the aged
laureate.
“P.A.L.” : A scribe from one of the Wel-
lington (M.L.) papers made a horrible 'mis-
take the other day. ‘ Arriving at his office
lie found himself down to interview (say)
a Thibetan visitor of some note. A quarter
of an hour to spare before the interview was
spent in the nearest bar. As he was being
whirled up in the hotel lift the influence of
four three-finger nips brought with it, the
recollection that his wife had abjured him
before leaving home that morning _ that he
was on no account to forget to get his collars
from the laundry. Absent-mindedly he took
out his laundry ticket, presented it and calmly
waited. What the Thibetan visitor said
couldn’t be taken down. No interview
appeared.
A Satchel of Books
In The Theocracy of Jesus Ignatius Singer deals
with a subject that is much too large for the space.
To compress the theory of Christianity into 54
pages is impossible, but he gets in a fair amount.
What he says is mainly to the effect that the
New Testament is a strangely composite document.
It has been said often before; still there are new
ways of saying it. One point is that the Saviour
never claimed to be of the royal House of David.
Possibly He took little' interest in the Jewish legend
to the effect that He was to trace his pedigree from
the »one Emperor of Israel. Possibly He wasn’t
dazzled in the least by the Emperor of Israel or the
fleeting empire either. Anyhow two pedigrees were
made for Him. They didn’t agree and neither of
them had anything to do with the case, for both
ended with Joseph of Nazareth, who wasn’t the
father of the Redeemer, and had no earthly connec-
tion with His pedigree. Jesus Christ bequeathed
to the world a beautiful gospel of peace and love.
Persons more or less unknown, some of whom wrote
so long after the event that they could hardly b?
first-hand witnesses, handed on the teaching garnished
with miracles and wonders. Some time after the
Church was started there arose a heated and bust-
ling man who apparently had never met the Saviour,
and this hasty person proceeded to write nearly one-
third of the New Testament off his own bat. The
Leader was an evangelist; the hot and dusty follower
was a theologian—which made a difference. The
Church suffered from the difficulty that it had to
start with very little Testament to guide it. It
hadn’t the Book of Acts, for that is in the main a
story of wanderings among churches already estab-
lished. It hadn’t Paul’s works, for he wrote copi-
ously to churches that had begun without him. In
fact it is almost certain that, it had nothing at all
except the Contribution of Mark, who seems to be
the earliest writer, and it is hardly probable that
it had even him. When the Church grew up on
its own and the Book on which it is founded
straggled in afterwards by instalments supplied bv
many writers over a very long period of years, con-
flicts were bound to happen, for an old father church
is apt to be cross when dictated to suddenly by a
baby revelation from a new writer. Also discrepan-
cies were bound to happen, and Ignatius Singer
makes no attempt to disguise the fact. (C. W.
Daniel, Ltd., publisher, London.)
A Sporting and Dramatic Career, by A. T. Wat-
son, issued from the house of Macmillan. For many
years Watson has combined dramatic criticism with
racing gossip and reporting in various London papers
—-Referee, The Standard, Punch, Illustrated
Sporting and Dramatic News, etc. —and has made
good in both department,. The Sabbath was always
his busiest day, for he had to write up for Monday’s
press the Saturday race meetings as well as the
new plays usually produced on that evening. Very
few journalists would bo able to stand such an
exacting task, and Watson is certainly entitled to
say this for himself: “If I may humbly claim to
have been fairly successful in my profession, it has
been by the accomplishment of extremely hard work.”
His wide knowledge, authority and thorough compe-
tence arc attested by his being selected to edit the
sporting section of the “Encyclopedia Britanniea.”
Outwitting the linn, by Lieut. Pat O’Brien (Heine-
maim, London). Pat O’Brien was an Irish-American
who learned to fly in the United States, crossed over
into Canada in order to get a chance of hitting the
Germans, performed well in England and France, and,
having been taken prisoner by the enemy, put up a
record of dodges and expedients in escaping. That
record included a jump from a, fairly fast train, and
as many other adventures as would serve a hundred
men of sportive instincts for their natural lives. A
book that turns its own leaves over.
Rough Rhymes of a Padre, by G. A. Studdert
Kennedy (Ilodder and Stoughton, per Cole’s Book
Arcade,:, Sydney). Verse of a kind that a lot of Aus-
tralian readers will appreciate. The author, known
as “Woodbine Willie,” has seen much of the war.
Three Golden Weeks in June, by Jack Eliot (The
Author, Mosman). A booklet of love verse that has a
certain daintiness without much force or skill. The
author has a sympathetic feeling for words, but his
thoughts are as thin as cigarette smoke; and his
“Princess Zephyr” isn’t the sort of person who seems
worth spending a whole three, weeks with.
The Unguarded Hour, by A. W. Marchmont
(Cassell, London). A tissue of yarn that doesn’t
matter —except that in war-time paper might be
considered.
Pros and Cons of the Second Ballot, Preferential
Voting and Proportional Representation, by Thomas
J. Ley, M.L.A., is a capable and interesting essay on
a subject to which people will give more attention
when they haven’t so many other things to do.
(William Brooks and Co., Sydney, publishers.)
RAVAGES OF WAR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In writing-fluid pale and slack
I trace the lines I think.
This war has made all things look black
But ink !

Gregan McMahon’s Repertory Theatre,
which has done much good work without
prospering greatly, has been formally
adopted by the Taits, who, in future, will
finance Repertory productions in Melbourne,
Sydney and elsewhere, retaining McMahon
as general director. The idea is to give
matinee and, where possible, evening per-
formances of the works of Shaw. Gals-
worthy, Ibsen and other intellectuals, the
casts, or the nucleus thereof, being supplied
by Tait artists, whilst the energetic brethren
will also furnish the theatres, scenery arid
staffs. The scheme will give the young firm
the chance of testing and training promising
“SEVEN DAYS’ LEAVE.” AT SYDNEY
OPERA HOUSE.
Captain Allan Wilkie and Colonel Herbert
Leigh, of the Intelligence Department,
discover the Crown Prince’s mistress.
amateurs, whilst providing an outlet for the
suppressed genius of the regular pros, who
feel that their art is not getting its full
opportunity in the ordinary work. The whole
proposition has many points of merit about
it, and it is relieved from any suggestion of
a siieculative enterprise by the fact that the
Taits hope only to gain for themselves the
celebrity of being a theatrical firm willing
to risk losses to supply superior dramatic
entertainment.
“Melb”: A revival of the old-time minstrel
show at the Bijou (Melb.) lately did not last
very long. The first bones-and-tambo show
I can trace here was that of Rainer’s Ethio-
pian Serenaders. Rainer afterwards had for
many years the tremendously lucrative
American Civil War panorama, which
trundled over its rollers until worm to rags.
It ran, even when a far better panorama of
the Franco-German War failed, with Kyrle
Bellew as lecturer in Melbourne, at the
Waxworks. The Backus Minstrels came
from the U.S. to Coppin’s Olympic. The so-
called Christy Minstrels, at the Theatre
Royal, did well, with Brown, Norton, Rain-
ford, Melvyn, Steele, etc. Next were the
minstrels at the Haymarket, with Abecco.
Herberte, Feuillade, etc. After that co. was
Weston and Hussey’s. Then Emerson’s;
then Kelly and Leon’s.
Adelaide Repertory Theatre has had a
heavy loss in the death of Douglas Walsh.
He came back from the war invalided, but
was able to attend one or two recent per-
formances, and his death in the military hos-
pital at Keswick came as a shock. As an
actor, Walsh was an ideal stage-lover, bright
and breezy, but earnest, and could at any
time have obtained a professional engage-
ment if he had cared for it. Another brother
was killed last year.
“W.T.S.” : The Tasmanian Censor Board
is causing no end of trouble to picture show-
men. Why the Speck needs a Board all to
itself, when one exists for the whole of the
Commonwealth, is, of course, best known to
those who’ authorised its formation. The
other day a Burnie showman who had much-
boosted Pauline Frederick in “The Love
That Lives,” received a wire a few hours
before starting to the effect that the Board
desired a special screening of the picture
immediately at Hobart. The unfortunate
exhibitor had to hunt around for an emer-
gency programme, and lost a lot of business
and prestige as a consequence. Similar
notice was given to a showman the other
night at exactly two minutes to eight. An-
other way in which showmen are penalised
is by film exchanges slipping in pictures
other than those contracted for, and conse-
quently unregistered in Tasmania. A num-
ber of displayers have had to appear before
the Court for this reason.
“Melb”: “Arrah-na-Pogue” has been
pleasantly revived at Melb. Princess’s, and
Boticicault’s dialogue and construction shine
amid the crudities. The drama was first
acted here 53 years ago, by Barry Sullivan,
at the Royal. Shaun the Post was among
his best characters, all his variety of Ham-
let, Richard, Charles Surface, Benedick
thrown in. Mrs. Heir appeared as a beauti-
fully exuberant Arrali. Harwood was a
good Feeney, but Rogers excelled him in
Sydney. Greville did the comic valet Win-
terbottom in a scene at the Irish Secretary’s
Office, Dublin, which is cut out nowadays.
After the run of Williamson and Maggie
Moore in London, with “Struck Oil,” the
management wanted to stage “Arrah-na-
Pogue” for them, and this caused a break of
engagement, because Williamson would not
offend Boucicault. It was great self-denial,
for Williamson's Shaun ranked with his
Stofel. Kerry and Rip Van Winkle. Andy
Mack excelled in the pathos and simplicity
of Shaun. Sullivan was most at home in
the manly attributes. These two, I should
think, excelled Boucicault.
“Private Boxer”: Some plays, no matter
what they are called, could get along very
nicely with no title at all. Take two money-
makers now in Sydney bills. None of my
playgoing friends mention “The Masque-
rader” by name, although they have all been
to see it.’ They have carried away memories
of Guy Post’s acting, but as to the title of
the piece, well, it might be “Much Ado
About Nothing,” or “Oh. Chilcote!” Simi-
larly no one seems to know—-or care—that
the Hebraic farce at the Criterion is called
“Business Before Pleasure” (and I’m sure
/ don’t know why). “Potash and Perl-
mutter” every time! Dramatists, I have
always understood, go through a horrible
brain-racking period when it comes to chris-
tening a play ; but it seems to me that their
trouble, after all, is wasted. The title doesn’t
matter much. A success by any other name
would be just as successful.
“Ibsenshaw”: I came across a copy of
Edward Sheldon’s drama, “The Nigger,” in
Melbourne Public Library the other night,
and put in an interesting hour perusing it.
It is well worth reading, as it was well
worth seeing performed; in fact it reads
almost as well as it acts. Most good plays
do. When seeing the production it had
struck me that the realistic Southern dialect
affected by the principals was just “put on ’
by the adaptable mummers intent on giving
us the correct “atmosphere,” but I found by
the printed book that the author has been
careful to write the dialogue as he wants
it spoken, which is. presumably, how
Southerners speak. Thus the white folk in
the play have no choice but to say “heah, (
for “here”; “ovali,” for “over”; “nearah,’
for “nearer” : “fo\” for “for ; “mistah for
“mister”; and so on. That this niggah talk
did not get on Australian nerves says much,
I think, for the all-round artistic handling
of the play by Bates Post and his associates.

“D.”: The manager of the Garrick
Dramatic Co. was named Rollick. He was
giving Ballarat an opportunity of witnessing
great plays by great players, and one of his
special engagements was the celebrated
metropolitan actor, James Cathcart. Rollick
when unfinaucial was profoundly apprecia-
tive, and the greater the strain the more com-
plimentary he grew. ' “Jimmy,”' he would
say. “you are one of the finest actors on
God’s footstool. You have a wonderful
voice, soft as a lute, commanding as a
clarion. For scholarly diction, dignity and
artistic appreciation of the value of a charac-
ter, I put James Cathcart first always.”
Then he would shake Jimmy’s hand, with
deep emotion, and Jimmy hadn’t the heart to
ask for his money. This continued for eight
days, arid on the ninth Cathcart appeared in
the manager’s office, pushing a stout, indig-
nant man before him. “Jimmy,” said Pol-
lick. “glad to see you. By the Lord Harry.
you were fine last night—magnif- ” “All
right, Rollick, all right,” Cathcart responded.
“I can’t ask for a cent. In mercy's name,
try it on my landlord!” And Jimmy fled
leaving Pollick with the unpaid publican.
“Spreader” : A dinkum Australian trio has
found its way on to Sydney streets at last.
There are only three members, all of them
graduates of the Binghi Conservetofium of
Music at La Perouse. The full-blood in
charge tenderly saws the violin while two
half-castes play a gum-leaf apiece placed
between the teeth. “Marching Through
Georgia” never fails to draw a crowd and a
hat sprinkled with small change.
“75”: Bayard Veiller offered “Within the
Law” to nearly every manager in N’Yark
and could not get a taker. At last a shindy-
kit bought it outright for £lOO, and made a
fortune. When it came to disposing of “The
Thirteenth Chair” Veiller got some of his
own back. He is alleged already to have col-
lected £60,000 in royalties, and the knife is
still dropping from the ceiling nightly in
N’Yark (where it has been dropping for 22
months) and in various cities and villages
in England, Australia and elsewhere.
“L.I-1.P."; Some recent writer said that
Bland Holt played “The Ironmaster.”
Wrong. He must have been thinking of
Bland Holt's production of Sims and Pet-
titt's melodrama, “Master and Man,” which
also deals with ironworks. Ohnet's French
play, "Le Maitre de Forges” was first done
in Australia by Marie De Grey in 1884,
under the title of “Lady Clare,” the English
version emanating from the pen of Robert
Buchanan, who first staged it at London
Globe, April 11, 1883. The scene was laid
in England, instead of France, and an Eton
boy and an American were introduced as
comic relief. Subsequently Pinero’s deftly
and delicately-written version entitled, “The
Ironmaster,” was staged in Australia by the
Majeronis, Harry St. Maur and Kyrle
Bellew-Brown Potter. For nearly 20 years,
dating from its production at London St.
James’s in ISS4, “The Ironmaster” was a
strong card in the Kendal repertoire, and it
would have formed one of their attractions, if
they had come to Australia in 1890 or there-
abouts. J. C. Williamson offered them
£6OOO for a 15 weeks’ tour. But there
was too much gold to be picked up in
America, and tin; Kendals never got beyond
’Frisco.
“Snell” : It was Miln. I think, an Ameri-
can ex-parson pro. playing at Melbourne
Opera House, coming down to the footlights
as the distracted Shakspearean king, and
emitting his hoarse roar : “A horse, a horse,
my kingdom for a horse!” From the
stalls a voice with a distinct Yorkshire burr,
loud enough to be heard in Bourke-street:
“I ve got a little mare I’ll let you have dirt
cheap!” The Royal was the scene of an-
other interjection that broke up the house.
Handsome George Rignold was giving ’em
melodrama, and in one of the hero’s most
effective moments a child started to cry in
the gallery. George lifted his voice to beat
down all opposition. The infant went one
better. George raised him. The babe re-
sponded gallantly. George stopped a breath-
ing space, arid then mother cut in: “Perhaps
if you make less noise. Mr. Rignold, I may
get Willie to sleep !” But the bull-roarer of
them all was Gus Glover. Gus was appear-
ing at the old “Aleck” in “The Golden
Farmer, or the Last Crime,” or words to
that effect, and the management had com-
missioned him to roar his darnedest in a
scene that called for some clamor. Gus
went out to roar. He roared himself to a
standstill, only to be greeted by a piping,
massive voice from aloft. "Speak up, Gus.
We can’t hear a word up here."
From London Tit Bits :
Madame Melba, who has just celebrated her 75th
birthday, possesses the secret of perennial youth.
And few, not knowing the facts, would guess that
over half a century has elapsed since her voice first
charmed an audience. Her debut was made at the
Yew York Academy of Music .when she was only
sixteen. She is said to have earned well over a
million sterling.
Melba’s debut was in 1887, at the Theatre
Monnaie, Brussels, as Gilda, in Rigoletto.
“II.” : Comparatively few playwrights are
alike eligible arid of military age. One of
them is Arthur Wimperis, who lately came
before a London tribunal as an applicant
foi* three months’
exemption. He is
under contract to
complete three
pieces before the
end of the year,
and if one of them
was not ready in
August a certain
theatre, its com-
pany and staff
would suffer
severely. The
chairman of the
Military Board told
him that theatres
were only a minor
and secondary con-
sideration in World
war-time, but that
he would tie ex-
empted for six
months. Wimperis
replied : “That will
be quite satisfac-
tory. I have no
wish to avoid mili-
tary service." He
is now 43, and
“The ’Nigger,'” at Syd-
ney Palace.
Gay Bates Post as
Governor Morrow.
is classed in Grade
1. He served in the South African war, gain-

ALL ONE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I loved a circus beauty then,
And I was barely one-and-twenty.
She chose me out of many men ;
Her ripened charms were good and plenty.
I did not marry Myra, though.
Her temper promised endless woe.
I ran, for peace and freedom aiming,
And left my Myra lion-taming.
I loved a little, lithe soubrette—
Her repertoire was quite extensive —
She left me very much in debt;
My Josephine was too expensive.
And then a dancer in the play
I loved a twelve-month and a day.
I left her (’twas a wish of mother’s),
For Loo admired too many others.
Five more I loved. From all I fled—
They were too gay, or else too doleful.
A small, pale, milliner I wed,
A simple creature, soft and soulful.
Though to my fitting fate resigned
It grieves me to the heart to find
The one I yielded to included
All those I thought 1 had eluded !
VICTORIA.
BILLY T.

The profit for the past half-year is given less
£lOO written off the Ballarat freehold and
£lOO off furniture. Gross profits, £5566,
show an improvement of over £7OO on the
average of the previous four terms and about
£1.500 on the figures of five years ago. But
expenses have also climbed. This time last
year the directors announced that as reserves
had reached £20,000. which was equal to the
paid-up capital, they proposed to make no
further additions to the fund, blit would allow
undivided profits to accumulate, and from
time to time declare bonuses and make calls
until the shares were paid up another ss.
This would bring them to the paid-up limit
of 255., the remaining 255. per share being
held as a reserve liability to protect clients.
The undivided profits now amount to over
£2OOO, which would enable the directors to
pay a 10 p.c. bonus and call up 2s. per share.
It is a very comfortable business and is ex-
panding steadily. Trusts in hand now total
£2,494,516, compared with £2.287,282 as at
June, 1917, and £1,712,250 five years ago.
Of the co.’s own funds no less than £37,041
is in public securities, while £1740 is in
cash, and £4476 in freeholds and furniture.
The investments alone probably contribute
about 6J p.c. of the dividend paid, so com-
missions have to provide only about p.c.
on the capital to maintain the present aver-
age dividend of 11 p.c.
* * *
“Henry Horsecollar’s” reflections :
A mining town, if it has nothing save mining to
depend upon, ought to be on wheels. To build a
permanent city round an industry that is here to-day
and gone to-morrow is bad enough, and sometimes
it is even worse, for the industry may be an
unusually plausible wild-cat, so that it was really
gone yesterday instead of to-morrow —in fact, it was
always gone, because it never came. People in the
Transvaal are thinking over this problem. The Boer
land, which made so much history, is the greatest
gold country in the world —in fact, it is about 40
per cent, of the world in this respect, and is equal
to the Commonwealth five times over. But it is a
poor sort of place, apart from its minerals,-and its
miscellaneous resources are like hairs on a bald hrad—
few and far apart. There are dazzling mines ; also
there are very many big solid mines that don’t dazzle,
but which, by reason of a huge output and much
cheap nigger labor, have paid well enough. Then the
war rushed up the cost of machinery and stores and
food ; and when the food of a numerous five-shilling
nigger goes up half a crown it is a much bigger
problem per cent, than if the food of a rather rare
£lO white man went up 10s. The further increases
in costs which are now in view will put many mines,
which had been living on hope and a profit that was
down to a few pence per ton, on the wrong side of
the ledger, and as no one can tell how long the situa-
tion may last or how much worse it may become, a
huge suspension is expected. The idea of using up
ore for a profit of about nothing per ton or less when
it might yield 10s. per ton after the war has no
attractions, for the ore doesn’t grow a fresh crop next
year like potatoes. A lot of small towns therefore
are likely to suspend. They will be still there and
still quite right when circumstances allow the owners
to come back —except, of course, for the ravages of
weather and insects, and the consequences of neglect,
and the disappearance of doors and fittings and sun-
dries which travellers, black and white, may use for
firewood, and the investigations of wild unclean ani-
mals when the doors are gone, and the smell of the
animals, and fires, and floods, and the occasional
disappearance of a house which somebody has put on
a waggon and removed bodily. Contents which the
owners were unable to remove will, to a great extent,
evaporate, as mi watched contents do, and the said
owners will have to pay for house-i’oom elsewhere
during the interregnum, and find work if they can,
which by reason of the rush they probably can’t.
If these towns were on wheels they might shift
bodily to pastures new, but they were foolishly tied
down. A proposal is made that the Government
should subsidise gold at so much an ounce, or ore at
so much a ton, to prevent worse things happening, for
if a town becomes- null and void a frightful amount
of paper value —credit, goodwill, land values, mort-
gages, advances on real and personal security, savings
bank and building-society investments and the like —■
become thin air. There is something- weird about
the idea of subsidising gold—not the prospecting of
new fields but the daily work of old well-established
ones. The logic of the thing is plain enough in the
present upside-down circumstances, but it sounds
like an old-age pension for capitalists.
* * * *
“Oof Bird - ’: Platinum is now quoted in
London at £2O per troy ounce, as against
255. per ounce 40 years ago. By ISBO it
had a value of 355. per ounce, by 1889 it
was selling at 555.. and a year later, princi-
pally on account of its use in photography, it
had buyers at 80s. Later on it was priced
at £5 ss„ and up till 1914 it could still be
got at £lO an ounce. Then the war came, and
its use in the manufacture of acids which
form the base of high explosives, its appli-
cation to magnetos and its utility at the
“contact points” of engines for aeroplanes,
hydroplanes, etc., have forced the price up to
the present figure and caused the Imperial
Government to make the metal a complete
Government monopoly in Great Britain. The
great bulk of the world’s supply comes from
Russia, and most of the balance is furnished
by the Republic of Columbia. A little has
been produced in N. 8. Wales and Tas-
mania, and a little less in Canada.
“D.F.” : New buyers have come into the
market for osmiridium, and the price has
soared to £27 an ounce—more than double
the best price before the war. Consequently
the hunt for it in the mountain country
beyond Waratah (Tas.) is very keen, and a
new field is being opened up near Mount
Stewart.
