The town-planners, holding pow-wow at
Brisbane, had a breeze over the Australian
capital. Fitzgerald wanted the work pro-
ceeded with at all costs, because the plan of
it was admired by town-planners all over the
world. Which is quite likely. The difficulty
is that Canberra, being without coal, iron,
water-power or a harbor and not even being
an oasis in a desert, has nothing special to
live upon, and even if it was as universally
admired as the ruins of Karnak or Luxor or
Louis XlV.’s palace at Versailles, this coun-
try can’t afford to keep it up. Some other
delegates declared that Australia wouldn’t
require a capital for another 100 years,
which was not worthy of a town-planner.
Australia wants a dozen new cities, with the
people as sole ground landlord, and.each good
enough to be a capital—not gilded pau-
pers, but places that can support themselves
like any other ordinary cities by reason of
their manufacturing or commercial or other
possibilities, and pay a big dividend in
unearned increment. These are. the sort of
cities that would have grown before this if
the railway policy of the three congestion
States hadn’t been gerrymandered from
Sydney and Melbourne and Adelaide in the
exclusive interests of Sydney and Melbourne
and Adelaide private landlords and private
bagmen. And if one of these self-supporting
cities was made a capital, then the extra
unearned increment which the political busi-
ness brought along would be so much the
more for the Treasury. The barren logicians
who say that Australia can’t afford a capital
might just as well advocate the destruction
of Brisbane, Sydney, Melbourne, Hobart,
Adelaide, Perth. Darwin and Port Moresby
on the ground that the Commonwealth can’t
afford eight capitals. They start with the
assumption that a capital, unless it is a
State capita], must be a pensioner, because
Canberra is a pensioner, and was always in-
tended to be one. It is just because Aus-
tralia is rather hard up just now that a new
metropolis of the right kind is badly wanted.
If the Labor leaders could spare a moment
from their efforts to get the baby-murderers’
Pacific Islands restored to them they might,
have a look at the almost-forgotten Public
Land Ownership plank in their platform.
This question is the good old plank.
Some newspapers lately have been pub-
lishing statements which reflect in a
measure on the war-work of the Y.M.C.A.
The gravamen of these appear to consist of
allegations that the canteens charge for
eatables and drinkables. Simple replies to
this are : (1) That these canteens were estab-
lished at the request of the army authori-
ties; (2) that charges are made compulsory
by army regulation, are inescapable and
are necessary; and (3) that if it sets out to
provision the army gratis the Y.M.C.A.
would be bankrupt in a week. The main
work of the institution, for which the funds
collected in Australia and elsewhere are ex-
pended, is not in connection with these can-
teens. A vast amount of gratuitous service
and comfort is by it afforded in the war
zone. It may be worth mentioning in a
casual way that any profits made out of
canteens by the Y.M.C.A., in consequence
of having to observe the scale of charges at
the neighboring regimental canteen, <*o to
the free services fund. In carrying gratui-
tous service and comforts to the men in the
trenches and to the wounded alone in France
£2500 is now being spent every pionth. The
institution operates closer to the line than
any other, and nearly everywhere the
advanced dressing-stations have Y.M.C.A.
orderlies close at hand. The work of the
Association has received the warmest com-
mendation of General Mon ash. The Bul-
letin reckons that is good enough.
To judge by a suggestion made by
Lieut.-Col. Jones, Vic. Inspector-General
of the Insane, at a recent meeting of the Aus-
College of Dentistry, the State’s men-
tal patients are getting a poor deal in regard
to their teeth. Jones’s mild proposition is
that inmates of the asylums would be greatly
benefited by “proper dental attention and
supervision, as is the case in England.” The
average “pauper lunatic,” put away at Yarra
Bend because he believes (say) that all
crockery was made to be smashed on sight, is
deprived of most things making for his com-
fort and well-being. Mostly he wears no
boots, because he might mistake an attendant
for a soup-tureen and try to crack him with
his handiest weapon. He is not allowed a
knife and fork, for obvious reasons. He is
shaved, at fairly long intervals, with a dull
razor. There is no brush and comb on his
dressing-table, as a rule, and no dressing-
table to put them on, anyway. Further, he
is in many cases debilitated by his mental
ailment.. Such a lot of things, indeed, mili-
tate against his ease that it seems extraordi-
narily callous that he should not have
adequate dental attention. The spectacle of
a . drooling, chilly-footed, unshaven wretch,
with, broken, insanitary teeth and perhaps an
unmitigated toothache, is more suggestive of
a German internment-camp than of a Govern-
ment hospital in a British country.
“Ritasun”: A lot of unostentatious patri-
otism is being practised by country traders
I know; yet many of them have cheap
sneers aimed at them because their names don’t
shine on published lists of donations. It is
no secret that many fine fellows found en-
listment practically the only way out of
their financial quicksands. They left owing '
scores to . the local traders—and the traders
mostly wished them luck and hoped silently
for a settlement some day. I know of many
cases where, on seeing the debtor’s name
in a casualty list, the creditor has sent out
a receipted invoice, marked “Paid.” Many
traders in these parts have lessened the
worries of many a worried grass-widow by
not even waiting for bad news. The account
duly receipted has been posted on the
first of the month as a cheerful surprise.
Round our way the scores wiped out have
run into thousands.
S. M. Hekald grows more addicted to
scoring bull’s-eyes or something. The latest
shot (9/8/T8) :
A further offensive on the West by Germany may
suggest that she fears the effect on her people of
such an acknowledgment of defeat.
Of course, everybody knows that an army
only makes an offensive as a proclamation
that it is in the soup. That is why iVh
became offensive on the Marne.

IMPORTANT.
Look to Your Teeth.
There are no teeth like those furnished by
Nature. For service, comfort and good
health artificial teeth cannot be com-
pared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to pre-
serve their teeth as long as possible by-
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap
and water are useful, but are somewhat
ineffectual. Rough powders, such as cuttle-
fish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.
EUDENTA TOOTH PASTE is scientifi-
cally prepared to clean the teeth, sweeten
the breath, harden the gums and disinfect
the mouth (thus preventing decay).
EUDENTA is therefore regarded as ’‘the
perfection of dental luxuries.” 1/- tube every-
where. Post free. 1/3. Free sample posted.

• Sydney, August 19, 1918.
My Dear Arini, —
Homes Day will be as successful as will-
ing hands can make it. The Voluntary
Workers’ Association has dated September
18 for the rally, and the money raked in will
be spent in the purchase of materials for
soldiers’ homes. The Voluntary Workers do
the rest. It’s clearly up to you to help
partially-disabled men back from the Front
—-men who are unable to do heavy out-door
toil—and widows and orphans of those
who sleep for ever under alien skies. A big
meeting in the Town Hall this (Monday)
afternoon, with Governor-General Ferguson
on the platform, knocked the preliminary
details into shape.
Though some of tin l comforts funds are
still wrestling with pyramids of Christmas
cakes, the private parcels have gone. On
Thursday last the early birds fluttered round
the G-P.O. and dropped their offerings into
that department of hopes and uncertainties.
Next morning the real crush began, and the
largest John Hop in town couldn’t have
turned aside two slips of girls, staggering
under heavy loads (they had missed a train,
and that made them cut the time to the last
second), who fell upon the A.I.F. parcels
office and triumphantly unloaded the cargo.
A moment later the G.P.O. clock struck tin;
closing hour—boom !
Some time ago, Hath more, the pleasant
gabled home at Neutral Bay for convalescent
soldiers, was given for that purpose to the
Bed Cross. The generous donor was Miss
Davey. For months some of the rooms have
been occupied, and there are garden-seats in
use amongst the flower-beds where once
upon a time editor Heney, of the Herald,
used to grow fine roses. This pigeon-cote
jiacl its official blessing from the Minim
Ferguson, lady last week.
Amidst a chorus of congratulations from
Premier Holman’s personal friends over his
record term of office, an opal and diamond
necklet was handed to Mrs. Holman.' The
presentation was made in Cootamuudra, the
electorate that hubby represents in the
Assembly.
The Amateur Patriotic Musical and
Dramatic Society gave a performance of
“The Cingalee,” at the Royal on Saturday
night, to raise more money to purchase com-
forts for our men at the Front. The 4th and
56tli Battalions will split the proceeds with
the Ist Australian Machine Gunners. Lady
Helen Ferguson was compressed into a box,
with Lady Doris Blackwood and a commit-
tee dame or two. In the long list of ama-
teur players, P. G. Smith (in the mixed
attire of Chambudy Ram) and Mrs. Roy
Buckland (in the part of Peggy Sabine)
kept the coffee-colored plot together.
Both were good enough to have saved
a much worse show from extinction. In
a front seat of the stalls, Mrs. Alex, Gor-
don, whose beautiful voice once thrilled Syd-
ney in the guise of Nanoya, the Cingalese
girl, watched dainty Eugenie Moreau softly
coo in the same part to a very wooden tea-
planter. The scenery deserved unqualified
praise.
Three weeks divided between the city and
points of interest in the country will be a
steady round of sight-seeing for the coming
French Mission- The sudden death in U.S.
of the leader, M. Metin, gives a black edge to
the gaiety. In spite of that tragic happen-
ing, the N. S. Wales Government will spread
itself to welcome General I’au and his col-
leagues. There’s to be an evening reception
at Government House and a garden-party as
the sub-regal share in the welcome.
The vice-regal Davidsons are making the
O. Falkiners’ homestead in the Riverina their
headquarters, darting hither and thither to
open country shows. They’re due back at
Macquarie-street in a day or two.
A kindly soul, best known to the poor in
her husband’s parish of Redfern, has gone
over the shadowy border. After a very short
illness, Mrs. Boyce, wife of Archdeacon
Boyce, died on Saturday.
Jessie MacKenzie Mills, only surviving
daughter - (a bright young sister was killed
by a fall from her horse a few years ago) of
Dr. and Mr|, Arthur Mills, murmured the
usual responses in St. Stephen's the other
evening. The happy man was Hugh Eccles-
ton Campbell, of the Merino Downs clan.
Roma. Bananaland. Bridesmaidens Lena
Campbell. Enid Edgley and Madge Sly had
Colin Campbell, Lieut. Harden, M.C., and
Pat Morrisey to escort them down the aisle.
The cake was cut at the Australia.
Mrs. Jack Hindhaugli, of Victoria, is in
Sydney on a health trip. Her three young
children are with her. By way of beguiling
many anxious hours —her husband has been
four years on active service—she has just
written a Gippsland yarn, “The Bridle
Path.” Jack Hindhaugh, who left with the
first Australian lot of Light Horse for the
scrimmage, was wounded in Gallipoli; but
he is back in the field again. He was one of
a number of Australian officers who had the
luck to recuperate in Italy, spending some
time in the Villa Medici, Florence. At pre-
sent he’s O.C. of a cyclist battalion in
France. Like all Australians who know the
bush he loves a horse, and his present job
doesn’t prevent his keeping one. “I call her
Mrs. Pankhurst,” he writes, “because she’s
rather excitable.”
Another good Australian has paid the toll.
Lance-Corporal Hugh Mcßride, M.M., was
killed in action on French soil on July 10.
He,and two brothers, James and John, were
noted athletes. They came from Uralla
(N.S.W.), linked up with the Field Engi-
neers in 1915, and embarked together. Hugh
got the M.M. last year for conspicuous gal-
lantry in action. Jim Wears the same deco-
ration, and with his brother John still belts
the enemy in France.
Sparks of kindness from German chaplains
to relatives of dead British or Australian
soldiers are rare, but they do occasionally
happen. The widow of poor Derek Hudson
(whose fine voice used to decorate the
J.C.W. Musical Comedy Co.) has had a line
of consolation from a German padre, one
Frederick Find, who saw the passing of the
badly-wounded Derek, then a lieut. in the
Flying Corps.
Lt.-Colonel Chapman, V.D- (Volunteer
Decoration, given after ”0 years’ commis-
sioned service in that branch of the forces),
saw lighting in the Gallipoli mSlec, and.
earlier, lent a hand in tlx; New Guinea ex-
cursion to fly the Australian flag above the
hated Hun rag. When in civilian togs lie’s
in the Crown Law Department. Recently he
had a wedding—his own. The bride 'was
Lily, youngest hope of Mrs. John Taylor, of
Bellevue Hill. John Ferguson was overseer
at the ceremony in St. Stephen’s kirk.
Though the size of the audience at the
Hospice concert in the Town Hall was dis-
appointing. the programme was excellent and
of generous length. Fred Collier and Elsv
1 reweek were off the usual beat in Elgar’s
“A Voice in the Desert.” Filippini recalled
memories of the defunct Gonsalez Opera Co.
in an air from “Un Ballo,” and in the ever-
green Prologue from “Pagliacci.” Winifred
Burston, Ella Caspers, Staell and a Hair-
bows’ orchestra from a Parramatta convent
school gave of their best. The lion. secs.
:Glair were Mrs. William Macleod
and Mrs. Leahy, and the lion, treas. were
Mrs. John Tooliey and Mrs. W. J. Adams.
John Browulow was lion, general handyman.
The Australian Historical Sooietv, which
has recently grown the prefix “Royal,” was
founded in Sydney
in 1901. A few years
later Captain James
H. Watson became
a member. That was
in 1904. Two years
afterwards he was
elected vice-president.
Since 191j5 he has
been lion, research
secretary, and if
more local Writers
on early New South
Wales would consult
him before lightly
plunging into anci-
ent chronology, there
would be fewer mis-
takes. For James
Watson has a mem-
ory ivhich seems to
photograph on invisible plates apparently un-
important events of long ago, and opposite
each event is embedded in a corresponding
brain-cell the date on which it happened.
Historical Australian research is now his
life study, and no student of the once frac-
tious infant settlement on the shores of
Sydney Cove is more eager and courteous
than he in passing on information. James
Watson is a Scot, who was born by chance
in England—near Plymouth. Amongst his
forefathers appear the names of Thomson.
Lindsay, and the Earls of Balcarres and
Crawford. His grandmother was a daughter
of that Colonel Lindsay, M.P., brother of
Lady Anne Barnard, who wrote the famous
song, “Auld Robin Gray.”
Before adopting Australia as his country,
and Sydney as his home town, this vigorous
Scot had a roving life and many adventures.
Destined for the navy, the death of Ills
brother in a Royal Naval Hospital in Hong
Kong made his parents decide that James
should hug the shore.,. He was sent to
Newcastle-on-Tyue to got a business train-
ing. In a long life (he remembers the
excitement when the first train arrived in
Plymouth in 1848) he has learnt nearly
everything worth knowing about ships
and their ways, and in 1869 signed
articles on a 900-ton vessel in order to work
his passage from Australia to Bombay, and
there entered the service of the Great
Indian Peninsula Railway Co. A serious
accident laid him aside for awhile. After
that. England, and back to Melbourne. In
Geelong (in 1875) he found the girl who has
tethered him to Australia. She was the
daughter of a local architect, William
Grundy. An only son, Lieut. William R.
Watson, is at the Front. Photo by May
Moore.
Born in Sydney, and doesn’t want to leave
it except to help settle the Hun, violinist
IV. J. Coad, has just persuaded the military
authorities to accept
him for active ser-
vice. Coad is a
young man who
was turned down
some time ago, but
he has now suc-
ceeded in making
himself physically
fit enough to pass
the doctor. His
brilliant technique
as a violinist is the
result of long and
patient study. In Syd-
ney he first handled
a bow under the
tuition of Mrs. 11. H.
Rice, after which
he drifted to Europe
to pursue his art.
For a short time he was with Seveik, in
Vienna, and then with the great teacher,
Cesar Thomson, in Brussels. This musical
giant, by the way. before whom strong men
often trembled, was a child to his wife and
a playmate to a tiny grand-daughter. After
picking up the crumbs of fiddlers’ wisdom
for two years in the Thomson studio, the
Australian returned home. While lie has
most of his time teaching, he has given the
public each year a series of recitals of high
artistic value. Audiences were given a big
repertoire of violin music, and the young
person received an education in harmony
that she will not get quite so often now that
this fine performer is getting measured for
khaki. He goes into camp early next month.
Photo from May Moore.
From Brisbane:—
The Show is upon us, and nightly homeless wan-
derers with hands full of luggage may be seen
wandering from hotel to hash-tower imploring to be*
taken in. The man from the bush will regard Bris-
bane as an immense city full of empty beds, and his
experience each year doesn’t seem to teach him
differently.
Driver E, 11. Holmes and his brother, both of the
Field Artillery, have won M.M'.’s. The two were in
charge of the rations for the 42nd Battalion, near
Zonnebeke, last October, and they carried up supplies
through violent shell-fire, returning twice through
a heavy barrage. A few days later they undertook
voluntarily the work of keeping the forward batteries
supplied with rations. Often they arrived on foot,
laden with bundles, owing to the fire being so heavy
that it was impossible to get pack animals through.
B. T. McKay, who has been carrying out the dual
duties of manager of the Small Arms Factory at
Lithgow and general manager for the engineering
firm of Walkers Ltd., Maryborough (Q.), has relin-
quished the latter appointment, and will in future
confine his activities to the Lithgow works.
A letter came to hand last mail from a
gunner with the Howitzer lot in France.
He and his mates got their Christmas pre-
sents from the 2nd D.A.C. Depot, Sydney, in
May. He says: “Your letter, which was in
the box that came to me, was dated August
4. 1917. Yet we only got our welcome gifts
this month (May).” He doesn’t know that
the depot women were hard at it all one
night to get their Christmas stuff to time on
the wharves the following* morning (August
5). Then the strike held up the shipping,
and in the dislocation that followed, soldiers
on the Western Front got nothing from their
comforts funds for months.
This year the American-Australian League
of Help (Mrs. Brittain on the box seat)
drives its workers, friends and bowing
acquaintances towards one goal—the fund
for Australian prisoners of war in enemy
countries. All the money goes to the Red Cross
for that purpose only. The Tait firm, by
way of giving the fund a push, organised a
matinee in the early part of the week. Guy
Bates Post and Mrs. Post assisted.
An Adelaide mam. William Carey Page,
recently chose a Roseville (N.S.W.) "girl for
his mate. She was Erica Noble, of the gar-
den suburb. The Rev. William Page, the
’groom’s father, came over from the grape
State to help the knot-tying.
Over here, from Adelaide, for good if he
can find a niche, poet Leon Gellert, whose
“Songs of a Campaign” count amongst the
five literary productions of the war. The
poet fought at Gallipoli.
George Crowley, of the City Mutual (Syd-
ney), took a party to Cairns the other day.
Ilis daughter, a recent bride, and her aunt
Cormack met the voyagers at Townsville, and
added their Saratogas to those on the way to
Barron Falls and Kuranda.
Mrs. Helen Barton, the lady who lias come
all the way from wee bit Scotland, ye ken. to
administer a blue-ribbon caution to this con-
tinent, has been heavily charged with the
teapot by farewelling friends. She’s off home,
having finished her curtain-lectures to unre-
generate bibbers hereabouts. Mrs. Joe
Cook, with a new handle to her name, pre-
sided at a party in the Town Hall.
For weeks past the rumble of a lorry and
its pull-up in front of a smart suburban resi-
dence, heralded the arrival of the “Old Clo’
Girls.” St. Vincent’s (Darlinghurst) de-
tachment of Voluntary Aids were after their
friends’ glad rags, boots and any saleable
article they could lay their hands on. Their
jumble-sale on Saturday afternoon went as
if a forced draught was under it. Buyers
surged into the City Mission Hall (lent for
the purpose), past Dr. Maxwell Allan and
other professional men who assisted as
doorkeepers. One hundred pounds fell into
the till in two hours. The money goes
towards the upkeep of the soldiers’ motor-
car at Rose Hall.
Mrs. Forrest got the following tribute from
France the other day :
Sevres, Seine et Oise,
•June, 1918.
My dear Colleague,
I have very much admired your noble and
charming verse, published in The Bulletin, of Syd-
ney. I should be very grateful to you if you have
the kindness to send mo a copy of your excellent
book, “The Bachelor’s Wife,” or another book of
verse. As a. French man of letters I should be
delighted of this h0n0r....
Cordially yours , of La Soviet o des gens de
lettres, France.”
Spring fashions focus in the most elusive
gossamer draperies that ever lady spider
spun to charm her victims. These silks,
soft as an Australian summer cloud, and
muslins fine enough to pull through a ring,
are for the Fortunate Few and the extrava-
gant. A wealth of decorative floral garlands
trail over the latest cotton voile, and the
luxurious georgette is the best in summer
flummery.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.
“M.L.E.”: llow much money is paid
annually by the N. S. Wales Railway Com-
missioners ns .compensation to consignees
for damaged goods? A friend of mine had
a case of fruit despatched to a suburban
station. On inquiring for it he was told
that it hadn’t arrived. Twice a day for
a week he received the same answer. At
last, he looked round himself and discovered
his case of fruit rotten. Compensation paid.
7s. 6d. A parrot sent me was inquired for
the day it arrived. Not there, I was told.
For the next three days I was still after it.
A bored clerk at last handed me the cage
with the dead bird in it. I’m afraid my
thanks overwhelmed him.
Australian rules of Supply and Demand: —
When there is no rain there is a shortage of meat.
When the rain arrives there is a further shortage.
. After the rain disappears the shortage is worse than
ever.
After the dryness transpires the only thing to do is
to wait for wet to restore the situation.
Subsequent to the situation being- restored it is
important not to interefere with it.
And when Heaven interferes pray for it to
have more sense.

Melbourne, August 19, 1918.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Pursey Cliirnside’s son (he is named after
bis dad) is due back on six months’ fur-
lough. Young Pursey is a lieutenant in the
sth Hussars. Dad and mother are in Eng-
land, but they will probably come rushing
home as soon as they can get passports.
Soldier-poet Vance Palmer has been
snatched from his final leave by influenza
and interned in the Base Hospital for ttvo
or three days. His unit is about to leave
for the Front, and he will make a big effort
to board the transport with Ids mates.
A Melbourne nurse, after three months
in India, has been made Night Matron of
the 44th British General Hospital at Deo-
lali. The place holds about 3000 patients,
mostly convalescents. In her night rounds
she has to climb several hills and jump
numerous ditches, as the blocks of buildings
are all some distance apart. She takes a
hurricane-lamp and a stout stick, and has a
native orderly with her. Snakes in the dark
are one of the few pests for which she has
to look out. It is quite an undertaking for
one little Australian woman and quite a
big honor. The Australian nurses there are
well looked after—excellent food and the
use of a car whenever they wish to drive or
go shopping. Every second day they have a
half-day off duty. Their bungalows are newly-
built and very comfortable and clean.
This nurse was 'drafted from Caulfield at
about eight hours’ notice, Avent to Bombay
Hospital as a sister, and then was as sud-
denly drafted to Deolali to her big job. The
doctors all speak in praise of the Australian-
trained nurses.
A small group of Australian V.A.D.’s on
active service have clung together from the
beginning of things. The adventurers are
Fisher (Hobart), Douglas (Launceston),
Swallow and Round (Vic.), Tlall and Ben-
nett (S.A.). They signed on together for
service abroad, and after putting in a spell
at a Manchester (Eng.) Hospital Avith
brooms and dusters crossed to France.
Bombs drove them from Boulogne and they
Avent to Marseilles, where they hoav are—or
were at latest neAvs. They Avrite home vivid
accounts of their good treatment.
Capt. Geoff. Grice, of the James Grice
clan, did not get an M.C., but a 8.E.0. He
is engaged to Muriel, the youngest Tom
Atherton sister.
The four Englishmen who lifted the Davis
Cup at Melbourne from Norman Brookes
and co. are scattered in various places
according to their war jobs. Irishman Parke
is married and a ma jor. He . has been
Avounded several times, but is still going
strong. Gordon Lowe, Avho Avandered round
Africa, Australia and India, after the out-
break of Avar, is back in England doing
a staff-officer’s job. C. P. Dixon, after doing
patriotic banking work for some time, has
broken doAvn in health and is in a private
hospital. A. E. Beamish is a lieutenant in
connection with the mechanical transport
corps, working round the Boulogne district,
and renews acquaintance now and then with
Australian friends.
Airman Alan Paterson, of Armadale, just
back in Melbourne on furlough from France,
did his share in the face of considerable
early discouragement. Before the war be-
gan he had a terrible smash-up with a motor-
bike, and came out of the plaster-of-paris
with a Avarped leg. When the call to arms
came, Paterson went into hospital, had the
leg broken again and re-set, came out normal
and set off for the recruiting-office. His
progress into the air Avas rapid. Brother
Alec is also a soldier.
Rev. Henry Potter, of St. Paul’s, Geelong,
has taken up a rifle and is off with the next
reinforcements.
The swagger Patriotic Tea Roomers (all
noble volunteers) are going on like a flock
of white cockatoos at sunset because an
officer, who should have known better, left
a shilling tip for the waitress who served
him. But it might have been worse; he
might have offered her London’s traditional
tuppence.
For the Henry Harper party at South
Yarra two big rooms Avere cleared of furni-
ture and stalls put in. The Langs, Gran-
thams, Clarkes and Stephens all helped in
the patriotic work.- The Dunbar Hoopers,
the Robbs, the Robertsons, General Mon-
ash’s wife, Mrs. Sargood and Mrs. Rutter
Clarke Avere in the midst of the spenders.
Dr. Spratt, of Tasmania, and his wife, are
in Melbourne with their just-returned
daughter and the new son-in-law, Lieut.-
Celonel Smith, who has been invalided home.
Lionel Robinson, an Australian who has
settled in England, has sent out a handsome
present to his old school. By his gift
Scotch gains a series of scholarships which
are so arranged that a clever youth may get
right through all the forms of the college.
The donor is a partner in Clarke and Robin-
son.
The Macedon people are putting in a
double line of oaks along the Mile-
road, to make an avenue of honor.
Premier Lawson planted the first tree last
week in honor of Lieutenant Arthur Gru-
mont, the first Macedonian to enlist. The
Henry Harpers. Bawdons, Hodges and
Oswald Symes were in the gardening group.
Mrs. Dyer (Louise Smith) has gone to
Coombe Cottage to convalesce after a severe
illness. Melba picked her up at the hospital
and motored her off to take the air at Cold-
stream. Mrs. Dyer's doctor brother, a 1914
veteran, is home on furlough and is hover-
ing anxiously round the patient.
Mrs. Webb is back in Melbourne after a
five months’ trip to Japan and China. “Not
too much of China,” she says warningly, and
adds that the country was too disturbed for
travel. Japan, on the contrary, is peacefully
booming. “One huge, rushing workshop
from end to end,” says the voyager. “But
living was expensive—30s. a day at the
hotels.”
Late grazier George Robert (“Mahonga”)
Jackson, of Urangeline station. Albury, left
over £149,000. His only child Beverley is
heiress to £20,000. Mrs. Jackson is a hand-
some blonde and was a Miss Rand. Dr.
Julian Smith, our clever surgeon, gets £3OOO
under the will.
Mrs. Major James, who has been doing
much war-work in London, brought back a
soldier son this week. Frediswyde Hunter-
Watts (Mrs. Allan Wilkie), who has been
testing the footlights in Boston, was also a
passenger by the oversea liner.
The publishing house of Lothian had a
party on Saturday evening. The invitations
mentioned dancing, singing, games and com-
petitions, and there was an orchestra to saw
off music. The party was in a long room
above the publishing house, four flights up,
and a Green Pierrette to work the lift.
The woolly Ledinghams have returned to
Melbourne after staying with the Bruce
Smiths at their Bowral place and admiring
the convalescent home for returned soldiers
run by Miss Smith.
Genthelp Conant has been on the sick-list.
He is convalescing at the Agar Wynnes’
Western District squattage.
Paul Kitz, son of the old original Kitz, of
cider fame, died in a private hospital the
other day. No doubt the death of his son
Paul at the Avar helped hasten liis end.
He was a jolly fellow of late years, Commo-
dore of the Brighton Yacht Club and a
beaming light on the local bowling-green. In
his younger days the girls admired him for
his musical taste and his singing. He
married one of the well-endowed daughters
of the late J. B. Watson, the Bendigo
millionaire. She ultimately Avent to England
to live.
A new owner has been found for Norwood,
the tremendous pile of bricks, mortar and
fal-lals on the foreshore at Brighton, built
by that patriarchal money-lender, the late
Mark Moss. He spent 40,000 guineas on
the place. Afterwards the late Robert
White, Melbourne’s “chain store” bootmaker,
had it. Son-in-laiv McCaughan lived in it
awhile. The latest oAvner, who got it for a
trifle of £lO,OOO, shorn of most of its sur-
rounding acres, is not inappropriately named
Riches.
A Melbourne bank manager gives this as
a true story of feminine genius for finance.
A lady depositor Avith a not very large
current account at any time Avas notified
that she had overdrawn to the extent of
£2 3s. 6d. She wrote the manager; “Sorry
to know’ my account has been overdrawn,
quite a mistake on my part; but am en-
closing my cheque on your bank for £2 3s. 6d.
and hoffe that will settle the matter.”
Adelaide’s revelations
Influenza has half the populace by the throat and
the rest by the nose. Even the Governor, who
should be immune from the ills of the groundlings,
had to call off all engagements for this \Vbek. Her
Ex. took his place at the meeting of the District
Trained Nursing Society on AVednesday evening, and
was at the Lady Victoria Buxton Girls’ Club annual
meeting on the same afternoon.
A good many congratulations will go to Lieut.
G. Forest Davies on winning the M.C. By birth
and enlistment he is a Victorian, but his mother was
a sister of the late Dr. Gardner, and that wins
him a host of friends in this State.
Port Pirie is, happily, to have a 10-acre children’s
playground fenced off and equipped with wading
pools, sand heaps, shelter sheds and anything else
that is needed. Public subscription is providing
the ground and 2000 or so workers from the smelters
are to put in Saturday constructing it, ready for
ita opening on Sunday.
The latest money-raising dodge is feminine foot-
ball. The first match was played before an appreci-
ative crowd at the Jubilee Oval on Saturday. The
feminine employees (or some of them) of two
drapery firms were the players, and they are so
fired by their success that they are already threaten-
ing a return disturbance.
In his latest report of the King Edward statue,
Bertram Mackennal speaks of the figure of South
Australia, which is to be on the front of the pedestal,
as one of the best he has yet done. The statue itself,
and the figures of Justice and Peace, which are to
be on the sides of the pedestal, have been cast in
bronze; but the sculptor warns the committee that
there is no hope of finishing the group till the war
is over, and that even when the foundries are free
it will take some time to complete.
Miss A. M. Martin, who founded the girls’ school
that bore her name long after' she severed her con-
nection with it, died in Home a few days ago, aged
76.
Abe Shannon has added his name to the list of
Australian Red Cross Commissioners and has crossed
the Channel to begin his work in France.
After less than two days’ illness, Charles Reinecke
died at his home Vt Kensington Parle on Thursday.
He leaves a widow and two daughters. His only
son was killed in France.
A surprising item of news during tlie past
week from the war was the intimation that
Major L. F. Giblin had taken to himself a
wife. Giblin was the only member of the
Speck’s Parliament to enlist, and was re-
garded as an incorrigible bachelor. The lady
was a Miss Burton, an English girl. She
visited Tasmania about three years ago.
Stella Power is ending her first year as a
star with the Wedding March. Willie
O’Rourke, a large young man, who comes
from Sydney, will have the part of bride-
groom. He was visiting America during the
singer’s visit. The marriage will be before
Christmas.
Miss Maud Harvie is an organising econo-
mist, so £B5 is the total of all working
expenses of her
Troop Horse Fund
(the Purple Cross)
since the work was
initiated in August,
1914, up to the end
of the last finan-
cial year. In that
time £3685 in cash
has been sent to the
Front, besides rugs
and veterinary in-
struments, costing
£373, and over
50,000 horse band-
ages. Miss Harvie,
after slie began the
good work here,
found that an en-
thusiastic horse-
lover, Miss Lind-af-
Hageby, had gone to France from Norway
to do the same kind of work, so the two
joined forces under the name of the Purple
Cross Service. Now it supports field-hospi-
tals, bases, vets, and nurses. They have horse
ambulances for transport, and the friend of
man is looked after specially on his boat
trips to' the various fronts. Miss Harvie
visited all the Australian capitals to initiate
the good work; but the thick end of it has
been done in Victoria. Mina Moore has
caught the busy woman in one of her few
leisure moments.
Pretty Mavis MacMillan, of the Gippsland
clan, is having a delightful trousseau de-
signed for her nuptials with squatter Edgar.
Laurie Kennedy, the ’cellist, goes to the
altar this week with Dorothy Mcßride, the
little pianiste.
The stork called at the Norman Bayles
Toorak humpy and there is now an heir.
Mrs. Norman was formerly Mrs. W. Bal-
four Neill, Melbourne's smartest and most
popular widow.
On the first night of “The Thirteenth
Chair,” at Melb. Royal, the critics were
handed notes from the managefuent request-
ing that they should not disclose who killed
Cock Robin or how the deed was done.
Weeks before the first night I had been told
by a dozen friends, unconnected with the
theatre: “You know r , so-and-so happens”;
which shows the impossibility of keeping
the “secret” of a play once it has been
staged. Still, the managerial request was
not so ingenuous as might appear, for, by
thus gagging the critics, comment was
stifled on two of the play’s most palpable
weaknesses. We are asked to believe that,
during a spiritualistic seance in a million-
aire’s home, while a medium and 12 others
are sitting in a circle in absolute darkness
with hands joined, one of the guests rises
from his chair, stabs another guest, not sit-
ting near him, and gets rid of the knife by
throwing it upward and causing it to stick
in the ceiling of the lofty room. This is a
bit steep, the more so in view of the
fact that the murderer, when he joined
the seance, had no idea that he would find
it necessary to kill anyone. Apparently it
was a little idiosyncrasy of his always to
carry, a carving-knife, about 9in. long, in the
pocket of his swallow-tail when he went
to a party. Of course there is nothing in-
herently impossible in this, but such things
are not done by the Very Best People.
Having recovered from wounds received
in France and obtained his discharge Fred-
erick Worlock, the tall, handsome, young
man who was the Caliph when Oscar Asche
did “Kismet” in these parts, has transferred
his talents to light opera. Ilis performance
in “Violette” is favorably noticed by 1.,
critcs.
Melba was among the big audience at
“The Thirteenth Chair” opening on Satur-
day night at the Royal. She sat in a box
in a low-cut evening gown and wrapped in
a pale sea-blue mantle trimmed with chin-
chilla fur. At the proper moment she rose
and flung flowers at Margaret Wycherly.
Willie Kelly, the songster’s guest, was nearly
swept away and trampled on; and son
George Armstrong and bis wife, the latter
in a cloudy blue gown and long white gloves,
had to dodge the flying bouquets. The party
afterwards motored the actress off to
Coombe Cottage for the week-end.
Governor Stanley escorted a house-party
to the stalls of the Playhouse on Saturday
night to see the Alliance Franqoise give its
usual little play with full stage settings.
An act of a Molifere comedy was set in Paris
in a frilly period, the men being dressed in
velvets with lace hanging over their boots,
while the women had hour-glass waists and
immense silk skirts. Florence Herd was a
particularly fetching lackey in breeks and
swallow-tails of pale yellow.
In “The Thirteenth Chair” Lizette Parkes,
who plays the distressed heroine, gets fine
effects with back A’iews. She weeps copi-
ously on tables, chairs or sympathetic
bosoms, and shows each time a very dimpled
neck and shoulders rising out of a baby-
bodice of amber-lined white net. Tien Ilogue
wears a dress that is a shade too long—
black, weighted down with heavy silver
braid. Nancye Stewart’s address is in pink,
cream and amber.
Mary Marlowe, who played the heroine
in the Bailey-Grant production of “On Our
Selection,” and who published her first book,
“Kangaroos in Ivingsland,” a few months
ago, has now followed it with a second
novel, “The Women Who Wait.”
Mary Worth, who played the cute little
blackmailer in the original Australian pro-
duction of “Within the Law.” has just fin-
ished a season with Ethel Barrymore, arid
has been booked by the U.S. War Depart-
ment as leading she of a co. that is to visit
the Sammies in their training-camps and
endeavor to reconcile them to not having left
for France,
Elwyn Harvey cables joyously that she has
annexed an engagement at San Francisco.
A Yankee visitor with a Buster Brown
top-dressing has disconcerted local feminin-
ity by revealing that her hair is not really
cut short, but is arranged to look like it.
Many Melbourne maidens (and some
matrons, who ought to know better), on
seeing some screen beauties with their manes
looking like that of the comic-picture young-
ster, rushed and had their hair scissored.
The young Australian who left here as
Enid Bennett and has since acquired a repu-
tation as a film star and the name of Mrs.
Fred Niblo, hopes to return in a few months
with hubby in tow.

DO THE JOB
PROPERLY
The whole crop of young horses, many of
them well off two years old, in spite of the regula-
tion birthday, are in training at Rand wick and on
other Sydney courses. In a few weeks they will be
reckoned ready for the fray, and those of them that
are precocious enough will go in pursuit of the rich
stakes hung out for juvenile racing. The allegation
that these proceedings are incidental to the improve-
ment of the thoroughbred horse long ago degenerated
into a gag. No one believes that any good comes of
it, nor are there many people who would deny that
two-year-old racing, as it is now known, leads to a
most gross abuse of horseflesh. It is more than time
that Governments took a hand in this business,
for much as this page would dislike to see clubs
like the A.J.C. and V.R.G. made subject to Red-tape
in framing programmes, it can hardly be denied that
in regard to young horses they fail, and fail badly,
to do wliat they ought to do. The big stakes for two-
year-olds are monstrously wrong. Racing two-year-
olds in the Spring is wrong. Under no circumstances
should they be raced before the Autumn, and then
the number of times they are allowed to start should
be limited with a rigid hand. It would be far
better for the horses, the owners, for racing and for
the thoroughbred nag as a general institution if
two-year-old racing was knocked right out. To the
fillies the existing system is ruinous in a double
sense. Season after season scores of them are raced
off their immature legs, patched up to catch a
suburban stake or country stake in the following
season, and then hurried off to service. ’ Lunacy is
a mild term of description for such happenings. Yet
they happen with the direct sanction and encourage-
ment of men who know the proceedings to be inde-
fensible and are, in the main, genuinely anxious
ro see the development of the Australian horse.
What is it that blinds and paralyses them ?
* n * *
A few of the nags that are in the list for
Ra,.a\ijci-. i„ter on were given outings at Warwick
farm (Sydney) on Saturday. Only one of them did
anything to take a note about. This was Poitrina.
Carrying lOst. 101 b., she gave nothing a look-in for
her division of the Pace Welter, six furlongs.
Amadis, the opening favorite for the Epsom, slumped
in the betting after Moorefield a fortnight before, and
slumped some more after Saturday. He started with
list. 71b. at 6 to 4 in the second division, for which
it would need some indulgence to class the field as
second-rate. At no stage did he look like a winner,
and was never nearer than fourth. But, of course,
there is time for this horse yet—and for the gentle
punter to wait a bit. Kenbaquahair was well beaten
by Mount Alf over a mile and a quarter. Tharia had
no chance with Rualma at the end of a mile. Lingle
was still further back. But these three were short
of Condition, and not expected to do much better.
* * * *
The general body of punters had a day
out at, Warwick Farm (Sydney) on Saturday. Of
seven races four were won by the actual favorite and
one by the next best. In the other two races the
winner was backed in some places for a substantial
wad. And to make matters worse for Israel, the
stable that provided the main double helped itself for
all there was. Ike wandered home fortified by the
reflection that there will be something doing soon at
Randwick.
*• *■ * *
“Trot”: It can bo taken for granted that
it was something Amadis had shown on, the track
last year which caused owner Stirton to exchange
loOOgns. for him, and hope of the horse showing it
again which led to him being backed so early ns he
was for the Epsom, the Metrop. and the Caulfield
Cup. Well, he may show it. but in the judgment of
the handicapper for Roseliill, it doesn't look as if
he will. Amadis is handicapped for the Epsom at
Bst., 21b. above Promised Land and 41b. above Nor-
bury and Satin Bird. For the Auburn Handicap,
seven furlongs, on Saturday, he is given 7st. 101 b.,
being placed well below these prads and reckoned of
less account than Cerise Colors and Persian Cat.
This handicap is a tip that for the Epsom the chance
of Amadis is vague and hardly worth mentioning.
* * * *
Mehi King, who after running second in
Wolaroi’s Victoria Derby and at the same meeting
third to Patrobas and Wolaroi in St. George’s Stakes,
let some big backers down with a. thud on several
occasions, died the other day at Rosehill (Sydney).
For a long time there was something mysterious about
this horse, or at least about the way he failed when
everything looked in his favor. It- is now said that
lie suffered from some disease of the bones. He broke
bis back during a gallop, and subsequent struggles
fractured more of his anatomy. East season he did
fairly well, and won City Tatt.’s Cup in good style.
* * * *
There is a whole heap of evidence about
the sort of punters who patronise the tote in N. S.
Wales in the circumstance that during the financial
year to June 30 £306,300 was machined on Svdney’s
four pony courses and £152,100 on the four pro-
prietary courses addicted to horse. And as long as
60, 20, 20 goes, it will be the same.
* * * *
A couple of Derby colts ran in the Three-
Year-Old Handicap at Flemington last Saturday,
with unsatisfactory results. King's Treasure was
going well enough when he broke a small blood-
vessel and was pulled up. This was not a good omen
for the future, anyhow. Tragic Star, on the other
hand, made all the running to the distance, but could
finish only third, so the suspicion that this handsome
black colt lacks stamina appears to be well grounded.
Private King, son of Son of the Marsh, had 261 b.
the best of the weights with Tragic Star, to be
sure, but when he tackled the top-weight he passed
him as though he were no class.
Ethiopian, the unpopular winner at Caul-
field on the previous Saturday, gave further evidence
of his quality at the V.R.C. meeting. With 9st. lib. on
his back he ran the six furlongs in lmin. IJlsees., and
won brilliantly at the finish. All going Well, Ethi-
opian should be one of the best in next Caulfield
Cup.
* * * *
John Hill, the veteran trainer of Glenelg
(S.A.), has led in his last winner. He had been
leading them in close on half a century. Though
only 69 at the end, and he kept going till last year,
when, with Petracia, which Quinn sent over to him,
he added the City Handicap, of the Adelaide Racing-
Club, to his collection of results. Somehow he had
always missed the “City” till then, but for every-
thing else local he had trained successfully over and
over again. Also he had wins in Victoria, includ-
ing a Melbourne Cup with Auraria, and two Austra-
lian Cups. Hill’s only boast was that he had
never asked boy or man to do anything but ride to
win. His memory of past events was a mine of
good things, as, for instance, that Adelaide Cup of
1881, in which “we ran Ist, 2nd, 3rd and 4th,”
with the astonishingly brilliant string, Totalisator,
The Assyrian, Venus and First Water. The old
man stuck to the old ways right to the end. For
the newer training methods he had no time.
* * * *
The St. Leger mare Maude, dam of St.
Elmn, this year’s M.L. G. N. Steeplechase winner, who
comes from Ouida’s family, was principal brood
matron at Douglas’s Te Mahonga stud, Hawkes Bav.
At least three of the dozen foals she threw were
nearly first-class on the flat —Mahutonga, Ivopu and
Mangaroa. The latter was a big raking mare who
galloped after the style of Mountain King, and trainer
Jim Munn had a few of the big Cups in his mind
with her. But she upset these calculations by break-
ing up. St. Elmn’s sire, St. Ambrose, died after a
very brief stud career. He was an imported St.
Simon horse.
“Hawkesbury” : The alleged docline of the
Australian racehorse, shown by the increasing wins of
the imported prad, is probably owing to ignorant
breeding and the influence of the idea that the
quality of the parents decides that of the offspring.
Hence the fancy prices paid for sires in all kinds
of live stock. • The laws of heredity in this matter
are no longer in doubt. In pure blood-stock, further
improvement can only be expected by line-breeding
—that is, by close selective breeding within the
chosen strain. Wide mating—even with pure bloods
—gives some of the results of the hybrid. An occa-
sional hybrid is equal or superior to the parents;
but the offspring are regularly inferior to the
grandparents. Breeding skill is shown by mating
closely related stock, so as ta§ preserve as much of
the good points and as few of the . bad as possible.
All other. forms of breeding for high quality are a
gamble with the odds against you. This theory has
been confirmed by the breeders of sheep, cattle and
poultry. The assumed advantage of the strange stud
sire—except for the first generation—is a delusion.
Given a good strain, stick; to it, use brains in
mating, and the second, third and later generations
give the reward. In horse-breeding this means
time; and a process so slow has found no long-
maintained support locally. If the imported nag
was barred, we should, under our favoring conditions,
evolve the finest horses in the world. The island
of Jersey, by barring imported cattle for hundreds
of years, has evolved her unequalled Strawberry.
“8.T.”: The old jock tells me, “The softest
thing I ever had was when that bloke Colder come
outer the AVest, with his hat V boots full iv raw
gold, ’n’ got plungin’ like a ’obbled ’orse in a pond.
He was the Mug. The Jubilee Plunger was a tight-
wad aside this one. Everybody got at him like
crows at a dead cow, V every bloke that reached
his way got somethin’. The Two-of-Diamonds crowd
sold him Crosscut. Now, if ever there was a dog of
a horse foaled it was Crosscut. He ran twice, like
a creation beater, ’n’ then he wouldn’t run no more.
The swine, he wouldn’t even limp. They sold him
that hideful iv old iron for about a thousan’, ’n’
Blister trained him, ’n’ drew all that was cornin’ to
him twice over. Crosscut was entered for a big
event, ’n’ I’m offered a thick bit to ride him.
That’s all righto, I ain’t pertickler. I’d ride
a whale in Melbourne Cup if you’d shake three
tenners at me, but I know what everyone else knows
but Colder, that this ’orse iv his ain’t got no more
chance of winnin’ than a roast pig would have,
carryin’ a 101 b. penalty. I dunno whether some
leg-pullers had got at Colder to save Crosscut for
the Cup, or what was doin’, but, strike me stiff, the
owner comes to me ten minutes. afore the race, ’n’
sez he aside and very sly: ‘Ned, I don’t want me
’orse to win this. Pull him.’ Pull 'im! Gorstruth,
pull Crosscut! ’N’ so.’elp me, ten men ’n’ a cat if
he doesn’t shove 50 quid into me fist. I rode Cross-
cut all I knew all the way, ’n’ he lost by a street.
Up comes Colder to me again. ‘Bv , you done
that well, lad!’ sez he, ’n’ may I fall down dead
if he didn’t slip me another fifty.”
* * * *
“II.”: The war quickly did Australian
cricket no good. Most of the young players dropped
into khaki and the game was left to the old ’uns.
The Board of Control and State Cricket Association
quickly caught the infection of <J< ing very little, with
the result that nowadays there’s certainly not
much more attraction in it than a dogfight. Still
there is no reason why these controlling bodies should
leave the dry bones to rot indefinitely, and a couple
of suggestions worth consideration are: (1) Reintro-
duction of the premiership competition and (2) in-
clusion of G.P.S. and colleges in the “A” grade
cricket. When the war-clouds roll away, the burden
of cricket will fall on the shoulders of Youth, and it
is no use imagining that present methods of instruct-
ing a few schoolboys do more than touch the edge
of the problem.

“Stumps” : “T.0.1.’5” statement regarding
N.S.AV. v. Victoria (B. l/8/’18) in ’56 being the first
intercolonial fixture, is historically wrong. Tasmania
and Victoria broke the ice at Launceston on February 11
and 12,’51. Tasmania won by three wickets, scoring
104 and 34 for seven wickets, against Victoria’s 82
and 57: Other early flashes in Australian cricket
were Jim Phillips’s first “intercolonial” century (115)
for Vic. against Tasmania in ’69 ; and Maryborough’s
0 against Undaunted at Paddington (Sydney) in
’76.
“Slip”: The help given by S. M. J. Woods
(B. 8/8/’18) to Percy McDonnell’s ’BB Australian
XI will not be forgotten. It was a team of 13
players and manager C. W. Beal; but the offer of
help from the stalwart Australian (then at Cam-
bridge) was gladly taken in half a dozen matches.
Sammy is among the few Australians who have
gathered all the wickets in an innings in first-class
cricket. Others were Bill Howell, 10 for 29, Aus-
tralia v. Surrey, ’B9; Albert Trott, 10 for 42,
Middlesex v. Somerset, 1900; George Gillen, 10
for 66, Australia v. Combined XI, at Sydney, ’B4 ;
S. M. J. Woods, 10 for 69, Cambridge v. Thornton’s
XI, ’9O.

Stan Gregory, lately awarded the Military
Cross for ill-will to the Turk, is a son of Albert
Gregory, and nephew of Dave, Edward, Charlie and
Arthur. The young man was addicted to the family
failing, and lifted a bat for Waverley in Sydney
premiership cricket.
Western Suburbs wiped out Glebe (12-81
in N. S. Wales Rugby League semi-final on Saturday
mainly through the kicking of A. M. McPherson.
Both sides scored two tries, but McPherson lifted the
wet, muddy air-bag through the uprights three times
against Glebe’s one successful kick. This raised Mac’s
number of goals to 42. Wests v. Newtown appear-
ance in the final on the coming week-end will be the
only time either club has achieved so much distinc-
tion.
* * * *
There was a crowd of nearly 30,000 at
Melb. Cricket Ground last Saturday to see the
Collingwood and Saints teams kick for the semi-final
—double as large as the attendance at the corres-
ponding kickball match last year, and three-fourths
of what it was in the year before the war.

St. Pat’s (Ballarat) claims a world’s record
in annexing the football premiership amongst local
colleges without having a point scored against them.
In their last victory over the Ballarat Grammar the
ruin of the opposition was shown in the final figures:
St. Pat’s 37 goals 17 behinds; Grammar, 0 goals
0 behinds. That, however, is not their highest
achievement in goal-kicking. A few years back
against Grenville the Pats finished with thd formid-
able tally of 44 goals 43 behinds.

Captain A. M. Robertson, killed in Prance
the other day, after getting the M.C., enlisted with
his brother, Lieut. A. D. Robertson, at Melbourne, in
February, ’l5. The latter is home wounded. The
Robertsons were a fine, hefty pair, somewhere about
2J,vds. each. At one time they rowed with Scotch
College and later with the Mercantile Club. Dad
is a parson at North Melbourne.
* * * *
Veteran Q. L. Deloitte. has again been
made president of the N. S. Wales Rowing Associa-
tion. He had a blade in the Fours which won
at Anniversary Regatta at Sydney in ’62.
For the Victorian Motor-cycle 24-hours
reliability proceedings there was a muster of 33
side-car specialists and 12 soloists. The route for
the small-powered machine had a mileage of 376 and
for the high-powered contraption 430 —through
Geelong, Warrnambool, Terang, Ballarat, Castle-
liiaine and Kyneton to the starting-point. The roads
were horrible and the riders had a heart-breaking
trip. But the spirit of invention survived well. One
flier arrived on time with a petrol pipe joint sealed
up with chewing-gum. Sixteen of the competitors
finished, but hopes of getting an absolute winner in
one pop evaporated as six of them (side-car men) arrived
without loss of any points. These were Yott, Taylor,
Parsons, McCall, Wright and Shillinglaw. There
will be an elimination contest later on. Meanwhile
the Barnett Glass Tyre people are remarking that five
of the six were carried on their goods, and 10 of
the 16 finishers were Barnett Glass patrons.

Oil the form shown in their recent billiard-
match in Adelaide, there is really very little to
choose between Clark McConnachie, the Maorilander,
and the English professional, Williams. The English-
man allowed a start of 3000 in 18.000 and nearly
wiped it out before the journey had gone' half-way.
The man from down south, however, is evidently
like some horses —goes better for hearing something
at his heels. When the scores were nearly equal,
he drew right away again, and finally ran out when
the scratch man was 16,872. He made the best
break of the fortnight, 343, and it seems more likely
that lie was dead out of form, than that Williams
was altogether “above himself,” in the first few days
of play.
* * * *
Havilah Uren, a brother of champion Tom,
who now wears khaki, had an engagement with Fred
Fitzsimmons, a rising exponent of bash, at Sydney
Hippodrome on Saturday. It lasted only as long as
the Raiser reckoned the war would persist. In the
first round judgment went against Fitz for hitting in
a breakaway, and Uren scored one for a miss. So they
will have to arrange it all over again, and Tommy’s
brother be lucky if he doesn’t miss scoring any.
A new code of boxing rules has been
issued by the U.S.A. War Department’s Commission
on Training Camp Activities. These emphasise the
importance of fast, aggressive punching, and place
before the camp instructors and referees the fact
that the object of training is to develop a fighting-
army. Under these rules the defensive fighter scores
nothing unless he mixes his defensiveness with ginger
—which is what ought to happen everywhere. Mere
defensive boxing is a weariness and dreariness to look
at, and wholly futile. Apparently these rules are
going to count in hash outside the camps in Yankee-
land, and it would not be much of a calamity if
some of their spirit penetrated to Australia.
* * * *
Jack Dempsey’s annihilation of Pulton in
one punch at Newark (LLS.A.) the other day may
be partly accounted for by what happened a little
earlier. After permission for the clash had been
granted the State Governor made a few remarks.
The points of these were (1) that he intended to
take a personal interest in the proceedings, (2) that
if anything happened off the level or in any way
calculated to bring the State into disrepute he would
dissolve the Boxing Commission, and despite the law
prohibit any public scrap in future. So it was
up to someone to bit.

“M.P.A.”: At the beginning of the war
I mentioned in these columns that wrestler George
Hackenschmidt was interned in Germany as an
enemy subject. A man named Pyke, who recently
arrived in England after three and a half years at
Ruhleben, says he met Hackenschmidt in Berlin and
got the shock of his life. Pyke says “Hack.” is a
victim of underfeeding, and if he went on a stage
to-day would never be recognised. When Pvke saw
him the good-natured Russian giant had shrunk from
laek of food as an old man shrinks with a#e.
Hackenschmidt is, of course, a very German name,
and means strong-smith, but the real name of the
colossus is Georges Bruen. Looking at a photo of
my oid pal taken at the very height of his fame he
looks a Russian of the Russians, though years before
he visited Australia he became a naturalised Britisher.
Th ® big. chap used to say that he owed his wealth
and positmn in the athletic world to his popularitv
ill England, so he hastened to make himself as near
English as possible. He was evidently blocked in
Germany while on a visit to his friend Jacob Koek,
the Graeco-Roman champion of Germany, and one-
time the Continental top-notcher in that style. Kock
wrestled as a side-line. He was a straw-hat manu-
facturer in business in a suburb of Berlin. Followers
oi strong men and wrestlers in Australia were greatlv
impressed with Hack, as a grappler, but there are
very tew who really knew what wonderful feats of
strength, skill and speed he was capable of. At the
time he threw Buttan Singh around like a doormat
he could have thrown every wrestler in Australia,
one up, one _ down. I missed a lot when I turned
down a genuine offer to tour the world with Hack.;
but I wouldn’t swap tucker with him to-day.
* * * *
“Chev”: Had a few days lately with the
deep-sea fishermen of the North Coast (N.S.W.)
snaring the succulent snapper, and incidentally strik-
,,uP acquaintance with many other inhabitants
of the Pacific. We exploited many of the reefs
from Moreton south to Ballina. A fine, healthy
game, but not exactly one for pleasure, and a bit
more than strenuous on squally days. These
men go out on business. While I was content witli
three hooks on a line Lou and Tom only stopped
with eight and ten —and they used to produce half
a dozen fish at a haul. The sharks are a fearful
pest. _ Time after time a snapper would be hauled
up with the tailpiece chopped off as clean as if
severed by a knife; many time the line would be
cut and tackle lost. The favorite bait with the
snapper-fishers is porpoise. Shark meat is good ; so,
too, is kingfish. Best of all is octopus. But a
school of snapper will take anything you like to
show them, coming at it like fowl after corn. Variety
is a strong feature of deep-sea fishing. Here is
the tally of an average day’s catch: 1 shark (which
got away with 5 hooks and sinker), 1 groper (landed
with the gaffing-hook), 1 kingfish, 3 teraglin, half
dozen or so parrot-fish, 1 barracouta (a ferocious
giant which jumped clean out of the water), 6 sea
perch (of the big eye), 1 sergeant-baker, 1 big
fish none of us were acquainted with, but were told
was a “Samson-fish,” 2 sea-eels or snakes (striped
brown, slimy and most repulsive), X octopus, 12
brilliant red rock-cod, arid 225 snapper. Oh, yes; we
had enough gutting and cleaning to do that night.
“Trist” : After the flood of Dec.-Jan. last
the waters of the Mclntyre, Barwon and Namoi rivers
teemed with fish. Good hauls were easily made,
the cod in particular being very fine specimens.
But during the past six weeks great mortality has
been noticeable amongst the fish, and the extra-
ordinary thing is that this is chiefly between Mun-
gindi and Goondivvindi (N.S.W.). The river has
been at summer level all that time, with a fair
stream of water flowing. Some local naturalists
allege that intense cold has caused the trouble. One
leather-faced gent, with 40 years’ continuous know-
ledge of local conditions, affirms that the slaying is
done by a marauder varying in length from three to
15 inches, of a blood-red colqj', and travelling in
schools. The same reliable authority says that a similar
phenomenon occurred in the Namoi waters after the
flood of ’93. Can any fishologist put my mind at
rest on this matter, and if the trouble is caused
by this invader what is the best means of strafing
him?

“Surat”: The treatise on guns by “Q.”
(B. 8/8/’18) would be amusing if it wasn’t so
darned idiotic. Let us consider same to date.
Armed with a second-hand gaspipe this mighty
hunter credited himself with the execution of a
hare at 104 yards. This being questioned and
recent tests of guns mentioned, lie blithers to the
effect that a 50-guinea gun contains £4O worth of
decoration, and quotes a gun of his father’s, attribut-
ing its wonderful capabilities to an unorthodox
loading of cartridges. The purchase of a single-
barrel weapon proclaims him a novice. Yet he has
the hide to assert that £lO to £l5 represents
perfection in guns. His remarks about “Nimrod”
and ejecting locks are equally absurd, as “Nimrod”
distinctly stated that a cheap gun, while it lasted,
was as effective as an expensive one. It seems to
me that most of these “game frighteners” spend all
their time talking and. backing down instead of
shooting something.

“T. 0.1.”: The Diamond Jubilee of Mel-
bourne Wesley Church has just been cel-
ebrated. The first minister was Rev. D. J.
Draper, who was drowned when the London
went down in January, 1866, with G. V.
Brooke and other well-known Melbourne
people aboard. This church has another
romance attached to it. The first Methodist
chapel in Melbourne was erected by J. J.
Pears on an allotment owned by him on
the N.W. corner of Swanston-street and
Flinders-lane. The little bethel later became
the kitchen of Tom Monahan’s public-house.
When the “connection,” as the Methodists
call themselves, sought another site, a Lands-
office official drew attention to the half-acre
on the N.W. corner of Collins and Queen
streets, which was secured for £BO. To that
corner a sad story attaches. At the first
land sale conducted by Surveyor Robert
Hoddle, on June 1, 1837, the corner was
purchased hy one Bowerman, _of Sydney.
He paid the usual 10 p.c. deposit on the lot
and set sail for Port Jackson. The little
ship in which he travelled was lost with
all hands. Some years later Bowerman’s
son got an official appointment in Queens-
land, and falling foul of his Chief, Under-
secretary Manning, clouted him on the head
with a tomahawk. The violent party was
sentenced to life imprisonment, and a special
Act of Parliament was passed giving Man-
ning a large pension, on the ground that he
could not live six months. He beat the
estimate by over 40 years! Bowerman’s
wife stuck to him and got him released after
ten years’ imprisonment, but she died on the
morning of his release without seeing him.
Bowerman wandered aimlessly for some
years, and wound up by suiciding in Sydney
Domain. The Methodists commenced build-
ing on the “Bowerman corner” in 1840, but
only half a church was put up. Many years
afterwards the corner was sold to the Bank
of Australasia for £40,000. The church
which has been celebrating and three others
| were erected out of this sum.

mn
A dry hacking cough is usually
caused by an irritation of the
mucous membrane of the throat and
bronchial tubes. Chamberlain’s
Cough Remedy will allay this irri-
tation and remove the cause.
When the cough is dry, loud and
sonorous it indicates a cold. Take a

Dike many other dangerous maladies diarrhoea often comes on unexpectedly and frequently
before the patient is aware of it some serious disorder hns developed. During the warm
weather attacks of this kind are very frequent and often so swift in their results that life is in danger before the physician
can be summoned.
Those who have the interests of their family at heart keep a bottle of Chamberlain’s Colic and Diarrhoea Remedy on
hand for immediate use in cases of this kind. It is without doubt the best medicine prepared for diarrhoea, dysentery and
other disorders of the bowels. If given at the first unusual loosenessof the bowels the attack may be avoided but even in
advanced stages it is a quick and most effective remedy.
Never leave home without a bottle of Chamberlain’s Colic and Diarrhoea Remedy in your luggage. The excitement
incidental to travelling and change of water and food often bring on diarrhoea, colic and pains, in the stomach for which
speedy relief must be obtained and Chamberlain’s Colic and Diarrhoea Remedy can always be relited upon to do this.

Legal Notices.
DE ROBERT KERRIGAN, late of
IV Cairns, in the State of Queensland,
deceased, miner, but latterly a member
of the A.I.F.
Notice is hereby given that all persons
claiming to be next of kin of the abovenamed
Robert Kerrigan, late of Cairns, in the State
of Queensland, deceased, who died on or
about, the Fifteenth day of February, 1917,
a.d., in Asia Minor, are hereby required to
forward proof of their relationship to the
Public Curator, Brisbane, on or before the
Twentieth day of October, 1918. And that
after the expiration of the last-mentioned
date the Public Curator will proceed to dis-
tribute the assets of the said deceased
amongst the parties entitled thereto, having
regard only to the claims of which he shall
then have had notice.
Dated this Eighth day of August, 1918.
R. .T. S. BARNETT,
Official Solicitor to the Public Curator,
Brisbane.
Government Notices.
COMMONWEALTH OF AUSTRALIA.
Department of Defence,
Melbourne, 3rd August, 1918.
Vacancy for Chemist in Charge of
Laboratory, Small Arms Factory.
APPLICATIONS are invited from per-
sons qualified for appointment as
Chemist, Small Arms Factory, Litligow, New
South Wales.
Pay £336 per annum inclusive of all allow-
ances except travelling, under Common-
wealth Government Factory Regulations,
Class D, Professional Division.
Applicants must have had a sound training
in Analytical Chemistry and Physics and a
good knowledge of the testing of materials
and metallography of steels; and should hold
a University Degree or Diploma from a Tech-
nical College.
Experience in industrial undertakings, if
any, should be fully stated.
Applications should be forwarded addressed
to the Secretary, Department of Defence,
Victoria Barracks, Melbourne, to reach him
not later than the 30tli August, 1918. and
should be accompanied by certified copies of
not more than three (3) recent testimonials
and two personal references. The date and
place of birth and nationality of applicants
must also be shown. First preference will
be given to returned soldiers, all things being
equal.
T. TRUMBLE,
Secretary, Department of Defence.
Department of the Navy,
Melbourne, 9th August, 1918.

A Tonic for Efficiency.
Debility is not only distressing to the indi-
vidual, but it is an economic loss.
If your blood is thin and your head aches,
if your digestion is weak and your nerves
unsteady, you capnot do your work well.
True efficiency consists in building up your
general health so that you again find
pleasure in work.
Stimulants do not give real help. They
enable you to draw on your reserve strength,
but this in turn leaves you weaker than
before.
Debility is pot a disease of any one
organ. It is a; general condition of unfit-
ness. It must be met by a remedy whose
good results will he quickly generalised
throughout 'the' entire system. In fact, it
must be corrected by building up the blood
which, being rich and red, carries renewed
health and strength to every part of the
body.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are a tonic, not
a stimulant. They build up the .blood and
have transformed thousands of sick,
grouchy, irritable, tired and nervous people
into active, energetic, capable, efficient men
and women. They will help you too.
“Diseases of the Blood” is a booklet full
of useful information. So is the pamphlet
on “Nervous Diseases.” They are sent free
on request by the Dr. Williams’ Medicine
Co., Box 594 G.P.0., Sydney.— Advt.

The oldest recorded whaling done out
of Sydney dates back to 1791, when the
Britannia (commanded by William Raven,
whose sealing gang at Dusky Bay did the
first shipbuilding in Australasia) and the
Mary Ann cruised southward along the coast
for 15 days. They killed seven whales in the
first two days, but bad weather robbed the
crews of all but two. For the rest of the
time it was too rough to send the boats
away, but Raven reported * that thousands
of whales had been seen. For some years
after this little or no whaling was done on
the Australian coast, the whalers who came
this way preferring to go over to the South
American side of the Pacific. Sealing was far
more important as far as Sydney vessels
were concerned. “Bay whaling,” which flour-
ished exceedingly at Eden (N.S.W.), Port
Fairy and Portland (Vic.) and other places
in the ’thirties and ’forties seems to have
been started in Tasmania, where it “boomed”
before 1820.
* * * *
“Wonga”: Bananaland for some years
has been running a very good scheme in the
way of children’s competitions for corn-
growing, and results are startling. In com-
petitions recently finished the kids on their
plots averaged 83.5 bushels of maize to the
acre, as against the State average of 25
bushels. Allowing for the fact that dad
would not have the time or inclination to
sleep alongside his paddock and tickle the
growing plant like the youngsters who would
be “all in,” the figures show that there is
room for a heap of improvement in the
cooky’s methods, which if followed would
mightily alter dad’s condition and tend to do
away with the depressing growl that occa-
sionally comes from the backbone of the coun-
try. Queensland is repeating the experi-
ence of the Boys’ Corn Clubs in U.S.A.
Corn is the big crop in the States, and its
average is being brought up to a great
figure. In Australia there is an enormous
area of land that will grow maize to per-
fection, but we have done little with it, as
it hasn’t dawned upon Australians what a
great industrial proposition maize is, for
no product is capable of utilisation in more
ways. The grain cob, husks, silk, tassel and
foliage are all used for manufacturing, pur-
poses in the U.S. In the Queensland com-
petitions it is disconcerting to find that the
boy who won the second special prize was
one G. E. Gon Gee, who produced at the
rate of 153.3 bushels per acre. However a
youth with a more Australian name, E. V.
E. Burton, beat him by recording 169.6
bushels to the acre.
* * *
English tanners are taking an added
interest in wattle-bark for tanning now
that the war has cut out some of their
usual supplies for that purpose. In London
the price of chipped wattle is leaping
rapidly. Experiments with the waste pro-
duct, i.e., the expended bark with the tan-
ning matter extracted, have proved that
it can be utilised in the production of a
wrapping paper of very good quality. There
should be an opportunity here for a new
Australian industry. South Africa is a big
supplier of the bark, well over 50,000 tons
being shipped annually. Perhaps it will be
necessary to import some of this to get the
industries going in the Commonwealth.
Importing and industrial sloth were
unknown diseases to Australia’s first
settlers—keen-eyed people who appreciated
the raw material they discovered everywhere.
On September 28, 1788, nine months after
he landed. Governor Phillip sent samples of
clay to Sir Joseph Banks for trial. It was
submitted to Wedgwood, the greatest pottery
authority of the day, who said that it was
excellent material for pottery and proved it
by turning some of it into medallions, show-
ing “Hope Attended by Peace, Art and
Labor.” That’s about as far as we’ve got.
“Wart”: Thirty years ago Thom Sing,
who had a small tobacconist’s shop in Laun-
ceston (Tas.), decided to try and make his
own cigars. He got a Chinese gardener
down on Invermay to grow him some tobacco
and began his task. Soon he had another
Chinaman out at Deloraine growing tobacco
for him and a cigar factory in Launceston
with 30 or 40 hands at work, and local
smokers proclaimed the quality of the
smokes. Then the importers began to get
in their fine work. They represented to the
Government that if little cigar factories
were going to spring up in the island, all
the revenue from imported tobacco would be
lost, while it would be impossible to keep
a close enough eye on the factories and
tobacco-growers to collect the excise. Also
they had a yarn about the home-made cigars
being injurious, having opium, etc., in them.
Likewise it was against the divine law of
Freetrade. W r hcreupon the Government, being
Freetrade and friends of the Fat importers,
nipped Thom Sing’s promising industry in
the bud.
“The Wanderer”: In close proximity to
Wingham (Manning River, N.S.W.) is an
unlimited supply of timber suitable for
making axe-handles, hammer-handles or fur-
niture. Saw samples of such timber worked
up the other day, amongst them axe-handles,
which are stronger and quite as good every
way as the imported. One resident made all
his furniture out of timber growing adjacent
to his holding. It is time the Forestry De-
partment crawled out of its hollow log and
ceased prosecuting road-contractors or others
for selling trees cut down in the making of
new roads. If a contractor has to cut down
a tree suitable for mill purposes, he can burn
it and be safe from the clutches of the law.
If he sends it to a sawmill, or uses it to
advantage, he’s liable to prosecution unless
he pays royalty.
* * * *
A high-quality paint is being manufactured
in Hobart from a pure white pigment dis-
covered in deposits in the Derwent Valley,
and it is being placed on the market at a
price considerably lower than any procur-
able. The dyes used to produce the various
tints are the only imported ingredient.
