The Half-way House.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
SOMEWHERE on the road of friend-
ship the Half-way House is set,
And a grey dove croons on the shingles
where the boughs o’er the roof
have met.
And what is the grey dove crooning when
the west is a mint of gold?
The lilt of the song is new to us—though it
be of the centuries old.
Oh, deep are the mossy pathways that lead
to the studded door
From the many feet that have grooved them
in the years that have gone before,
And the copper reds of autumn and the
Spring’s transparent green
Are under the mats of the summer moss
where the feet have gone between,
And ringing the shadowy garden are the
hedges whose roots were there
When Man first looked in the Woman’s eyes
and knew that those eyes were fair.
And here, when the moon is in the sky and
the. stars are blinking through,
Old ghosts come hack for the roses that
only this morning blew....
And they weep o’er the scattered petals and
grasp at the nodding red ;
But they cannot enter the Half-way House,
for their road lies on instead.
On to the end of the Foolish Dream and
down to a grieving sea.
That is salt with the taste of the dead sea
fruit and the tides of Satiety.
Thousands of others have seen the light and
have followed it, nothing loth,
As it lures o’er the sweep of the open way
as the flame has lured the moth.
They have laid their fingers upon the latch,
and smiled in each others eyes
And challenged a world that they left be-
hind to burk them of Paradise!
Have sworn to tether the laughing boy—
who can never be tethered more—
And have not known that the Half-way
House is built with another door.
Yet out of that door they have crept at last
where the weeds and nettles grow,
And one has passed with his restless feet —
and the other with feet too slow;
And one went forth with a bitter word —and
one with a broken moan ;
And the grey dove crooning among the trees
has crooned to the stars alone.
The wind that blows from a hundred hills
dips here with the weight of flowers ;
And one does not reck of last season’s leaves
in this summer’s burgeoning bowers.
And the wise Spring spins with her master
hand at a carpet for her floor
Till we may forget that the Half-way
House had ever another d00r....
Let us never enter the Half-way House
though the lamp for us is set.
And the delicate twigs of the singing trees
right over its roofs have met.
Let us never knock at the yielding door in
the place where friendships end.
Let us keep to the smile of the Open Road
and the handclasp of a friend!
Queensland.
M. FORREST.
The Midelman and the Kidd
with the Joobious Nose.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IHAV redd a lott abowt the midelman
and the hi prophets he is making. I
am a midelman, at leest Briggs, my
boss, is. I liv on the swet of the toiler; at
leest Brigs duz.
In the morning -when the kok, after
laying his kase before the arbitrashun kort,
has relucktantly resumed his ill-pade labors
and krowed, I nock at the Ist dore in the
roe of flatts. It is opened in coarse of thyme
by a kidd with a joobious nose. I say good
morning. She says nothing worth menshun-
ing. She loox at me as if I waz a gost. She
sez she wil ask her femail progenitor. The
shapeless and kommodious progenitor kant
kum for a wile bekaws she is in her dishab-
bilay. That iz to say her feet are bear and
so is most of the rest of her kommodious
person. Wen she kums she wunders wether
she wil have rump stake. She is unabel
to make up her giggantik intellekt. Birried
in thawt she loox into spase.
Sew she kalis Erniintrood. This long-
legged flaper is reluktant to apear bekaws
her hare isent dun. Wen I look at it I
wish it waz never begun. At lastt she pores
like a refreshing streem on the arrid sur-
phase of the landskape. She sez they
haven’t had oks tale for a long thyme. It
isent my afair if they havent had oks tale
sinse the deth of Punch’s Pilot. They both
hessitait. The unlimited projenitor in dis-
habbilay thinks in a fainte voice that there
is a lott to be sedd for tryppe. In the end
they fiks on a pownd of sheep chopps, cut
thin, with no fatt on the wish-bone. I hav
spent 15 minnits at wun bob an hour wich
is thripence geting an order for wun pound
of chopps cutt thin with no phatt on the
wish-bone, wich is Bd. under the new law for
the regulashun of the speed of beef.
They awl look at me as if I waz a ghoste.
I am lait with the meet bekaws at every
dore I am mett by a kidd with a joobious
noze and she has to go into komitee with
her ampel and kapashus projenitor, hoo is
in bedd or not propperly washed, and even

PRAISE FOR PERFECT ZAM-BUK HEALINC,

writes: “Sometimeago, while
working in a timber mill, I
had the misfortune of get-
ting my right hand
injured by a fall of
timber —one of the
planks almost dislo-
cating my wrist —
and the bones were
greatly injured,
causing me terrible
pain. Having great
faith in Zam-Buk,
I applied it to the
injury, and after a

little effect of the accident
was left.
“After this I met with another accident with a
circular saw. This cut my left wrist, and inflicted a
very nasty gash. I treated it with Zam-Buk, and it was
soon healed. These are two of many experiences I have
had with Zam-Buk, and I recommend it to everyone.”

Taming a Savage.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
** HY do you wish to go?”
1 W “Because I just want to,” said
the girl.
“But you know that I don’t like your
going. Who are you so anxibus to see over
on Keube? Oh, yes—your pepple. You have
told me that two-score times, i There are no
people of yours over there. Who is it?”
The girl laughed till the flower wreath on
her brown bosom danced.
“Oh, Tiorti is jealous!” she cried glee-
fully. “You needn’t be. Nei Manoua!”
Stepping to the door she loudly repeated
her call. A slender girl, whose raven locks
bulged from under the thick wreath of
heavily-perfumed t’uri blossoms, and flowed,
a glittering mass, over her shoulders, came,
half diffidently, to the door.
“See, Tiorti, I have brought my cousin,
Nei Manoua, to stay with you until I re-
turn.”
“Nei Tokanemene, I have told you often
enough that you can’t go. I forbid you,”
cried the white man angrily.
The girl laughed mockingly.
“Oh, Tiorti is angry—and how it becomes
you ! Your eyes are like, the sharks’ but
fiercer, with the flames in them. Don’t you
like Nei Manoua?” she cried in sudden'pre-
tended surprise : “Is she not pretty enough ?
She is as good-looking as I am, is she not?”
“Nei Manoua is right enough,” said the
man ungraciously; “but you can’t”
Nei Tokanemene lightly evaded him,
jumped out on the verandah and down on
the ground.
“Farewell, my Tiorti! Be good to Nei
Manoua as to myself!” she cried gaily. A
moment she stood smiling under the great
double-branched paw-paw, then waved her
fingers to him and was gone.
Tiorti glared moodily down into the vege-
tation-overhung road which had swallowed
her.
“Tell me. Nei Manoua. who is she going
to see? Why this sudden unquenchable
desire to visit Keube?” he asked.
The substitute was shocked. Tell on her
cousin ! Perish the thought!
“Yes, yes, I know you are a good loyal
girl,” said the wily Tiorti. “But just tell
me. It will go no farther. And I’ll give you
a bottle of sandalwood oil —a large bottle!”
A gleam of desire sprang into Nei Man-
oua’s eyes.
“Well, of course,” she admitted hesita-
tingly, “if it remains between us two it
would not really be telling, would it?”
“Of course not,” Tiorti agreed heartily.
“She is going across with Nan Takabei
Tarawa. Don’t you know that he has
boasted all over the island that he can take
Nei Tokanemene away right from under
your nose? You’ll let me smell at the cork
to pick the bottle I like best, won’t you,
Tiorti?”
Tiorti nodded in reply and also compre-
hendingly at his own thoughts. He might
have known it if he had had a grain of
sense: that fellow had been hanging around
a good deal of late! All the island would be
on tenterhooks to see the outcome. He saw
it all so clearly. In the morning someone
calling at the store for the purpose would,
casually, inquire after Nei Tokanemene. Did
she leave on the trip? When would she
return? All unsuspecting he would reply.
When out of sight the visitor would go into
convulsions of delight. So would the audi-
ence of full-grown brown children when with
suitable embellishments the conversation was
recounted. He saw- the dignified old men
amusedly protest at the jest at the white
man’s expense, the while enjoying it. It
could never be allowed. That was certain.
* * * *
Through the peep-hole in the foliage
where he lay hidden on a few hastily-cut
palm-leaves Tiorti could see the water lap-
ping the beach. Though mirror-smooth
under the lee of the palm forest, the irresist-
ible ocean-swell »ulsed over the outer reefs.
The full moon sailed serenely overhead, cut-
ting a broad silvery roadway lagoonward
from the beach. Half-way out across the
reef, where the breeze, cheating the palms,
gently ruffled the water, the moon’s smooth
path became a rippling glory of frosted sil-
ver. Close to the beach a large sailing canoe
gently swayed on the lagoon. From the.
village came the insistent odor of freshly-
roasted shark and sword-fish.
From village to canoe, carrying sleeping-
mats, baked babai, cocoanuts —ripe, and for
drinking—a live robber crab in a strip of
palm-leaf skin, his huge pincer-claws broken
off, and othev belongings, the natives passed
so close that Tiorti could have touched them,
and all their artless conversation was audi
ble. As always, the bustle was great and
the result meagre. Articles put into the
canoe by one were hurriedly removed by
someone else, and later again brought down
by a third party moved from place to place
by others and finally unconcernedly moved
back to the original place by the first per-
son. Then a nude urchin came scurrying
down, an almost equally nude old crone in
hot pursuit, captured and, loudly protest-
ing. lugged back to his sleeping-mat. Now a
stiff-legged old man, supporting himself on
a long stick, ambled past, breathing noisily.
Tiorti saw the loop of the twine around the
neck of the molasses bottle passed over one
finger of the right hand, and the other
fingers clawing at the folds of the loincloth
which would come undone. Clear as a bell
behind him a voice said :
“Won’t Tiorti be wild when he hears about
it!”
There was girlish laughter, and Tiorti
knew how homicidal mania feels.
At last everything was ready. The sail
was being opened out, and Tiorti had begun
to think that Nei Tokanemene had thought
better of going, when suddenly she and Nan
Takabei Tarawa, with arms encircling each
other's waists, came slowly down the path.
When Tiorti quietly stepped before them the
man instantly dropped his arm from her
waist and stepped back. Tiorti, looking him
over, reflected that the girl had some excuse,
for he was a handsome youth, straight-limbed,
clean-skinned. He knew that the man was
as unlikely to interfere as the people in the
canoe who had paused to see the perform-
ance.
The girl’s laugh rang out sharp, high-
pitched : “O Tiorti, how kind of you to come
to see me off!” But her eyes met his un-
certainly.
“Don’t act the fool, Nei Tokanemene,” he
urged good-naturedly. “Go back home.”
For answer she laughed disdainfully and
stepped into the water. Quickly he grabbed
to stop her. She wriggled. His handwdipped
on the bare shoulder and sank into the long
loose hair. Mechanically he took a turn
around the hand and jerked her backward.
She tripped and fell on the beach. In a flash
her Kanaka lover disappeared. As she fell,
all her laughing good-nature vanished. On
the instant she became the bestial Kanaka,
giving the volcanic passions free vent. She
yelled for help, screamed, bit, clawed, spat,
smote the beach smashing blows with both
fists, twisted with lightning rapidity, kicked
both heels into the air and wept with rage;
but even then the Kanaka respect for the
white man forbade her touching her Tiorti
to free herself.
The screaming laughter from the canoe
grew fainter as the craft was leisurely poled
out to where the breeze caressed the water.
Suddenly, with two quick upward heaves, the
great triangular sail was stopped. The sen-
sitive craft instantly took a leap and sought
the wind. With deliberate haste the old
pilot reached for the steering paddle hanging
in its grummet and forced her before the
wind. When the bow and outrigger waves
ran aft and out, golden over the glittering
silver, Tiorti released the foaming savage.
With a bound she was on her feet and had
unclasped the keen-honed penknife she
carried with blade doubled on a strand of
hair. Holding its point against her bosom
she made rapid stabbing motions till the
blood trickled in tiny rivulets from the
punctures into her loin-covering.
“I am not to go, eh! I am not to go!”
she croaked, hoarse with rage.
Tiorti looked calmly on, though he very
well knew that the fury would end up by her
stabbing herself. He had doctored too many
wounds, self-inflicted in passion, to doubt it.
He knew, too, that any attempt to gain
possession of the knife would precipitate
matters.
“If you intend to stab yourself, take this —-
it’s better than yours,” he said calmly.
Holding by the point the large sheath-
knife he had used to cut his bed of palm-
leaves he offered her the handle. In incred-
ulous wonder she stopped her noise and hesi-
tatingly held out her hand. Instantly he let
the knife fall to the ground. The hand shot
forward seizing the girl’s wrist, forcing the
knife out of her fingers. Catching sight of
the disappearing canoe Nei Tokanemene
flopped heavily on the sand, kicked out her
legs and howled with despair.
Abruptly Tiorti turned and without word
walked up the path. As he disappeared in
the shade he heard a tear-suffocated call: —
“Tiorti!”
He stopped, invisible behind the bushes.
He could hear her weeping and sniffling as
she stumbled among the fallen palm-branches
and nuts. Catching sight of him she stopped,
and he saw in the moonlight the handsome
face swollen and repulsive with weeping and
passion. A moment he waited, then walked
on till again he heard the barely audible
call. But when he stopped she did the same,
and finally when out of sight, instead of stop-
ping, he walked briskly on. Quickly chang-
ing into pyjamas he slipped under the mos-
quito-netting. Soon she emerged from the
shadows into the moonlight flooding the open
space before the house. When her step
sounded on the verandah he feigned sleep. She
was still sniffling; her bosom and throat
heaved and worked and choked after convul-
sive sobbing. Sinking down on the palm-
branch floor by the netting she whispered :
“Tiorti. Tiorti, I taneriko, Tiorti.... I
love you !”
Receiving no answer she turned away and
sobbed anew. Gradually the man passed from
sham into real sleep as Nei Tokanemene, more
asleep than awake, sobbed her submission :
“Tiorti, I tan.,, ,er.... . iko, I tan....”

direct influence of evil spirits, and exorcism and magic
were invoked to cast it out.
Science has taught us wisdom. The evil spirits exist
still. We call them “Disease Germs,” and they also
must be cast out. Once lodged in the stomach or
intestines, fever with its hallucinations, or biliousness with
its aches and pains, is the result.
ENO S FRUIT SALT’
is the approved remedy for driving out disease germs.
Its action is quick and thorough. It clears the intestines,
rouses the torpid liver to new life, stimulates the mucous
membrane to a healthy action, and cleanses and invigorates
the whole digestive tract.

bottle in the house .
